I Will Hold My SpaceX Shares Forever, Says Sequoia's Maguire

Bloomberg Technology(728.0K)·17:12·11.3K views·2026-06-16
Shaun MaguirePartner, Sequoia Capital Ed LudlowHost, Bloomberg Technology Elon MuskFounder, SpaceX
TL;DRSequoia Capital Partner Shaun Maguire outlines a hyper-bullish, long-term thesis on SpaceX, asserting that its unique multi-layered model—spanning dominant launch capabilities, elite engineering talent, and rapid scaling of both terrestrial and orbital AI compute—makes it a compounding powerhouse whose shares he plans to hold "forever.

TL;DR

Sequoia Capital Partner Shaun Maguire outlines a hyper-bullish, long-term thesis on SpaceX, asserting that its unique multi-layered model—spanning dominant launch capabilities, elite engineering talent, and rapid scaling of both terrestrial and orbital AI compute—makes it a compounding powerhouse whose shares he plans to hold "forever."

The Five-Layer SpaceX Model & The Cursor Integration

Maguire conceptualizes SpaceX as a five-layer business ecosystem, with recent developments significantly accelerating its artificial intelligence capabilities:

  • The Five Layers of SpaceX:
    • Layer 1: Launch: The foundational bedrock of the company, driven by the Starship program.
    • Layer 2: Connectivity: Starlink and Direct-to-Cell technology, which are highly predictable compounding assets.
    • Layer 3: Compute: Currently terrestrial AI cloud infrastructure, transitioning to orbital data centers in the future.
    • Layer 4: The Model Layer: SpaceX AI/Grok, which represents the company's proprietary intelligence layer.
    • Layer 5: Moonshot Bets: Long-term infrastructure projects, including "Terrafab" and potential lunar bases utilizing rail-gun launch systems.
  • The Cursor Acquisition: Maguire strongly refutes labeling the Cursor deal as a mere "acquihire."
    • Financial & Team Strength: Cursor is a three-year-old company with a reported $3 billion+ run rate and one of the finest engineering teams ever assembled.
    • The "Musk Trial": The team successfully navigated an intense two-month trial period working directly under Elon Musk, proving they could operate flawlessly at extreme pace and intensity.
    • Strategic Integration: Cursor's deep integration will optimize data cleaning, orbital satellite planning, and hardware infrastructure, making Maguire "99.999% confident" the acquisition will be highly accretive.

Unrivaled Moats: Starship Launch and Team Distillation

Maguire believes SpaceX possesses the deepest competitive moats of any company in history, driven by two primary factors:

  • The Launch Moat (Starship): Maguire describes Starship as the most complex single engineering project ever attempted by a private company.
    • Technical Complexity: He evaluates Starship's intrinsic complexity as "meaningfully beyond" ASML’s Extreme Ultraviolet (EUV) lithography machines.
    • Payload Capabilities: Future Starship iterations are projected to carry 135 to 200 metric tons of payload to orbit, with structural scaling benefits making even higher capacities achievable.
  • The Human Capital Moat: The secondary moat is a 20-year "heavy distillation process" of talent.
    • Talent Sourcing: Recruiting the absolute top tier from institutions like MIT and Stanford.
    • Retention and Culture: Only a fraction of a percent of elite engineers survive the intense, mistake-free, team-oriented culture. This distilled engineering team is now being deployed to disrupt terrestrial data center construction.

The AI Cloud Transition: Terrestrial and Orbital Compute

SpaceX is aggressively positioning itself to dominate the generational shift from legacy CPU-based cloud computing to AI-specific cloud infrastructure:

  • Terrestrial NeoCloud Dominance: SpaceX AI is building massive physical data centers (such as Colossus 1, 2, and 3 in Tennessee) faster and more efficiently than traditional hyperscalers.
    • Capital Efficiency: SpaceX is highly competitive on a dollar-per-token basis.
    • The Google & Anthropic Deals: Selling compute capacity to major rivals like Anthropic and Google is a highly rational, de-risking move. It generates immense, immediate revenue that justifies heavy capital expenditure to public markets post-IPO.
  • Orbital Compute Outlook: Maguire is exceptionally bullish on orbital data centers, which the prospectus targets as early as 2028.
    • Simpler Hardware: An orbital compute satellite is actually less complex than a Starlink satellite. It relies on proven components: a server-rack-sized compute element, solar panels, laser communication links, and massive radiators to dissipate heat.
    • Starship Dependency: Because orbital compute servers must be physically large to run inference effectively, their rollout is strictly bottlenecked by the scaling speed of Starship payload flights. Maguire expects orbital compute satellites to launch within six months of Starship’s first commercial payload flights.

Market Dynamics & Black Swan Risks

Despite intense competition from established AI players, Maguire sees an expansive path forward:

  • Unlimited AI Demand: While OpenAI and Anthropic are formidable, Maguire believes "intelligence" is the defining capability of our era, meaning there is virtually unlimited global demand to support all three players. SpaceX’s unique advantage lies in being the dominant physical provider of the raw compute that powers this intelligence.
  • The Primary Threat: When asked what Sequoia’s chief "Black Swan" concern is regarding SpaceX, Maguire pointed directly to regulatory risk as the most critical variable to monitor.

Notable Quotes

"I am personally going to hold my shares in this company forever. Like quite literally, I think that... this is the biggest vision, the biggest mission of any company in history, going after the biggest markets of any company in history, with the biggest moat." — *Shaun Maguire*
"We're in a generational transition of cloud from kind of legacy CPU-based jobs to AI cloud. I strongly believe that SpaceX and SpaceX AI is the best in the world at building this next generation of AI clouds... you have the best rocket scientists in the world now using their skills to build terrestrial compute." — *Shaun Maguire*
"The intrinsic complexity of making a Starlink satellite is probably a little bit greater than an orbital compute satellite... Almost all of the difficulty of orbital compute satellites is in Starship, it's in the launch vehicle." — *Shaun Maguire*

AI Devices Are Coming | Qualcomm CEO Cristiano Amon

CNBC International(1.4M)·49:40·6.5K views·2026-06-16
Cristiano AmonCEO, Qualcomm
TL;DRQualcomm CEO Cristiano Amon predicts that 2026 will be the inflection point for "agentic AI," triggering a paradigm shift where personalized AI agents displace the smartphone as the sole center of digital life.
This transition is driving massive demand for on-device...

TL;DR

Qualcomm CEO Cristiano Amon predicts that 2026 will be the inflection point for "agentic AI," triggering a paradigm shift where personalized AI agents displace the smartphone as the sole center of digital life. This transition is driving massive demand for on-device processing, new wearable form factors like smart glasses, and next-generation chip architectures, even as the industry battles severe near-term memory shortages.

The Paradigm Shift to Agentic AI and the "Compute Continue"

  • The Rise of the Agent: Amon forecasts that 2026 will be the year AI agents scale globally.
      • Major operating system (OS) vendors are expected to integrate orchestrators and agent "claws" directly into their systems by summer.
  • Agents as the New Apps: Traditional applications are transitioning from human-facing interfaces into backend endpoints that AI agents navigate on behalf of the user.
      • Bespoke Functionality: For example, instead of manually navigating a banking app, users will instruct an agent to retrieve transactions, plot custom charts, or dynamically pay a bill via a QR code.
  • The "Compute Continue": The binary debate of "cloud versus edge" processing is obsolete. Computing is evolving into an integrated hybrid system where edge devices handle low-latency, real-time responses while seamlessly coordinating with cloud-level models.
  • Model Context Protocol (MCP): This open-source protocol is essential to the agentic era, allowing AI assistants to connect and communicate dynamically across different applications, databases, and cloud services.

The Evolving Landscape of Personal AI Hardware

  • A New Center of Gravity: The smartphone will no longer be the absolute center around which wearables orbit. Instead, a user's chosen AI agent will become the center of their digital life, with phones, glasses, and other devices acting as peripheral endpoints.
  • The Dominance of Smart Glasses: Amon identifies smart glasses as the most natural form factor for multimodal AI because they position cameras, microphones, and speakers directly close to human senses.
      • Market Scaling: Industry shipments of smart glasses are currently in the "multiple tens of millions" annually, with a clear trajectory to scale to hundreds of millions in a couple of years.
      • Technological Roadmap: Future smart glasses will advance beyond simple cameras and audio to incorporate projection displays, eye-tracking, and hand-gesture controls.
  • Alternative Form Factors: Qualcomm is actively collaborating with AI and cloud partners on more than 40 distinct wearable designs, including jewelry, camera-enabled earbuds, watches, pendants, and pins.
  • New Market Entrants: Because agent-centric ecosystems are horizontal rather than vertical, the barrier to entry has lowered. This allows non-traditional consumer brands—such as fashion houses and AI startups like OpenAI (partnering with hardware designer Jony Ive)—to enter the consumer electronics space.

Silicon Architecture Demands and the CPU Renaissance

  • The Efficiency Imperative: Packaged AI workloads must run efficiently on wearable devices that cannot support liquid cooling or heavy batteries. This plays directly into Qualcomm's core mobile expertise of optimizing high-performance compute in thermally constrained environments.
  • The CPU Renaissance: While Neural Processing Units (NPUs) and Graphics Processing Units (GPUs) are expanding on System-on-Chips (SOCs) to handle localized AI models, the central processing unit (CPU) is seeing a critical resurgence.
      • Orchestration Workloads: CPUs are highly critical because the sophisticated "orchestrator" software that manages agent tasks lives on the CPU.
      • Data Center Expansion: To capitalize on this, Qualcomm plans to announce its new CPU for data centers on June 24.

Semiconductor Supply Chain and Memory Bottlenecks

  • Severe Memory Shortages: The industry is grappling with post-pandemic style supply constraints. High-bandwidth memory chips are being heavily funneled to AI data centers, creating a severe bottleneck for consumer electronics.
  • Capacity Resolution Timeline: While some industry experts predict supply constraints will persist until 2028, Amon is more optimistic, anticipating that new capacity and more efficient silicon architectures will become available in the second half of 2027.
  • A Structural Upgrade Cycle: Amon emphasizes that the current demand spike is not a typical semiconductor cycle. Instead, it represents a structural, global upgrade of the world's computing infrastructure to support token generation and AI-native software.

Notable Quotes

"The phone is going to continue to be your phone... But the phone also's going to have those orchestrators... You're going to see between now and summer, every OS vendor talking about their OS having an orchestrator... agents are the new apps." — Cristiano Amon
"In the future, and is a very near future, especially as agents start to get scale: the center of your digital experience is the agent that you choose... And now everything is around the agent. The phone is around the agent, the new classes of devices... are going to be around the agent as well." — Cristiano Amon
"We have significantly more demand than supply. And memory has become a bottleneck... I think some time in '27 or at least the second half we should see more capacity becoming available, different architectures that are going to make use of memory more efficient." — Cristiano Amon

And that goes to the point about what's changed to allow devices these days to be more personal? Okay, it is a number of technologies and I'm going to break it down for you. I think the first one is those large language models got pretty good. And they got pretty good, and they also got very specialized. Not only language models, but also visual models. You're starting to hear about words like "multimodal", meaning you have models that are going to take different inputs: they're going to take audio, they're going to take language, and they're going to take vision. You start hearing about small models, mixture of experts models that are very specific for certain functions. You start hearing about the distillation of models—models where there's a big model that got trained and then became a smaller model. And you're starting to hear about those models being connected to different applications. You hear about technologies such as MCP protocols, where a model can go to the internet and connect to other apps and other cloud services. And then the last part of this basket of technologies is, I'm sure you've heard about OpenClaw, those techniques called orchestrators—agents that go operate your computer and your device for you. They're going to look at your data, they're going to look at the models, and get things done for you. When you put all of this together, you create the conditions for us to start changing our mobile experience. You start to interact with mobile devices in the way we're going to interact with agents. It's very different. There's no keyboard, you don't need to touch, you just need to see and talk. And I think that starts to create the precondition for us to see this mobile revolution that is happening because of AI. So Cristiano, let's tackle some of those. You talked about the models because we've been discussing a lot about large language models, these huge, massive models that have been trained and are then underpinning these chatbots that so many people use. But there is also discussion about more of these models becoming smaller to run on the device rather than in the cloud. And that's a big part of what you're working on at Qualcomm. How important is the development of models right now in terms of underpinning the future of some of the agentic AI experience which you've been talking about? Look, it's really important. And those models will continue to be developed. But I think we're actually getting to the point in the industry now that we're starting to reach maturity of understanding how those models and computers are going to work together. I'm going to be a little bit provocative because I remember there was a lot of discussion about a year, maybe two years ago or one year ago, with a lot of debate about: is this model going to run on the cloud or is this model going to run on the edge? Can I run this exact model that is running on the cloud on the device at the edge? But I think that was the wrong discussion. Think about for example, Arjun, if I ask you: how many apps do you have on your phone? You're probably going to tell me probably over 200 apps organized in folders. And if I ask you: let's go one by one and see which app runs on, or what portion of the app runs on the cloud, and what portion of the app runs on a device, it's probably a useless exercise. We know they're all doing both because if you put your phone in airplane mode, it becomes not very useful. So I think the same thing is happening with AI. AI is just a new form of doing computing. And models are now developing in a way that there are models that are specific that are going to be running on the edge, and models that are specific that are going to be running on the cloud, and they're going to go work together as one. There's actually an industry term now called "Compute Continue". It's going to be like one integrated system. And I'll give you an example of what I mean. If I am going to interact with my future personal AI device, let's say smart glasses, and I'm going to interact the way I'm interacting with you right now, I need to have an instant response. I may be walking on the street and I say: "Who's this person?" And the model is going to say: "That's Arjun from CNBC, you know him, you probably want to say hi." I cannot wait. I cannot just ask the question, keep walking, and wait for the tokens to come in. It's the same thing if I read a menu and I say: "What does it say?" You want an instant response. If I look at a QR code: "Please pay this for me." So what is happening is the compute is naturally breaking down into what's going to happen on the edge and what's going to happen on the cloud. They're going to be integrated; one is not going to function without the other. And I think that's going to kind of define how we think about the future of consumer electronics and devices in the age of AI. And I'm just going to tell you: it's no different than what happened to cars or what happened to robots. Actually, those are great examples of edge AI. You know, they actually run on the robot and they run in the car. What exactly it means... Well, we've obviously got the smartphones, but over the past year there's been an explosion, of course, of all these different types of chatbots. We're thinking of OpenAI's ChatGPT, we're thinking of Anthropic's Claude, we're talking about Google's Gemini—all of these now. And they're getting much more sophisticated. And now, device makers are thinking: how can we integrate all this AI into all these different types of electronics? And ultimately, what does it mean for the way we're going to be using devices going forward? This part is really important because we're about to talk about different types of AI devices that are coming up, and you're going to hear mention of things like smart glasses. If you haven't seen those, you know, Meta produces some smart glasses which have sort of a camera in them. Samsung, Qualcomm, and Google are all working on smart glasses. There are lots of companies looking at this space, many thinking this could be a new frontier for AI devices as well. The other thing we're going to mention here is AI pins. It was basically a small square thing that you attached to your clothing. It had a camera in it, no screen, but you could talk to it, and it would give you answers and would also project text onto a surface such as your hand or a table or something. One word that is a huge buzzword right now, and we've mentioned it already, is this idea of agentic AI. Now, when I hear it being spoken about, the kind of utopia of it is effectively: these AI systems are going to be able to autonomously do things on our behalf, like the smartest personal assistant out there. When it comes to the agentic experience on consumer devices, what does that look like in your view? Okay, this is actually one of the questions I love answering because, even though we have been changing Qualcomm and we're becoming a much more diversified company, we're still one of the biggest providers of mobile technology. And it's fascinating now to see what's happening with those agents and what's happening with the mobile phone. When we talk about agents, and I'm kind of convinced 2026 is going to be the year of agents, we're starting to see this now getting scaled. And those things became really popular and went viral, I think on the internet, when OpenClaw was launched. And you have now this thing that you install on your computer and you ask the agent to do things for you. You create agents and it goes to your data, does things on your behalf, operates your machine for you, and goes to the internet. And then what you saw happening, I remember people were going in and buying computers—there were a lot of people buying Mac Minis. They were sold out. And they put them into their office, and they installed OpenClaw and let the thing run, consuming tokens like crazy, going to their Anthropic accounts and the OpenAI accounts consuming all the tokens. Now, what that did was show a path for how we're going to interact with our computers. We are going to use our computers, but the agent is going to use our computers on our behalf and do things for you. Now, in a mobile environment, there's no way you're going to get a backpack, put a car battery, put a Mac Mini, and carry that alongside your phone. So those things are going to happen in the phone. And I think that's also going to define the phone experience, given that billions of people carry phones. So now we have clarity about what an AI phone and an AI PC is. And I'm going to break it to you. What's going to happen: your phone is going to have dual personas. Your phone will continue to be your phone. The phone's not going to go anywhere. You're going to get your phone, and when you pull your phone out of your pocket, you're going to go to your apps and you're going to do those things. But the phone is also going to have those orchestrators, those claws. You're going to see between now and summer, every OS vendor is going to talk about their OS having an orchestrator and a claw. And you'll be able to also get agents to do things for you, and it's going to operate your phone on your behalf. It's going to go to your apps, and you're going to give them instructions. It's so profound, this change, that even if I go a moment outside the phone, it's telling you what's happening with software companies and the whole SaaS industry. Now software companies develop their software for a human to utilize, and there's a human interface, but also for an agent to utilize to go into their software and do things. And the way this is going to play out is fascinating. For example, you're going to have the things that you do yourself, but it's going to be the agent doing things for you. I've seen some examples where somebody has a meeting, and then another invitation comes up and the agent will say: "Cristiano, you have this podcast with Arjun from CNBC. Does this take priority after this other meeting?" I'm going to say: "Of course it takes priority." Then it says: "Okay, can I reschedule this other meeting for you?" And you say: "Yes." And the agent will call the person if it needs to talk to a person, will send an email, and will figure out how to reschedule the things for you based on your calendar. And slowly, we're going to start getting used to those new experiences, and over time your phones are going to be doing both. It's going to be operated by the agent and it's going to be operated by you. I'm just waiting for an agent that can clear my inboxes for me. That's the biggest thing. And Cristiano, when you look at the roadmap then for this, you said 2026 is going to be the year of the agents. It feels like the last two years a lot of smartphone companies have been talking about AI integrating into their devices, but actually none of it has been of this kind of agentic type of experience you're describing. Do you believe that happens in 2026, and to what level, I suppose? And then, as you look forward with all the device partners you work with, what's the roadmap for that becoming more advanced? It's interesting. If you remember the transition from a feature phone to a smartphone, which was a huge transition—the phone became a computer. What happened in the early days of the smartphone, the very early days, was the apps that you had were the apps that came from the OEM, right? So I'm sure when you bought your iPhone, it came with iTunes, it came with that app where you can move drums and instruments and play around. But eventually, what happened is the App Store became available and developers started to write all sorts of different apps. And I can tell you right now, the absolute majority of the experience that you have on your phone today didn't come from the OS itself, it didn't come from the OEM, but actually came from developers. It is a platform, it is a computer. Actually, the smartphone is the largest platform ever created by mankind. Okay, now let's draw a parallel to what we saw in AI. AI happened. The first application that we saw was chatbots. People go in, they ask a question, they get an answer. Then, they started to do more advanced things: "Summarize this for me." And then a lot of the OEMs said: "How can I apply AI to my camera? How can I apply AI to this different functionality?" It was a bit mixed in usability—some things were very useful, some things were early days. But then something happened: those orchestrators and those agents happened. And then everything started to change because now AI can become very useful and AI can do things for you. I think we are at that inflection point. And that's why I said '26 is the year that those things start to become clear and those things start to get scale with those agents. So much so that you're going to see between now and summer, every OS vendor talking about how they're going to have those orchestrators built into the OS. Because that becomes—well, agents are the new apps, and those orchestrators become the new App Store. And we're going to see the coexistence of the old and the new in the same device because, in the case of a phone, you only carry one device, you don't carry two. And then we're going to see some other classes of devices which are just agents. No legacy. Like smart glasses, personal AI devices, and pins. And the reason I like to draw this parallel is because it went from how an OEM or an OS will add first-party AI capabilities to an endless set of possibilities as people create their own agents and agents start to operate devices on your behalf. We're going to talk about those new types of devices in a sec, but my first question on the back of that answer is: does this mean that apps are dead? They're not dead, but apps are going to change. Absolutely, apps are going to change. I'm going to go back to something I said two years ago. All of us have a banking app—you have your bank of choice and you have the app. So you have a developer, and imagine: how would the app be that most of the bank users are going to like? What are the colors? It's going to fit onto a screen of your device, and it's going to have a menu. And you're going to start the app, and it's going to say: "This is my checking account, this is how much money I have in the bank, these are my last transactions, this is my credit card, these are my investments." And the way it's going to be done is going to be the way that most people are going to like it. Okay, now that banking app in your phone has your credentials, so it can go to the bank, authenticate itself as a proxy of you, and get that information to you. Now, you can have an agent that you can give the credentials of your bank to. And the way the app is going to be—the same app experience—I'm going to describe it to you. You're going to say: "How much do I have in my checking account?" And it's going to say: "This is how much you have." "What are my last three transactions?" "Can you please plot a graph for me about this?" And then if you say: "I want this information to be displayed on my screen, but I don't like the colors of the app; I want it to be different colors," it will render it for you. So this is the difference. This is the fundamental difference in how apps are going to evolve into agents. And they're going to become way more powerful, way more bespoke, and they're going to be integrated into everything. You can be in a restaurant, look at a QR code with the bill, and say: "Pay this for me. Please take it out of my checking account and notify me when it's done." That's going to be different than you opening the app and going through the process yourself. And among many of the important things you said through this episode so far is that you believe the smartphone is still going to be critical. Do you believe this is still going to be central to the world of agentic AI? And how do you expect our relationship with our smartphone to change? Yes and no. So let me step back a little bit and talk about how our digital life, especially from a consumer standpoint, is going to work. We are coming today, in the present time, into a world where the absolute center of our digital life is our smartphone, our most inseparable device. Everything is around the smartphone. Like, the smartphone is the sun, and every planet is around the smartphone. So much so that if you look at what was defined as a smart wearable, those smart wearables, their job was to extend the functionality of the phone to you. Let's go back to a simple thing, exactly like a smartwatch. It will give you the time, send sensor data from you to the phone, and get notifications from the phone back to you. And it was so integrated that actually you see how the industry actually works: the same phone company is the one doing a lot of the wearables and its ecosystem. There's a big change now. In the future—and it is a very near future, especially as agents start to get scale—the center of your digital experience is the agent that you choose, or the multiple agents that you choose. And now everything is around the agent. The phone is around the agent. The new classes of devices, and I know we're going to talk about that in a second, are going to be around the agent as well. And the agent will be the one that will understand human intentions and will do things for you. So there is a shift in what the center of gravity is for your digital life. And that's going to change the relationship with our devices. And with that, I'm going to answer your question: will the phone disappear? Absolutely not. The phone is an incredibly useful device; the scale of the smartphone is tremendous. But we're going to use our phones a little bit differently. Remember when the smartphone arrived, some people said the laptop is dead. Absolutely, the laptop was never dead. The laptop is still here and it is still strong. But the way you used to work on your laptop changed; workloads shifted. I like to provide this example because it's easy for most of us to understand: we started doing e-commerce on a laptop, and we started doing social media on a laptop—that's why you go to your Facebook account. Now, most of e-commerce and social media is done on a mobile device. So your phone is still going to be there, but certain workloads are going to shift to new classes of devices because it's going to be better for the agent to interact with you in this new class of devices, and it's going to be better for you as well. So I think in summary: your phone is still going to be there, your phone is going to change, and it's going to have a lot more computing because you're now going to do your phone things, but you're going to run agents that are going to operate the phone for you. And then your experience on a phone—some of those experiences are going to migrate to new devices that are easier to interact with in human language and human vision. So let's talk about those devices then, Cristiano, because you mentioned a few throughout this conversation, and maybe we should start with glasses. Smartphones, just so you know, are about 1.2 billion phones a year. And the reason that is becoming a clear winner is because when you wear glasses—first, humans wear glasses, some of us do, right? So you already have an industry at scale for glasses. The second thing is glasses are very natural for you to put cameras on, because when you turn your head, whatever you're seeing, the camera is seeing. So "see what I see." It's close to your ears for speakers, close to your mouth for a microphone. You will be able to read what you read. So glasses become very natural for you to interact with a model using multimodal human language and vision. And then it becomes very easy for you to understand how certain workloads that are very low-friction for you to interact with on those kind of devices can easily migrate to a glass, and start to get scale. And I go back to what I said earlier about the role of a wearable in a world where the smartphone is in the center versus the role of a wearable where the agent is at the center. It's fascinating. When the first smart glass was launched, the job was to take a picture and post it on Instagram or generate a story for Instagram. It was basically an extension of the camera of your smartphone and also the ability for you to listen to music. That was it. But now that you have agents, every week you get a new feature. Every week you get an integration with another cloud, and it develops completely independently of how the phone develops. And I think that's exactly how we describe it, and I am very bullish on glasses, as you know. What are some of the big innovations you think are coming to smart glasses in the coming years? Yes, everything that you just said. That will create control points for you to operate, especially if you have images that are displayed back to you. The other device you mentioned in this conversation, Cristiano, was pins.
