And Cristiano, I know it's an open secret you guys are working with OpenAI on their own device, and I know there's not much you can say about that. But it also underscores how non-traditional companies who haven't really been in consumer devices before are thinking about consumer devices in the age of AI. You mentioned you've got all these different designs in the works. Going forward, are you expecting more companies who haven't typically been in consumer electronics to get into this space because of the opportunity they see with AI devices? 100%. And let me explain to you why this is happening. Because it's an important point, and I will touch on one thing that I said before, Arjun. Remember I told you when the smartphone is at the very center of your digital experience, it is more conducive for you to have a vertical ecosystem, where the same company that provides the phone provides all the wearable devices. But now, when the agent or a collection of agents is the center of the ecosystem, you may have a horizontal play. You may have multiple devices because they're loosely connected, because they're connected to the agent. That creates opportunities for many companies to enter the space. As a matter of fact, when you look at glasses that humans buy, those are fashion devices. Those are fashion companies becoming technology companies—those are not necessarily consumer electronic devices. And to go back to your question about why those companies are doing this: as we go through this transition, there was training, the creation of models, and AI in the data center. Then there was inference to generate tokens, but now you have agents that generate demand for those tokens. What those companies realize with the rise of agents is that the AI is going to be utilized by humans. It's not like those humans are going to show up at the data center, ring the bell, and say, "Give me some AI." So what happens is all the devices that we wear become endpoints for agents. And those AI companies understand they have to win those endpoints for agents. And there is one last piece of data that nobody talks about, but we're going to remember this conversation in a few years when people start talking about this. The reason we have AI today is because we digitized all of our information—all of our books, things we write, emails, stuff on the internet, social media posts, and web pages. All of this information on the internet is digital, so now you can train models. But once we start using agents every day, walking around with our smart glasses to see what we see, that amount of data is massive. It's exponentially larger than what was used to train models. So those companies want to have access to the data because it's important to train future models and get context for you, because your consumer experience is going to be bespoke. All of those things point to why those companies are interested in doing devices. And we're very excited that all of the chips that we do—from an earbud of sub-two milliwatts to a 2000-watt chip as we enter the data center—become relevant to all of those companies. Right, I just want to talk about chips for a second. Not chips the type that you eat—you know, depending on if you're in the UK, that means something very different than if you're in the US. But I want to talk about a term called SOC, or System on Chip. Now, this is effectively a piece of silicon with lots of different types of chips on it. And you'll hear Cristiano Amon mention some of those. So I just want to quickly lay out what they are. The first one is the Central Processing Unit, or CPU. This is effectively the brains of a device. It does all the general computing tasks, like handling apps and the system as well. The second one is a Graphics Processing Unit, or GPU. This renders graphics for gaming or the actual device itself. Another one is called the Neural Processing Unit, or NPU, and this handles the AI workloads. And all of these are integrated into one piece of silicon and thrown into your devices. And that's something effectively that Qualcomm designs. That, however, is manufactured by other companies, and if you've heard previous episodes of the podcast, we've spoken so much about the chip supply chain. But right now, there are a load of bottlenecks within the entire semiconductor supply chain. One of those is around memory. Now, all your devices have some sort of memory in them—your laptop, your phone. And what's happened is there are very few companies in the world that can produce these memory chips. All of that right now is being funneled towards AI data centers and AI chips. And so there's a big shortage of memory for things like consumer electronics devices. That is a big topic right now of focus for all kinds of electronics companies, and it's something that Cristiano Amon and I spoke about. At the heart of all of this is the chips that are going to power these devices, and Qualcomm of course is one of the leading players in this space. What does all of this proliferation of new types of devices running AI mean for the way you're thinking about chip architecture? There is a desire to pack more and more on a chip and to make them more powerful and more energy-efficient. So as you think about all these new devices, what does it mean for the way chips are going to be designed going forward? It's an incredibly exciting time for semiconductor companies, and for semiconductor companies like Qualcomm. We had the privilege—and it was a privilege—to be developing our capability in the mobile industry, because the mobile industry is very unforgiving. Every generation, users want a more powerful phone that does more things. They play games, they have big GPUs and big CPUs, but it still has to fit in your pocket, still has to have an all-day battery life, and it still cannot get hot. I don't have the ability to do liquid cooling and put a radiator into a smartphone. So that created a DNA in our semiconductor engineering: you have to pack more compute in a very efficient way. And I think that is a great problem for a company like Qualcomm to solve. It's also true on glasses. How can I put a lot of compute and be very efficient with battery in a glass or in a pendant? So I think that is fundamentally changing architecture. We're increasing dramatically the ability to have neural processing units—neural processors that are going to run those smaller models on those devices, working alongside the big models in the cloud. That means the area of the silicon and the amount dedicated to running models increased dramatically. That's true on both the GPU and the neural processing unit. We need a faster and more power-efficient CPU because agents drive the CPU. And we need to do this in a way that continues to be smart about how we manage connectivity and battery life. Our entire roadmap is in a process of upgrade right now—our entire roadmap. Because I believe none of the devices we have today are prepared for this future. You're going to have to upgrade your phone, you're going to upgrade your PC, and you're going to have those new classes of devices like glasses and pendants. Your car is going to get upgraded so that you're going to be able to talk to agents in the car. And I think that's going to happen across everything that we interact with. It's a new form of computing, it's a new form of software, and it requires a new architecture of silicon. And how important will the CPU be in this? It's been a big debate so far, with people talking about the CPU having a renaissance, not just in the data center now, but even as we think about these consumer devices. Super important. It's 100% correct. And let's go back to this example I gave you when we started the conversation about agents. You have parallel processing at scale, like a GPU or even an NPU. But those orchestrators are actually handling very complex tasks. They're going to check your data, check the model, and go to the internet. And that's a CPU that orchestrates all of this. Those orchestrators live on the CPU. So now all boats are rising. At Qualcomm, our CPU became very important. And we have the leading CPU in the industry in phones and PCs, and soon we're going to announce our CPU for the data center on June 24th. CPUs are going to be very important, NPUs for inference and power-efficient inference are going to be very important, and also GPUs will continue to be important. So this is why I think it's a great time to be in the semiconductor business right now. And the semiconductor industry is seeing a huge explosion of interest, innovation, and demand. With that, of course, has come a number of bottlenecks around the supply chain, whether that's in access to memory or the actual manufacturing of these chips from the fabs. There's a big debate over whether some of the cyclicality that we've historically seen in the semiconductor industry is changing. How are you navigating all of these different challenges right now, particularly as it pertains to things like memory and capacity constraints? Well, first of all, before answering that question: if you have some memory, I'll buy it from you, okay? So I think the industry right now, unfortunately, is dealing with something similar to what we had to deal with during the pandemic. We have significantly more demand than supply. And memory has become a bottleneck. I think capacity was not built for this AI reality. But I'm optimistic that the industry has found ways to deal with those things, and capacity will be built. Regarding the question about the cyclical process, I feel this is a little bit different. I think the whole computing infrastructure of the world is being upgraded—the whole computing infrastructure. AI is basically a different way to run software. You need different and more powerful computers. What you generate is tokens; that's the new currency of the AI world. And as I said during our conversation, the existing compute infrastructure is not capable and needs to go through a process of upgrade. And the same demand that we're starting to see in the data center will continue, but it's also, as agents take hold and start to get scale, going to happen with the rest of our devices. I wish we had more memory. I think we have been working with the industry to get more capacity, and that is also true of semiconductor manufacturing. But you know, it's a good problem to have, and I feel optimistic. Some people talk about this only getting resolved by 2028. I feel more optimistic about that. I think sometime in 2027, or at least the second half, we should see more capacity becoming available, as well as different architectures that are going to make use of memory more efficiently. And I think that creates opportunities for a company like Qualcomm, which has been very economical in the use of power and memory, building devices at the edge, to do something disruptive in the data center. That's what we want to show when we unveil our roadmap at the end of June. Cristiano, I always learn so much speaking to you. It's so much food for thought as well, and I'm excited to see some of these things you've teased on this episode coming out over the next few months and years. So thanks so much for joining me. My pleasure. Always a pleasure talking to you, Arjun. And I look forward to seeing you soon. Some really interesting stuff in that conversation with Cristiano Amon. But actually, I'd love to hear from you. I'd love to hear your thoughts about the episode or anything that Cristiano was speaking about there. You can get in touch directly with me. I'm on X, LinkedIn, TikTok, and Instagram. Just search my name, Arjun Kharpal. You can also email us at thetechdownload@cnbc.com. If you did like this episode, subscribe, drop us a like, drop us a comment—whatever you like. And we'll catch you next time on The Tech Download.

Simulating Humans at Scale: Simile's Joon Sung Park

Sequoia Capital(222.0K)·38:46·3.5K views·2026-06-16
Joon Sung ParkFounder and CEO, Simile
TL;DRSimile, founded by the creators of Stanford’s landmark "Smallville" study, is building a simulation platform that accurately models human behavior and societal dynamics.
By capturing human diversity and irrationality, the platform enables enterprises like CVS to conduct...

TL;DR

Simile, founded by the creators of Stanford’s landmark "Smallville" study, is building a simulation platform that accurately models human behavior and societal dynamics. By capturing human diversity and irrationality, the platform enables enterprises like CVS to conduct rapid market testing and holds the long-term potential to simulate complex macroeconomic and geopolitical crises.

From Smallville to Simile: The Genesis of Generative Agents

  • The Smallville Experiment: In April 2023, Joon Sung Park and his Stanford research team created "Smallville," a virtual town populated by 25 generative AI agents.
      • These agents were equipped with architectures for memory, planning, and reflection, allowing them to lead "lived experiences" such as executing morning routines, going to work, forming relationships, and planning complex, emergent social events like a Valentine's Day party.
  • Social Simulacra (2022): The precursor to Smallville simulated entire subreddits with thousands of distinct personas to observe emergent community dynamics.
      • The Design Challenge: Historically, testing social platforms required high-cost, risky field tests. Social Simulacra demonstrated that generative personas could accurately model collaborative online behavior, such as planning hypothetical trips together.
  • Transitioning to a Company: While academic research excels at "breadth-first search," Joon Sung Park, Percy Liang (head of the Center for Foundation Models at Stanford), and Michael Bernstein founded Simile to perform a "depth-first search" to scale these capabilities for real-world impact.

The Technical Paradigm: Building the "GPU of Intelligence"

  • The "CPU vs. GPU" Analogy: Park frames today’s frontier models (OpenAI, Anthropic) as the "CPU of intelligence"—highly rational, single models optimized for technical problems with objective answers. Simile is building the "GPU of intelligence," consisting of diverse, parallel subunits designed to represent human irrationality, values, tastes, and varied perspectives.
  • Bridging the Say-Do Gap: Traditional LLMs are trained on attitudinal, web-based text data, which fails to capture how humans actually act. Simile focuses on behavioral datasets, such as randomized controlled trials (RCTs) and pricing studies.
  • Data Collection & Grounding: Rather than relying solely on prompting LLMs with demographic personas, Simile grounds its agents in real human data.
      • Gallup Partnership: Simile partners with polling firm Gallup to recruit real people for qualitative "story of life" interviews and quantitative surveys.
      • Efficiency Engine: Simile uses a reinforcement learning loop to train AI interviewers to extract the maximum amount of "long-tail" personal history in the minimum amount of time.

Commercial Traction and Enterprise Use Cases

  • Predictive Validation: Simile validated its architecture by simulating 1,000 Americans, predicting their behaviors and survey responses with 85% of the accuracy with which humans replicate their own choices.
  • Enterprise Concept Testing: Fortune 500 companies, including CVS, use Simile's SaaS platform to bypass slow, expensive consumer panels.
      • Massive Scale: Instead of testing 5 to 10 product concepts, enterprises can instantly test 1,000 different concepts across 1,000 distinct, representative subpopulations.
  • Simulating Second-Order Effects: Beyond direct feedback, companies can test long-term, systemic market reactions. For example, an automotive client can simulate how launching a new electric vehicle shifts consumer perceptions of their existing internal combustion engine lineup.
  • Earnings Call Simulations: High-level executives and board members regularly use Simile to run multi-agent simulations of upcoming earnings calls to anticipate investor and analyst reactions.

The Long-Term Vision: A "CERN of Human Society"

  • Solving the Social Sciences: Park envisions a future where simulations tackle macroeconomics, policy, and history. Simile aims to establish the statistical baselines for simulation modeling, aiming for a Total Variation Distance (TVD) of less than 0.15 for quantitative decision-making.
  • Simulation Types: Park distinguishes between two core types of human simulations:
      • Convergent Simulations: Dynamics that naturally stabilize regardless of minor errors (e.g., simulating social networks where centralized "hubs" inevitably form, akin to PageRank).
      • Divergent Simulations: Highly chaotic events where minor changes alter the entire outcome (e.g., elections or geopolitical crises). These are run hundreds of times to construct bootstrap confidence intervals and observe the spectrum of possible futures.
  • Grand-Scale Modeling: In the future, running a massive simulation could cost $100 million and take several months, functioning as a high-tech telescope for human systems. This could help policymakers model bank runs, climate cooperation, or the early structural warning signs of a collapsing democracy.

Notable Quotes

"Today's frontier models are built to be super-intelligent, rational, and amazing at objective problems. But people are irrational. We have subjective values, preferences, and tastes. We need a next frontier of modeling that is geared towards actually modeling people's diversity." — Joon Sung Park
"A company is a machine for depth-first search. You have a conviction on an area, you find the hill that you want to climb, and this is the vehicle that lets you put together resources and an amazing group of people to go after a singular vision without hesitation." — Joon Sung Park
"I do see a future where today a simulation is quick and fast to run, but what about a simulation that takes actually a hundred million dollars to run once and could take many months to run? But when we run it, it solves one of the fundamental questions of our society. That is genuinely a very exciting possibility." — Joon Sung Park
"Hubble telescope really changed the trajectory of how we understand the universe. Simulation can be that for human society." — Joon Sung Park
I am somebody who is quite inspired by science fiction, and when you read science fiction that covers societies that have progressed far enough in their technological maturity, you always see two pillars. You have some version of AGI, and you have some version of simulations that really help guide the society. I do see an opportunity today to really take the first crack at building the simulation. I would not have said that even five years ago, but that is the conviction that we have built up over the years as we are going deep into this research. Today we're delighted to have Joon, founder and CEO of Simile. Simile is building an applied AI lab simulating human behavior and societies, and I'm very excited to have you here to discuss what you're building. Same here. Thank you for having me. Okay, take me back to April 2023, Stanford, California—specifically Smallville, Stanford, California. What was that? So, Smallville was a project that we were running at Stanford where the idea was that we made this observation: large language models can now encode a lot of human behavior that is embedded in their training data from the web, social media, and so forth. If you probe it at the right angle, you can actually get a lot of micro-behaviors out of these models. Given a very specific demonstration or description of a situation, what would person X do? It would actually generate really interesting behaviors. We found that to be so interesting, and we found that to be the ingredient we had been waiting for to create really complex agentic behaviors. So, Smallville actually was an experiment where we decided to push this as far as possible to see what a society created by these agents would look like. We basically created generative agents paired with a generative AI model with memory, planning, and reflection to create this lived experience of agents living in this small town. Smallville was basically a game town of 25 agents living in it. Individual agents had a description of a persona, but they would actually wake up in the morning, do their routines, go to work, and actually have relationships sort of like real people would. They would also have emergent phenomena, like having parties and so forth. So, that was the experiment that we ran. What were the most surprising things to come out of the experiment? So, one of the surprising things was that the simulation itself actually takes place the day before Valentine's Day. You actually see these agents—for instance, one of the agents thinking, "Well, I run a cafe." She's a cafe owner named Isabella. She thinks it would be great if she can host a Valentine's Day party where they invite a lot of friends and customers. You actually see her, on the day before Valentine's Day, going around, gathering materials for the party, and telling her customers, "Hey, we're going to have this party, please come." On the day of Valentine's, you actually see this emergent party get formed with all these agents coming to basically the cafe. Did anyone not get invited? Well, some of the people did get invitations but they forgot. That's one thing that did happen. Some of the agents did not explicitly get invited, but we had one agent who got the invite, Klaus, who decided to ask his crush out on a date. He would actually bring his date, and they would actually have a party at this cafe. It was quite surreal. So, how did you end up building Smallville in the first place? Were you studying human psychology and social behavior, or was this coming from the customer side, or was it coming from the technology out? My particular team has been excited about simulations, and we saw the vision of simulation fairly early on. My career as a researcher at Stanford really started back in 2020. That was the year when GPT-3 was about to come out. It wasn't quite there yet, but as it was just about to come out, we started to get its first demos. In my first year, we wrote a paper called "Opportunities and Risks of Foundation Models" alongside many other Stanford researchers. It was led by one of my co-founders, Percy Liang, who is now the head of the Center for Research on Foundation Models at Stanford. When we were writing that, the part that I was really focused on was: here is a new class of models that we have not seen in the past, models that can be very generalizable in ways we didn't quite have before. I got to thinking: if we can imagine the kind of interactions we can create with these models, what would that be? Many of my colleagues back then were surprised that these models could do classification or simple generation. That was really incredible to see because these models weren't explicitly taught to do those things. But the part that was surprising to me wasn't just that they could do that, because from an interaction perspective, we've known how to do that for a long time. The interesting part was that these models can actually encode human behavior. What does that mean if we were to push this as far as possible? Part of the research tradition I come from includes what we call social computing. Social computing within human-computer interaction really has to do with this idea of how we can build better technological platforms that enable social interactions and collaboration. One of the most difficult challenges of building a social platform is not necessarily testing the UI/UX of the system. Rather, it is more about when you have tens of people, millions of people, and down the line billions of people—how do all these people come together to create emergent phenomena that are both good and bad, and how can we design for scale? Until now, we didn't really have a tool that would enable us to test for that. The only way we test it today is to basically field test it. You release your prototype, see what happens, and sometimes it actually comes at a real cost. Obviously, it is a high cost in terms of human hours and the time it takes. But at the same time, if you have a bad design—imagine you have a feed on social media that is more likely to propagate negative emotions—then obviously that is something we want to avoid. Yet, this currently gets tested in the field, so we wanted to see whether we could actually create a simulation that would let you test for this. So in 2022, which was actually a year before generative agents, we worked on a paper called "Social Simulacra," which was the precursor to the agent paper that we ended up writing. The core thesis was: imagine you're building a subreddit. You're a designer on that subreddit, and you want to see what people might do, which is a surprisingly hard task even for practiced designers. We basically decided, hey, we have this model that seems unique; let's use it to create simulations of the entire subreddit. You define the goal, you define the moderation strategies, and you populate it with thousands of what we called personas back then, rather than agents. This was basically the 2022 version of Smallville, which is quite interesting looking back. When we saw that, we actually got a lot of really important insights. We simulated a community where the entire idea was for people to discuss places to sightsee in Pittsburgh. All of a sudden, you start to see these personas collaborate to actually discuss, "Hey, XYZ places are amazing, do you want to go on a trip together?" and actually plan those trips live in this simulated subreddit. That is how we got excited. We saw the vision, the excitement, and the potential applications fairly early on. But then the work we had to do was to demonstrate how we can go beyond simple personas to create complex agents that can actually think over time—because we want to simulate the longitudinal aspects of our society—and then validate that these simulations are actually accurate in practice. Was there a point of model evolution at which you felt like, okay, we're there, the models are good enough for us to actually have a faithful representation of human society? GPT-3, when it came out, was what Social Simulacra was built with, and it was very janky. It didn't do any instruction tuning and did not follow your instructions, so just to get it to listen to you and do what you wanted, you had to do some weird tricks with prompting and so forth. But you could actually see the promise. The model had encoded a lot of human behavior, and you could actually see the trajectory. By the time we wrote the Generative Agents paper, it wasn't quite ChatGPT yet, but we now had instruction tuning. This allowed us to build much more complex agents that could reason about their memory, which wasn't really possible when we did Social Simulacra. Since then, of course, the models have improved, so where we are today is that models at their foundation level have reached the point where we can actually imagine building these kinds of applications. Now, the part that I do think is quite interesting here is that if you look at many of the large language model companies today—whether it's OpenAI, Anthropic, or many of the new labs that are getting formed—their North Star is to build super-intelligent machines. These machines are meant to be rational, and they are supposed to be really amazing at technical problems that have an objective answer. So maybe that's not even the best simulation of true human society then. Turns out people are irrational, right? We have a lot of subjective values, preferences, and tastes. You actually start to see a divergence where model size goes up, but there is a plateau in its ability to predict and simulate human behavior. With the current modeling paradigm, our ability to really simulate humans has sort of plateaued. It is at a good starting foundational level, but to make it really amazing, we need the next frontier, which is more geared towards actually modeling people's diversity. Very interesting. At what point did you realize that what you did with Smallville could become a company? Right. Again, the promise of application was something that I was very much inspired by early on by simulation with Social Simulacra and so forth. But what I realized over time is that research and a company have very different functions. Research is an amazing vehicle if you want to basically do breadth-first search. You are in a lab surrounded by a really smart set of people, where each researcher owns a small piece of a thesis, and some of those theses blossom into amazing research products. But we are not necessarily known for finishing our job, and we are not usually the ones to bring that research impact to the real world. A company, on the other hand, is a machine for depth-first search. You have a conviction on an area, you find the hill that you want to climb, and this is the vehicle that lets you put together resources and an amazing group of people to go after a singular vision without hesitation. We got that conviction, I would say, about half a year after Generative Agents. After the original Generative Agents paper, we got so much inbound interest. Initially, it was actually from social scientists who wanted to run their experiments and RCTs on our platform. Very soon after, many Fortune 500 companies saw the demo—their board members and CEOs who sometimes visit Stanford saw it and started asking, "Well, we run all these surveys and experiments, and there are so many research questions about the market that we cannot answer today. Can we run that in simulation?" That started to really intrigue me because it showed a clear line toward real-world impact for research, which is not always an opportunity we get. So that is when we decided we actually wanted to validate whether the simulations are accurate. We went out and actually created simulations of a thousand people representative of the US population. We demonstrated that using our architecture and models, we can actually predict people's behaviors 85% as accurately as people replicate their own. When we saw that, we thought, okay, this is something that we feel comfortable providing to our users as a platform for simulating their really important decisions. That is when the co-founders—myself, Percy, as well as Michael Bernstein, who was a researcher and my advisor at Stanford—got together. Both of them were actually my advisors, and the three of us had been working together for five years at that point, and now at Simile for six years. That was when we got together to have the initial conversation of: can this be a company? Got it. Amazing. Maybe walk me through a customer engagement end-to-end today. Like who's a canonical customer in which department, and when they come to you, what are they asking you, and what product or service do you deliver to them? To give a concrete example, CVS has been partnering with Simile for nearly half a year, and they've been an amazing partner. Our main buyer at CVS is a Senior VP who leads human insights. The original story there was that he basically read my paper validating the agent simulations and thought, "We have to bring this to CVS because today we are bottlenecked by the number of questions we can field test, and we are also bottlenecked by the physics of human society." It's one thing to ask surveys and run experiments, but a totally different thing if down the line you actually want to simulate the entire market and map out all the second-order impacts of the decisions you suggest to your leadership. He had been looking around for that solution, and his cousin happened to know me and told our buyer, Shri, that the authors of the paper were actually looking to start something. So, that's how we got connected. In this particular engagement, the way this usually goes is that our customers are very much used to working with polling or panel companies. They typically go and ask these companies to help them understand specific populations of interest and run research studies on those topics. That initial stage looks very similar for Simile. Our buyers come and tell us they want to better understand a certain population. Then Simile goes out—and through our partnership with vendors, including a strategic partnership with Gallup—to actually reach out to real humans. These simulations are grounded in real data. We work with our vendors to collect data that we believe is efficient and generalizable about that person. Imagine you have 15 minutes: what are the magical questions you can ask these people during that time? We collect that data and use it to create agents or simulations of these people, which can then be used to answer a large number of questions going way beyond the original domain. We load that onto our platform, and it is basically a SaaS product. Our customers can then log in and ask any question about the group of people they are interested in. So interesting. It reminds me of in autonomous vehicles, you know, you go and collect a bunch of data from the road and then you're able to augment it with simulation. Is this a similar concept or are there big differences to what you're doing here? It is a similar concept. With self-driving vehicles, of course you want to create a model that is based on real-world physics, but you also want to create a model that is generalizable beyond your training data, into different locations with different weather conditions. It is a very similar concept here. We want to reach out to real people, understand something fundamental about them, and encode that into the model. I would have thought that the large language models would be such a good representation of, you know, the whole world that you could almost narrow it down. You could tell Claude, "You are a 34-year-old woman living in a, you know, bicoastal metropolitan area" and you know, it would be able to have a faithful representation. So I'm actually surprised that you go out to Gallup. Maybe can you just explain why you have to go out and collect any real-world data at all? Yeah. One of the big questions here is the "say-do" gap. There are things that people say, and then there are things that people actually do, and the gap between them is real. A lot of large language models are trained on attitudinal data. Fundamentally, it is the things that people have said online, which covers a large quantity of their training data. One of the things that Simile's simulation platform does is actually closing that gap. So, a lot of the data that we end up collecting is behavioral by nature. It also includes data that actually comes from questions like, "Just tell me the story of your life." It turns out if we understand the story of a person's life, the kind of data we get is what we consider to be the long-tail information about this person. It's not about what you've done in this particular moment, and it's not about very broad questions like, "What's your view on politics?" It's about where you grew up and what some of the difficult decisions were that you had to make in life. What's interesting about this data is it is an amazing way to build a translational layer between attitudes and behavior. We combine these kinds of datasets, but fundamentally, that is the gap we want to close. What sort of behavioral data do you have? Simile does run a lot of experiments. For instance, we have a huge repository of RCTs—randomized controlled trials run in social scientific contexts and around pricing studies. One of the models we are training is basically the foundation model of human behavior in a very literal sense. We have all these behavioral signals from RCTs, and we want to encode them into the model so that the end outcome is a model that can basically predict the results of any RCT. At the same time, our customers see that potential and their minds immediately go to, "Wow, we have 90 million customers at CVS. How can we leverage this kind of data to create better simulations?" So, there are also conversations around how we can responsibly and ethically leverage existing in-house data from our customers to create augmented versions of Simile's model. That, of course, is going to be fine-tuned specifically to the population of these customers, but that is the kind of data we would be leveraging. And are you doing these interviews typically by voice? Is it a survey that you filled out? What's the modality? It's a huge breadth. The quick answer here is that it's both. Interviews are fantastic if you want to get long-tail information about people. In the original study that I conducted back in 2024, we literally asked the question, "Tell me the story of your life." Now, the way we do it is we train our own model, so it's a reinforcement learning loop. Basically, imagine the objective function here is: how can you spend the minimum amount of time to get the maximum amount of visibility about this person? That is one of the things we do. We are basically training an interviewer that is not really asking for factual information or experience about a particular platform, but just about the life stories that people have. That can then be used to train our models for these agents. For the more factual or discrete choice questions, surveys are also very efficient. They are time- and data-efficient because people can fill out many questions in a short period of time. We leverage them when we just want to have a broad understanding of people's viewpoints on certain topics, policies, and things like that. You describe yourself as an applied AI lab. How do you think about where you want to build your own models versus where you want to rely on other existing models? In terms of building our own models, the core thesis here is that there is an amazing model to be built that really encodes the diversity of people's values, preferences, and tastes in ways that a purely rational model cannot do. One way I actually pose this is to imagine today's models as akin to the CPU of an intelligence unit. It's a single model trained on amazingly rational data that is great at solving very complex, objective questions. Simile's model is much more akin to developing something close to the GPU of the intelligence unit. The idea here is we don't actually need a model that is superhuman; in fact, we want a model that is as human as possible. We want to make sure that these models, at the level of individual sub-units, can represent real viewpoints of different subpopulations. Where we see that gap, that is when we go develop our own model. At the same time, we do leverage frontier models as a way to coordinate the research. Frontier models are amazing at coming up with a research plan, so that is where those models actually get leveraged. Are people typically coming to you with questions around new product launches, how they should be marketing their companies, pricing, all of the above? It is all of the above. Our customer journey usually starts with very concrete use cases and problems they are trying to solve. Concept testing is a big one, and it is also very straightforward. They have a new concept, a new product idea, or a new marketing message they want to test, and they want to hear what users think about it. This is a way for them to quickly test those ideas. The promise they quickly see is that, while right now they are in the practice of testing five to ten different ideas at most, with simulation they can instantly test a thousand different ideas across a thousand different subpopulations. That is the initial vision they see. From there, we really get into the nitty-gritty details of where simulation goes. They pretty soon start asking, "Can this be used to do product testing—not just simply submitting an image, but asking these agents to go experience this product for 10 minutes and tell us what they saw?" You are basically adding a temporal dimension. Then you go into things like multi-agent simulation. Some of our customers actually routinely ask us to simulate their earnings calls. This is a use case that surprised me at first, but it is a surprisingly common ask because CEOs and board members always need to think about how they are going to design their earnings calls and how the audience will react. So that is something we do, and it is very much a multi-agent simulation. You know, it seems like there's so many use cases that could potentially be tested once you have like a simulated almost customer population, right? I'm curious the value of research and testing in Sim versus just like, let's say you have a new product concept that you want to test. Why not just go run a thousand Facebook ads and like you actually get the click-through rates on this stuff? Isn't that real-world data almost more useful than the simulated data on how people might behave that you then correct for with your own models? It's a great question. I think the answer has to do with scale initially, and then down the line, the new capabilities that come because you can simulate interactions. The scale question is quite straightforward: yes, you can absolutely run Facebook ads and tests, but the kind of experiments you can run in a simulation are actual behavior simulations at scale. You can basically pull in any number of users—it doesn't even have to be bounded by the population available on Facebook. It is also much more representative, because only certain groups of people will actually respond to online experiments. One of the key promises of the models we are creating at Simile is representativeness; we do the hard work of gaining a representative set of people and collecting the data to represent them properly. Scale and representativeness are things that many of our users do not have easy access to. One of the common pain points we hear is that their big bottleneck isn't even about what questions to ask, but rather how to access the population they are excited to talk to in the first place. Down the line, you can really start to imagine what all the downstream implications of your decisions are. It is not just about whether you like a product, or whether you would pay for it. Imagine you're a car company and you launch an electric vehicle. It might do really well, and we can help you with concept testing around its marketing, but what does that do to the perception of your non-electric vehicles? Does it change the market perception, and what does that mean for the rest of your product line? How do you balance those kinds of second-order impacts in a way that is evidence-based? Today, there is no way to test for this outside of simulation. Going beyond asking one question at a time to think about the long-term, systemic implications of decisions is something our customers are quite excited by. I'd love to understand how you think about how predictive your model is in actually simulating real human behavior. I imagine you have a lot of evals on this. I guess what is your North Star metric? How do you guys do on that and you know, what do you think is the theoretical limit? It's a great question. A theoretical limit certainly does exist, in the sense that there is a lot of genuine randomness in humans. If you ask me the same question twice, I might answer it slightly differently. There is certainly that degree of randomness in human behavior, but there are still huge gains in performance we can achieve today in the way we predict people. At the level of a population, we measure the distribution of responses if it is quantitative. We measure Total Variation Distance (TVD), which shows how close the simulated distributions are to the ground truth. That is a metric we run across all the use cases our customers have, and we have a certain threshold that we believe is good enough for decision-making. A TVD of less than 0.15, for example, is actually quite strong evidence for making a decision. That is the North Star state we want to hit for quantitative use cases, including RCTs. But then there is a really interesting question to ask around multi-agent simulations and downstream implications: what does the evaluation of those look like? Yeah, and then do daisy chain errors as you kind of, you know, if this one is 85% accurate and then this agent is telling another agent something and they, you know, like do you accumulate errors as you go, as you go towards multi-agent? Exactly. One of our core theses is that we basically see two categories of simulations: those that converge and those that diverge. Sometimes they actually coexist, and it really depends on what research question you have. For questions that converge, it doesn't actually matter if you have a little bit of error. The error cannot be so dramatic that it is completely detached from reality, but you are okay even if the errors compound over time, because the pull toward convergence is strong enough that you understand where everything will fall. A good example is simulating a network of people; that network will always have a hub that gets formed, which is what network scientists call a scale-free network. This is actually what powered Google, too. One of the core observations of PageRank was that it doesn't matter how these networks formulate, you always see some web pages that get exponentially more links. This is a fundamental behavior in humans that we also see in simulated networks, and that convergence always happens as long as you replicate human behavior with a certain threshold of accuracy. Then there are questions that genuinely do diverge, like classical historical questions: was World War I inevitable or not? There, it is sometimes difficult to run the same simulation over time and get the exact same outcome. Imagine you are running a simulation of an election—will the same person win every time? There are a lot of downstream implications of every single decision, so it diverges. In those cases, the core evaluation is around confidence. If you run the simulation a hundred times, how many of those times does the result come out to be X? We can use that to calculate confidence using bootstrap resampling. A huge part of the power of simulation is to show the diversity of possible outcomes when things diverge, so that people can understand the causal mechanisms and prepare for those futures. Are there any mathematical descriptions of like why something would converge or diverge? Like I'm imagining if you have like an average function maybe you converge and then if it's like a, you know, if you're splitting outcomes to a binary then you might maybe diverge. But like... Yeah. The intuition, I think, is close, and this is technically a research topic. Simile is certainly a company where we go deep into these research topics. I see simulation as a field that is akin to developing the early days of inferential statistics. Inferential statistics scientists actually had to do a lot of discussion and research over time to decide that a p-value of less than 0.05 is strong enough evidence for science. Simile is working on setting the same kind of thresholds and standards for this field. Your intuition is exactly right, but how to make a robust mathematical equation around when something is going to converge or diverge remains a real research frontier for simulation. Thank you for being nice to my "vibe mathing." I'm curious, though. A lot of Fortune 500 companies are coming to you, but I'm wondering whether there are non-corporate use cases that might solve some of the great mysteries of our society. For example, I'm wondering about economics and Central Bank decisions. Oftentimes, I've personally believed that in macro, nobody knows anything, and a lot of the issues come about from human psychology. To me, macroeconomics is a function of simulating human behavior at scale. I'm thinking even in the venture capital use case, where we often debate internally: "Does value accrue to this company or not?" You could run a simulation of all the different layers of the AI stack and almost figure out where durability and value accrue. If you had a perfect simulator of human behavior, there is so much more you could do than serving the Fortune 500. Do you agree with that? And if so, are you serving governments or the like? Yeah, it's interesting. When we were still researching in this area, the way I actually got my advisors back then, Michael and Percy, excited was by telling them, "Look, if we do this right, there's a Nobel Prize to be won there." I truly believe that. It's not surprising when you look at classical economics simulations like agent-based models, which pioneered our understanding of things. Back in the day, they studied topics like, "How does segregation happen?" and, "What are the causal mechanisms for segregation?" Scholars like Thomas Schelling built agent-based models that were extremely simple and rudimentary, but showed something deep about human macro-behaviors, and he went on to win a Nobel Prize. I see the same opportunity here but in an augmented way. Back in the day, agent-based models were very deterministic. In a segregation model from 30 years ago, an individual agent was simply a red dot or a blue dot. Every game iteration, they would look around their corner, see how many of their neighbors were of the same color, and if it fell below a certain threshold, they would move to a new location. That was it. But now, we can actually create real agents that replicate the full richness of individuals and run those exact same kinds of simulations. The kinds of questions we can ask go far beyond commercial use cases. In the context of macroeconomics, some of the questions I have actually been asked by economists are things like, "When does a bank run happen?" or questions around climate change. One of the core blockers of solving climate change is the collective action problem of many nations—can we actually simulate that? What are the signals of a democracy that is about to collapse? Can we understand the origin story of the monetary system? These are the kinds of simulations that I believe ought to be the North Star of this field. It is interesting to imagine what that would look like in practice, because these would involve very large-scale simulations with many agents interacting with each other. I see a future where—unlike today, where simulations are quick and fast to run—we might have a simulation that takes a hundred million dollars to run once, and takes many months to run. But when we run it, it solves one of the fundamental questions of our society. That is genuinely a very exciting possibility. I agree. I'm even thinking that politics, for example, could be forever changed. Today, everyone has an agenda when they argue how a policy change will impact things. Why don't we just run the simulation and understand all the downstream implications—not just what's going to happen this year, but what it means in the next 5 to 10 years? Exactly. Fascinating. I was going to close by asking you what makes you excited about the future. Is it what we just talked about or is it something else? I am somebody who is quite inspired by science fiction, and when you read science fiction that covers societies that have progressed far enough in their technological maturity, you always see two pillars. You have some version of AGI, and you have some version of simulations that really help guide the society. I do see an opportunity today to really take the first crack at building the simulation. I would not have said that even five years ago, but that is the conviction that we have built up over the years as we are going deep into this research. What's exciting is there's a lot of focus on natural sciences, but how can social simulation really unlock our understanding of humanity and social sciences? How can we actually use that to make our society a better place? That's exciting. Totally. I remember reading that somebody was excited about a small but breathtaking chance that the field of economics, as we know it, may actually be solved by simulation. I would extend that not just to economics, but to everything that deals with human behavior and social sciences, which ultimately is everything around us. Truly. Wonderful. Thank you so much for joining today and sharing the story of both Smallville and what you're now up to at Simile. I really enjoyed the conversation. Same here. Thank you for having me.

AP EXCLUSIVE: Nvidia CEO Jensen Huang says society needs 'new social norms' in age of AI

Associated Press(4.5M)·51:43·2.9K views·2026-06-16
Jensen HuangCEO, NVIDIA
TL;DRNvidia CEO Jensen Huang outlines a massive, multi-trillion-dollar transition to reinvent modern computing, emphasizing that the U.S.
must pair world-class AI model development with domestic manufacturing, robust energy growth, and updated social norms to lead this new...

TL;DR

Nvidia CEO Jensen Huang outlines a massive, multi-trillion-dollar transition to reinvent modern computing, emphasizing that the U.S. must pair world-class AI model development with domestic manufacturing, robust energy growth, and updated social norms to lead this new industrial revolution.

Reinventing Computing and the Next Era of AI

  • A 60-Year Paradigm Shift: Jensen Huang states that the tech industry is reinventing the computer for the first time since the invention of the IBM System/360 in 1964.
      • Computers are transitioning from pre-recorded, coded instructions to real-time problem-solving systems.
      • Over the next 10 years, tens of trillions of dollars in capital expenditure will be spent to replace the old global computing infrastructure.
  • Smarter, Truth-Grounded Models: Future AI will be safer, have better guardrails, and be grounded in deeper truth.
      • Rather than "guessing" answers, next-generation AI will actively research, evaluate multiple sources of information, and analyze truthfulness before delivering results.

Re-industrialization and Silicon Photonics in Texas

  • Strategic Coherent-Nvidia Partnership: Nvidia is partnering with Coherent at their Sherman, Texas facility, utilizing a $50 million federal CHIPS Act award to strengthen the domestic AI supply chain.
      • The partnership will quadruple the output of the Sherman facility in just one year to meet the massive demand for accelerated computing.
  • The Role of Silicon Photonics: The Sherman plant manufactures world-leading 6-inch indium phosphide lasers.
      • These lasers convert electrical signals to light (silicon photonics), allowing chips separated by hundreds or thousands of feet inside an AI factory to connect and work seamlessly as a single supercomputer.
  • U.S. Manufacturing Revival: The rapid build-out of AI data centers, chip fabs, and packaging plants has already created approximately 600,000 jobs in the U.S.
      • Huang emphasizes that this shift brings back high-paying manufacturing jobs for "builders and makers," balancing an economy previously dominated by information workers.

The Five Layers of AI and the Energy Deficit

  • The AI Stack: Huang outlines the five foundational layers of AI: Energy, Chips, Infrastructure, Models, and Applications.
  • The U.S. Energy Bottleneck: While the U.S. is world-class in chips, infrastructure, and models, it is "woefully behind" in energy production.
      • Energy Imperative: Huang argues that AI data centers present a once-in-a-generation opportunity to use market forces—completely free of government subsidies—to fund sustainable energy, nuclear power, and grid upgrades. Without pro-energy growth, the U.S. cannot sustain its AI infrastructure.
  • The Application Layer: U.S. society must overcome its hesitation to adopt AI at the top application layer (e.g., healthcare, manufacturing, and transport) to avoid being left behind by international competitors.

Geopolitics, Export Controls, and Taiwan's Ecosystem

  • Balancing National Security and Economic Prosperity: While national security must be a top priority, Huang warns against "over-exercising" export controls, which could have unintended consequences for U.S. economic leadership.
      • The China Market: Huang stresses that the U.S. should continue to export previous-generation hardware to compete in China, noting that completely ceding the massive Chinese market would only force regional competitors to scale and grow into global giants.
  • Taiwan as a Critical Partner: Taiwan remains the global epicenter of the chip supply chain.
      • Beyond manufacturing chips, Taiwanese tech giants (such as TSMC, Foxconn, and Wistron) are strategic partners actively helping the U.S. establish domestic fabs and assembly plants in states like Arizona.

Adapting to AI: Social Norms and Public Engagement

  • The Need for New Social Norms: Huang compares the anxiety surrounding AI to the early days of automobiles, when society had to develop new social norms, sidewalks, crosswalks, and seatbelts to safely integrate cars.
      • "We have to deal with regulation, technology, and social norms at the same time."
  • Lowering the Tech Divide: AI is the most accessible technology in history because users do not need to learn coding to use it; they can simply talk to it.
  • Active Public Engagement: Huang urges the public, including workers concerned about job displacement, to directly try free AI tools like ChatGPT to explore how it can assist them in their existing careers.
  • Skepticism Toward Government Ownership: Addressing floated proposals regarding government ownership stakes in AI companies, Huang questioned the necessity, pointing out that Americans already naturally benefit from these domestic companies through tax revenues, jobs, and investment portfolios.

Notable Quotes

"We're reinventing the computer for the first time in 60 years... You no longer have to code, and no longer record information in advance... It is able to now solve problems in real time." — Jensen Huang, Nvidia CEO
"When automobiles came, we needed to create new social norms... We have sidewalks, we have crosswalks, we have jaywalking discouraged. All of that combination of social norms, regulations, safer cars, seatbelts... it's all of it at the same time [for AI]." — Jensen Huang, Nvidia CEO
"It is a false choice to have... we can have national security, that is absolutely foremost, we want our technology industry to succeed around the world, and in order to do that, you have to compete." — Jensen Huang, Nvidia CEO

That goes down a whole bunch of optics, travels as far as you need it to travel, and on the other side of it, you pick up the photon with detectors, and you convert that back to electricity, and that electricity then drives our chip. Now the two chips, or millions of chips, are communicating with each other. Data + AI Summit Keynote 2026 | Day 1

Databricks(160.0K)·179:24·12.5K views·2026-06-16
Ali GhodsiCo-founder and CEO, Databricks Matei ZahariaCo-founder and CTO, Databricks
TL;DRAt the Data + AI Summit 2026, Databricks unveiled Genie 1, an enterprise-grounded AI coworker, alongside major data-layer unifications including Lakehouse RT, Lakebase Postgres, and LTAP—a storage-level technology that eliminates complex Change Data ...
Capture (CDC)...

TL;DR

At the Data + AI Summit 2026, Databricks unveiled Genie 1, an enterprise-grounded AI coworker, alongside major data-layer unifications including Lakehouse RT, Lakebase Postgres, and LTAP—a storage-level technology that eliminates complex Change Data Capture (CDC) pipelines by natively transcoding operational data into open lakehouse formats.

The Agentic Shift: Genie 1 and Genie Ontology

  • Context Over Intelligence: Databricks CEO Ali Ghodsi emphasized that frontier AI models do not suffer from an intelligence problem, but rather a context problem. To drive autonomous work, AIs must be grounded in an organization's specific data, operational history, and security structures.
  • Genie Ontology and OntoRank: To address the cost and accuracy limitations of generic AI agents, Databricks introduced Genie Ontology.
      • Automated Context Graph: Running in the background, it constructs a comprehensive knowledge graph spanning Databricks tables, Google Drive, SharePoint, email, and calendars.
      • The OntoRank Algorithm: A proprietary, PageRank-style algorithm designed by the Databricks research team to assess the authority of various enterprise data assets, code snippets, and organizational relationships.
      • Proven Results: Internal benchmarks show a 30% improvement in reasoning accuracy and a 50% reduction in query runtimes compared to standard coding agents.
  • Genie 1 Launch: Now generally available, Genie 1 is positioned as a data-smart AI coworker integrated natively with Teams, Slack, iOS, and Android. To accelerate adoption, Databricks is offering $10 per user/month in free token credits.
  • Expanded Genie Family:
      • Genie Agents: Enables business users to quickly turn conversations or specific document folders into shared, custom agents (e.g., automated HR bots).
      • Genie Code: A specialized coding agent optimized for data engineering, pipeline writing, and automated machine learning modeling.

Breaking the OLTP/OLAP Barrier: Lakehouse RT, Lakebase, and LTAP

  • The HTAP Problem Solved: Historically, Hybrid Transactional/Analytical Processing (HTAP) failed due to engine complexity and storage compromises. Databricks introduced LTAP (Lake Transactional Analytical Processing) to unify these systems at the storage layer.
      • Transcoding at Storage: Using idle CPU capacity on storage services, LTAP transcodes row-oriented transactional data into column-oriented formats (Delta/Iceberg) inside the data lake in real time, bypassing performance bottlenecks.
      • Eliminating CDC: This architecture completely bypasses fragile Change Data Capture (CDC) pipelines. Operational tables updated in Lakebase instantly appear as column-oriented tables in the Lakehouse with no pipeline maintenance required.
  • Lakebase (Serverless Postgres on the Lake): A fully managed, decoupled operational database that stores data cheaply on the lake.
      • Sub-Second Provisioning: Provisions instances and generates branches/clones in under 500 milliseconds, allowing agents to instantly create isolated testing environments.
      • Cross-Cloud Disaster Recovery: Offers fully managed, cross-cloud replication (e.g., active replication from AWS US-West to Azure US-East) for instant failover during cloud outages.
  • Lakehouse RT (Powered by the Reyden Engine): A real-time analytical engine designed for strict sub-second SLAs.
      • Algorithmic Dispatching: Utilizes machine learning models trained on trillions of historical queries to select the most efficient processing algorithm at runtime.
      • High-Throughput Performance: Demonstrated executing 12,000 queries per second with a tail latency of only 37 milliseconds on standardized TPC-H benchmarks.

Automating Data Pipelines: Lakeflow and Genie ZeroOps

  • Lakeflow Ingest & Design Upgrades:
      • Lakeflow Connect: Expanded to include over 100 native enterprise connectors.
      • Zerobus Ingest: A fully managed, Kafka-wire-compatible service that lands streaming data directly into the Lakehouse at up to 12 GB/s without generating fragmented small files.
      • Lakeflow Designer: Generally available visual, no-code data preparation environment that compiles directly into open-source Spark Declarative Pipelines.
      • Spark Real-Time Mode (RTM): An open-source streaming framework capable of running continuous pipelines with sub-10 millisecond latency.
  • Genie ZeroOps (Autonomous Maintenance): Recognizing that data engineers spend over 50% of their time on pipeline maintenance, Databricks launched Genie ZeroOps to act as an autopilot for data operations.
      • Data-Plane Residency: Unlike external agents, ZeroOps lives directly inside the secure data plane, eliminating security risks while providing native access to metrics, logs, and lineage.
      • End-to-End Self-Healing: The agent automatically trains table-specific ML models to detect anomalies, traces root causes using Unity Catalog lineage, builds safe fixes, and tests them in isolated shallow clones before prompting engineers for PR approvals.

Enterprise Governance and Agent Control: Unity AI Gateway

  • Taming Runaway AI Costs: To control skyrocketing token consumption and govern autonomous agents, Databricks announced the Unity AI Gateway, integrated directly into Unity Catalog and MLflow.
      • Centralized Observability: Provides a single pane of glass to track token spend, enforce safety guardrails, and audit outputs.
      • User-Level Budgets: Allows administrators to set granular, rate-limited spend ceilings down to individual user groups and employees.
      • Token Flexibility: Enables enterprises to apply their committed Databricks contract spend directly toward frontier model tokens (OpenAI, Anthropic, Gemini) across any cloud.
      • Model Context Protocol (MCP): Authenticates and manages both internal and third-party MCP servers, providing single-sign-on access to agent skills.
  • OpenSharing: An open-source extension of Delta Sharing that allows cross-organizational sharing of both Delta/Iceberg datasets and active AI assets (agents, models, and skills).

Strategic Acquisitions and Platform Expansions

  • Panther Labs Acquisition: Databricks announced an agreement to acquire Panther Labs, a Python-based security platform. Panther's technology will power Lakewatch, an agentic, cloud-scale SIEM (Security Information and Event Management) system that stores security telemetry cheaply in the open lakehouse and deploys agents to autonomously triage threats.
  • CustomerLake: A new agentic Customer Data Platform (CDP) built directly on top of the Lakehouse. It leverages LLMs for automated customer identity deduplication and introduces "Infinity Campaigns" to generate continuous, hyper-personalized consumer marketing.

Notable Quotes

"AI does not have an intelligence problem right now... The problem we have is that AGI is not really completely permeating our organizations. Inside of your companies, you don't have hundreds of agents working for you doing autonomous work." — *Ali Ghodsi, CEO of Databricks*
"With LTAP we can finally combine the separate continents of OLTP and analytics into a single giant continent... [It] is HTAP done right and accomplishes the goals of HTAP without actually having a single query engine for it." — *Reynold Xin, Co-Founder of Databricks*
"One thing we really stand for at OpenAI is keeping humans at the center... That means we have this opportunity to sort of leverage these systems in cases that we really want, but also have a lot of time to think about where do we want the humans to fundamentally remain." — *Greg Brockman, President and Co-Founder of OpenAI*

So how do we do this without getting locked in both to the data, the context, but most importantly also now to the AI? We don't want to get locked in just to one particular AI. We want to have choice when it comes to the AI. So that's what we're focused on today. My whole keynote is just focused on how do we solve these four, okay? So the four things we're focused on is we want you to have choice and not get locked in, we want to be able to get the security and the control and the cost control as well as the context, and then get that to the AI, and that way we can have more impact together. Okay, so how do we do that? So let's start with choice, okay? It started with your data, as I mentioned. So we have all the data, and we want to get that data in an AI-ready form. So step number one is we've actually made huge progress just in the last year to get that data and move it into an open lakehouse. So open infrastructure on top of any of the clouds, and we get your data ready there. That's what Lakeflow does. So Lakeflow has actually been developing a lot of connectors over the last year. Two years ago when I was here, we barely had any connectors, and today we now have over 100 connectors. So Lakeflow can now connect to all these different systems that you have. It's not just Salesforce, Workday, NetSuite. It can pull data from Meta or Google Analytics or from any of your docs or any of the corpus. And if there's any data source that you do not have, please let us know and we can help you get those into the open lake, okay? And it's not just getting the data in there, we can do it in many different modalities. So three things that I would like to highlight: one is we released ZeroBus last year, it is now GA, and then actually we've added a lot of capabilities to it. So what does ZeroBus do? So as the name implies, you don't need a bus anymore for the data infrastructure. So instead of using something like Apache Kafka, you can just hit this API of ZeroBus and you can give it data as it comes in. It could be bursty, it could be tiny rows. You don't need to worry about having millions of files or any buffering or anything like that. ZeroBus just takes care of taking all those entries that you hit it with at a very high rate and it just makes sure that those appear in your open lake in a way that you can probe it later, okay? Scalable, manageable. Okay, so that's great. And then last year we announced Spark Real-Time Mode. Spark Real-Time Mode, or RTM as we call it, is an open-source framework that lets us actually get down to tens of milliseconds. So this is really amazing. We can do now operations super fast. Historically, Spark was great for everything but you couldn't really get down to 10 milliseconds, okay? The real-time streaming would always take this micro-batch of one second or so. But with Real-Time Mode, we can now really make that super fast. So many of you, for your streaming infrastructure, you might have Apache Flink. That was the only option that many of you would pick. But now you can actually do a lot of those workloads directly in Apache Spark with Real-Time Mode which is open source. Okay, and the final one is Lakeflow Designer. I personally actually really like doing all of my data engineering and data movement using Lakeflow Designer. So the way Lakeflow Designer works is that it kind of looks like Alteryx. It's visual. You don't need to see any code, but you speak to the AI, which is Genie, and you tell it, "Hey, I want to do this transformation, I want to join these two tables." And it just visually shows you what that looks like, and then you approve it. But under the hood, it's all just Spark, open-source Spark. And you can actually inspect the code, you can version control it, you can make it production-ready. So that's super awesome. Okay, so those are three things, they're all GA.
