All right, so it doesn't really matter. Actually, if you're using Databricks, it doesn't matter if it's in Delta or Iceberg. We don't care. It's actually the same now, thanks to V3; it's the same data format that's on the disk. Okay, perfect. So that's that. So now we're done with how you can actually get your data into this open format. It's really seamless, just one way of doing it. It's all Spark-based under the hood. So let's move on and look at how we actually then start doing analytics on that data. The data warehouse that we announced about four or five years ago, we call it lakehouse. In fact, our tagline was "The best data warehouse is the lakehouse" because it's open, so you control the data yourself, and it works with machine learning workloads. And we made huge progress on our lakehouse in the last year since the last Data and AI Summit. We've added over 110 features that legacy data warehouses have, so that we can more easily migrate those over. We also released a lot of AI functions. This is the main thing that people want with lakehouse. So there are all these AI functions you can just call. You have your data, maybe they're in tables, that's the kind of thing you work with in data warehousing. But now you can just in your SQL queries call these SQL AI functions, and you can say, "Can you do sentiment analysis, tell me for each of these cells what do people think?" Or you can tell, "Hey, for each of these cells can you extract for me the company names that you see in it?" Or, "Can you do a prediction of what this number is going to be?" And it can just do that at really high scale. So this is the most popular thing that people do, actually, on lakehouse. And we've taken Lakebridge, which is an automated converter that lets you migrate from existing data warehouses that you might have on prem or elsewhere in the cloud, and we can do that now really, really well. So, we're very excited about that. And actually, you all are using this, so we've over doubled the consumption of our data warehouse just in the last year. So we're very excited about this. But what I'm really excited about is my co-founder Reynold getting on stage and talking about the new engine. Okay, so we have a new engine. He's going to talk about it. It's called Reyden. And what's really cool about Reyden is that it's the world's fastest engine that we've ever seen. We've benchmarked it against anything else. We've got the latency of it down to just tens of milliseconds. So far faster than anything we had done before. So we'll have him in a couple of talks in the keynote today talking about this. Okay, so that's our data warehouse. That's the lakehouse. What about transaction processing? So I mentioned in the beginning, we really love Postgres. We've really embraced Postgres. We think AIs today really prefer Postgres. If you ask any of the AIs today what kind of database should I pick, they will say open source Postgres. But we're excited about what we call Lakebase, which is a way in which you take the Postgres and you separate it out so that you have all of its storage just sit on cheap lakes. Okay, so now we're talking Lakebase, so this is OLTP, right? It's like MySQL, Oracle, transaction processing. And once you have that data sitting on the lake, you can now start doing amazing things on top. So what can we do on top? Well, we have a little animation here. If you look, you can get serverless autoscaling. So you can scale up and down Postgres. And this is really fast. So you can do it in less than a second. You can scale it even down to zero. That's really awesome because if nobody is using it in the middle of the night, you don't have to pay for it. You just pay for cheap storage that's sitting in the lake. Okay, this was not possible before. You know, others had specialized storage, but this is just sitting in an open source format on your lake. So why do you care about that? Why should your company care about that? Because it significantly cuts the cost of running databases down. So you get a significant reduction in TCO by doing this kind of autoscaling. So that's a game-changer. But that's actually not even the coolest feature of Lakebase Postgres. The best thing about Lakebase Postgres is that it has this feature that agents really love. And that feature is called branching. So it kind of looks like that. I don't know, that's our attempt to animate and visualize that. But what that means is that you can take a gigantic database, petabytes, and you can say, "Hey, can you make a clone of this for me?" And it will in less than a second say, "Done." What it's doing is not actually copying the whole database. It's just keeping track. The data is anyway sitting on the lake. It'll just say, "Okay, we have two copies," but it's actually just one copy. And then when you make a change to one, it'll just keep track of like, "Okay, this change is in this copy, this other change doesn't exist in the first copy." So, it's what's called copy-on-write. So it actually does that for you. Why does that matter? Well, it matters because it actually completely changes the way you organize databases inside of an enterprise. Most enterprises have thousands of database instances that they're running and we have DBAs that are running around trying to manage all of these. And the way you do that—the reason you have thousands of these and you pay for all these thousands of copies of the data that are siloed inside of the enterprise—is because you need to have a user acceptance testing database. You need to have production database 1, production database number 2. You need to have a staging database, an R&D database. So you have all these copies. But branching, what it enables is to just have one instance. And then you just have different branches of it. And that's just lower TCO, easier to manage, and agents love that. Because agents love to just branch out and just experiment with the data, try something out, and they want to do it quickly, so that one-second latency is very important for them. They don't want to wait 10 minutes on a database to come up. And then finally, if something goes wrong, they want to be able to go back to an earlier snapshot. If the data gets corrupted or the agents did something by mistake, they should be able to go back to an earlier version. So this is really awesome. So please try this out. If you're doing any development going forward, please use Lakebase Postgres. In the next 12 months, we're going to see more software be written than ever in the history of mankind. Okay, all that software that your organizations are going to write using LLMs and coding tools need a database behind the scenes. Let's make sure it's just open source Lakebase Postgres with low cost; that's what these agents love. But today we have another talk that I'm really excited about, and I'm not going to talk too much about it here, but Reynold's going to come back and he's going to talk about something we call LTAP, okay? Lakebase and lakehouse unification. So it's like a breakthrough that actually the industry has been working on for 40 years. We think we finally pulled it off. So he's going to come and talk about that as well. Okay, so we talked about Lakeflow to get the data in, we talked about lakehouse which is a data warehouse to do analytics, and we talked about Lakebase which is transaction processing and the talks that we're going to have on that. So, hopefully we now have also the choice, the data layer at the bottom ready for the AI, okay? So that it can be consumed fast by the agents and it's seamless for them and they can experiment with the data. So this is really important, we've got to get that data layer right. But as I mentioned, we also need to figure out control, okay? So we can't just unleash the agents on all of the data like that. We need to make sure that we're controlling what they are doing. So that's why we started the Unity Catalog project a bunch of years ago. When we started that project, what the industry was doing is that they were having catalogs for structured tables. So data warehouses were looking at structured tables, rows, and columns, and they were saying, "Okay, well this column is somebody's salary information so can we hide that?" So that's what this was about: access control on structured data like that. But we called it Unity because we think you shouldn't just do control on structured data, you should do it on all the data assets that you have in an organization—any data and AI assets, okay? So you could have unstructured files, PDFs, you might have AI models, and 50 other asset types that we added over the years. We want to be able to do it with all of these, okay? So that's idea number one for Unity Catalog. Number two, you don't want to just do access control. If you lock everything down, you're done, but you want to also be able to do discovery and democratize the data in the organization. You want to also be able to track the lineage of the different datasets—where does this data come from—and visualize that graph. You want to be able to do cost controls on everything that's being done because otherwise the costs just will run out. You want to be able to look at the data quality of everything you have in your organization. So these are all the things that are part of Unity Catalog, and it's extremely important. That's why we never actually charge for Unity Catalog. Unity Catalog is just free and we actually open sourced it a few years ago and we're doubling down on open sourcing more. In the last months, we've added more people that are open sourcing pieces of it. So it's moving very, very fast, and we're excited about that. And also, we announced that you want to be able to share data. This was really important for us. The rest of the industry, they were doing this kind of locked-in sharing, where they were saying, "Buy my software, and then if you want to share it with someone else, they should buy also my software and they can share datasets between them." That's why we open sourced Delta Sharing a bunch of years ago, as part of the Delta Lake project. And now you can take any data assets and you can share them with anyone in the ecosystem. And it has actually had great growth, lots of partners in that. But what we're very excited about this year is that we're actually open sourcing a new project that's a superset of Delta Sharing, we call it OpenSharing. And what this enables you to do is share not just with Delta, but also with Iceberg because they're now the same format. But also, you can share not just data assets but also AI assets, okay? So now you can share your agents, your skills, and your models, and you can do that also on-premise. Okay, so we're very excited about that. This is very cool. Okay, so we got all these different things you can do in Unity Catalog, and we have OpenSharing. But in the last year, what has happened is that governance—which has always been AI governance, always been part of Unity Catalog—is becoming very complicated in most organizations, okay? So we're seeing that organizations now have lots of agents. You're all building agents; maybe you're using LlamaIndex, maybe you're using LangChain. The SaaS providers are now each providing you an agent that they want you to use. They have agent builders that they want you to use. They have their own MCP servers that the SaaS vendors are saying, "Please use our MCP servers. Here are the skills you can use with those." Your organizations, your developers are themselves developing MCP servers. They're themselves developing skills files. There are all these models that are being developed now by the frontier vendors. There are many different versions of them, and they all have different capabilities. You know, they exist, they don't exist. There are coding tools out there, vibe coding things, and now we're starting to see more and more co-work like agents in the organization. So, it's kind of a quagmire. It's a complete mess, okay? So, what is the problem with this? So problem number one is that actually cost is running away. I think this is the biggest problem right now. Anyone we talk to, they're really worried. The costs of this are just skyrocketing. And no one has visibility. Is it someone's agent, which model is it, what were they doing? So all this token maxing is happening and there's no way to actually set limits on everything holistically or rate limit it. So that's problem number one. Problem number two is that there's no way to control what the agents are doing. How do we make sure that the agents actually have access to the appropriate data, that we can audit them, that we have guardrails for their inputs and outputs, and how do we manage their identities? Who are they acting on behalf of? How do we track this? And then finally, and this is the most important one as I said earlier, how do we do this with choice, without lock-in? Because the state-of-the-art models now have a lifespan of one month. November last year, we were super excited about Gemini when it came out from Google, and it was like the best model, and we were all switching over to it and we said, "Okay, that's it, this is the best model ever." Then in the January/February timeframe, it was clearly Opus that was the best ever. And then OpenAI came out with GPT-4, and that was the greatest model ever. And then last week, we had Mythos, or Fable, and it was out and we were all going to switch to it, but then they canceled it and then, you know, it's back and forth. So, how do we make sure that we can actually have flexibility, so that we can pick any of them and that we will have choice here, okay? So, those are three problems that are really important. And that's why we're super excited to announce, as part of Unity Catalog, we are breaking out one place where you can manage and control all of your agents, all of your AI spend. We call it Unity AI Gateway, okay? So we're excited about this. So what is Unity AI Gateway? It's a single pane of glass and there you can actually have your one entry point for all of your agents, for all of your AI, for everything that happens. Any of the harnesses that you're using can go through this. Again, this is part of Unity Catalog, which we open sourced. We don't charge for this. And also, the AI Gateway is part of MLflow, so it's also open sourced. But one of the most important things that we do that not maybe everyone in the room knows is that we will actually provide you capacity for the frontier models. So, let's say you committed to pay $100,000 to Databricks. You might use Lakeflow for that to consume things, you might use our lakehouse. But you can also just use tokens for that $100,000 directly from us on OpenAI's models or Anthropic's models or Gemini. And you can do this on any cloud, whether you're on Azure, GCP, or AWS; you can just use this. So this is available on the platform. We actually offer that. Two, you can actually get perfect observability on all of the spend inside the organization with dashboards that give you the observability of how that spend is happening, and you can set limits. You can set budgets. You can set budgets on different groups, subgroups, all the way down to the individual level. And when someone exhausts that budget, you will get an alert, or you can actually just stop it and rate limit it so that you can make sure that nobody is spending too much. Or every day they get an email saying how much they've spent. So this is super important. And then finally, we can enforce safety, compliance, auditing, and identity management of all your AI, okay? This includes harnesses, this includes models and agents. So, we're very excited about this. So that's what Unity AI Gateway is. And now you can manage all these asset types: MCP tools, models, agents, and skills that you have in the organization. And I just want to emphasize: this is not just MCP or the tools that you have in Databricks. These are any MCPs that you have in the organization by any vendor. You register them in Unity AI Gateway and you will track all the usage and you will see how it's going. And in fact, it has a really awesome capability where you just authenticate once for the MCPs and it'll do it for all of them so you don't have to authenticate again and again and again. So, this is really awesome. Check this out. Okay, so now we have also control and we have cost. So now we've dealt with how do we get your data ready, so hopefully we have our data ready in an open format that's fast for the agents, we figured out how to do control so we can do governance, and how to do cost management with Unity AI Gateway. Okay, so that brings us to the context layer. So as I said, AIs are plenty smart. AI does not have an intelligence problem, it has a context problem. So how do we actually get that context into the AI? So this is the most important thing. So what do I mean here? So let me give you an example of how it works today. Today, you're using these agents. You might be using a coding agent. And if you look at the best agents out there, they kind of work like this: there's a big graph of all the documents and everything that everyone is doing inside of your organization. And what they're doing is, as you can see in this red dot, when you ask a question—for instance, you say, "How is my product revenue the last two weeks in Europe?"—it goes and it looks at one document. And then it reads that document. And it says, "Okay, is there anything interesting? Oh, there's a link," and then it goes and opens up an MCP server to another document. It reads that document. And it's sort of for-looping live through your corpus to get the data, okay? But there's a big problem with this approach that all the agents today are using, even the frontier ones. Problem number one is that it's very time-consuming. This live loop of going searching for your data live is just going to take a very long time, okay? And we all see it—you ask a question, even a simple question, and it takes 10 minutes, 15 minutes to just come back with an answer, because it's live going through the MCP servers. It doesn't know where to search. It's just trying live to do this. It doesn't have the context. Number two, the costs are super high. The joke is you tell it, "Please rename this file for me," and it takes 10 minutes and it charges you five bucks, okay? So that's kind of how it works because it's, again, it's going through and it's doing this search and it's using up a lot of tokens to construct all of this. But these two are not even the biggest problem. This gives you a really bad user experience and it's expensive, but the biggest problem is that the quality suffers. The reason that is is because all of the documents and all the data assets in your organization are gigantic, okay? So you're really just doing a small random walk of a small subset to get to the answer. It's remarkable the answers it gives you—it's game-changing, we're all excited about it and we were all using a bunch of it and the costs were going up—but actually if you look at the accuracy, it's quite problematic. Okay, so how do we solve this? So we are very excited to announce, and this is one of the most important things I'm going to announce, Genie Ontology, okay? So what's Genie Ontology? So let me explain. So Genie Ontology, what it does is that it actually connects with all the data that you have in an organization and in the background—so this is not when you're asking a question, this is happening in the background—it's constructing a graph of the most important knowledge that you have inside of your organization and it's making it ready so that we can give that context to any of the agents, okay? And one thing that's really important is that it's not just everything that you have in the lakehouse and everything you have in Databricks. It also connects to your Google Drive. It connects to your SharePoint. It connects to your email, Google Calendar, anything that you set up, and it can actually branch out and access all of these things that you have there. And the research team developed an algorithm called OntoRank, okay? I know it's a bad name but, you know, the research team came up with it and it's like "ontology rank". But what is OntoRank? So what OntoRank does, it's kind of like PageRank for those of you who remember. So PageRank was how Google, 25 years ago or 30 years ago, figured out how to take the whole web and create an index for what are the most important sites. Before Google, AltaVista, Yahoo, Lycos, they were just looking at how many times a word was mentioned on a site. It led to search engine optimization, or SEO, where people were adding the same word a thousand times on a webpage to get higher ranking. But PageRank could just figure out from the graph itself what's important, okay? So OntoRank does a similar thing on all of the assets that you have in an organization. But it's not PageRank. This problem is harder because it's not just webpages with links. It's all the assets that you have, but they're actually different asset types. So it needs to identify, you know, source code is different from a Google Doc. Also, here we have users. And the users inside of your organization, they're part of an org chart and it actually matters how often they access things, so we have this information as well. So we take all of these different asset types, all the people in the organization, and we construct this ontology graph that then helps us actually do amazing things with this for the agents. So this is the context that we're going to feed. Now you might say, "Hey, I already put together context, I already built semantic layers at my company, I already have a business glossary that I built over the years." Genie can take that from any of the sources, so you can actually bring your own semantics as well. So as part of Unity Catalog, we have something called Unity Catalog Semantics where you can bring from our partners—whether it's from Atlan or AtScale, or from existing BI tools that have semantics or any business glossary that you already have—and you can have humans curate that. It'll happily take that and it'll help it get smarter. So, depending on whichever camp you're in—do you think this should be completely autonomous and happen by itself, Genie Ontology does that. If you want to have lots of manual curation, you can do that with model semantics. They both work well together. Then these are fed into our agents. We'll talk about the agents that we have and we'll give you demos, but there are three categories of agents that are important. I'll talk about them: Genie ONE, Genie Agents, and Genie Code, and they all are fed this context, which makes them faster, makes them much more cost-effective, and makes them much higher quality. So you can much faster get the quality answers you want without paying too much. Okay, so that's what this is. If you're using other agents, you want to use other agents, they can use Genie as an MCP server, and they can just connect to that agent Genie and they can actually get the semantics from Genie, so you can also do that this way. You can bring any client, any agent you want, you can connect to this ontology that you have there, so that you can get that semantics and the context into any existing AI that you have. So this is what it looks like in the product. You can actually click on the graph and you can sort of explore it. In this particular graph that we're exploring here, this is just one instance of Databricks for ourselves, this is our own Databricks that we have for Databricks employees. It has over 4.5 million ontology data snippets that it has collected and constructed. So obviously, nothing you could do live. Like, it would be infeasible for any of these agents to live go one by one and look through 4.5 million ontology snippets, but it's done that and it's constructed this so that you can actually have this. Okay, so that's super awesome. So this is now fed to our agents. The most important agent that I'm excited about is Genie ONE, okay? So let me tell you about Genie ONE. If you have 100,000 employees, they then log in and they just get Genie ONE; they don't get the data warehouses, they don't get all the rest of the Databricks infrastructure. And they just ask their questions there. So it looks like any other agent that you might have. But there are some differences here. It looks at all your data pipelines, or if you have data science machine learning models, and it sees that, okay, this pipeline is down at 8:00 a.m. It will then go investigate why this pipeline is down. "Oh, the pipeline is down because there's an error. It's saying that, you know, this data... I'm not familiar with it. There seems to be a new column in this data." They'll go see, "Okay, what's the name of that column? Can I add that column?" It'll actually experiment with writing the code in a separate environment without affecting your production pipeline. How do we actually make sure that we have cost, governance, control, and security figured out? And then, how do we get this context into the agents with the Genie Ontology? So that's great. But I think actually something more profound is happening in the industry. Something is happening with software, and people are talking about it. So what is that? What does the classic Software-as-a-Service (SaaS) stack look like over the last two or three decades? It kind of looked like this: you had a system of record, which was a database, so that you had the most important golden records about your HR data, your CRM data, or whatever it was. And you had some custom workflows inside of that—somewhat rigid, but you could customize them if you brought in contractors that would augment that for you. It would take a long while, and then it was a UI. It was customizable. No one really loved those UIs, but most of us learned them, and once we learned them, we didn't want to switch to a new one. And we had one of these for each of the domains inside of the organization. You have one for Sales, you have one for Ops, and you have one for Finance. So there's one of these for each. But what's happened in the last two years is that each of these vendors is now providing you an agent of their own. They're saying, "Hey, we have an agent for this; use my agent to access my system of record data here. We're headless, you can just access it with the agent." But the problem is, when you go to these agents and you start asking questions, you never have just questions about the data in that system of record. Your questions span things, and you need to access data that's elsewhere. So it needs to go and access systems that are elsewhere, and this gets quickly very complicated. Most of these vendors don't want to play well together anyway because they're competing with each other. So it's very messy inside of the enterprise right now, and it's very confusing. How do you make that actually work and go across these different systems? So the big question is, what does the future of the software stack look like, or the SaaS software, or the apps of the future? What are they going to look like? We don't know, but we think it should look something like this. You have all your agents, and they should just access an agentic system of record. They should just be there, and you should be able to access that seamlessly. It should be very fast; you shouldn't have to go through lots of other agents, and you shouldn't have to try to go to a production database—that's too risky. You should just be able to have it at your fingertips and let these agents do that really fast, quickly, cost-efficiently, and securely. So what would the agent system of record look like in the future? What do we think that's going to look like? Well, we think it probably looks something like this: you have all your data and you can access it all in one place, in an open format. You see where this is headed. So I can just click through the next few slides. On top of that, you want to have unified governance, right? So you want to be able to have control, and you want to be able to do cost management on top of that. And of course, you don't want these agents to go and go one doc at a time and spin up tokens without knowing what they're doing. You want them to have the enterprise context so they can quickly do that, and the context should span all of these different organizations. We think that the new system of record is actually the data and AI platform that we've been talking about all along. We think that the world will look more like this—whether it's Databricks or someone else—but this is going to be the structure of the agentic system of record of the future. That way, you get choice. You get the choice of running this on any cloud, any SaaS vendor, and any of the AI models. You can do the cost controls, and you can do the governance controls. And of course, you should have the context for this. So that's what we think the future looks like. Let's augment the stack with that as well. The first one is security. We announced two months ago that we're launching a SIEM—it's called Lakewatch. So it's an agentic SIEM; we call it a security lakehouse. It looks kind of like this, and let me explain what this is. It's built on the agent system of record that I mentioned earlier, so you collect all of your security data in a lakehouse. We have to do it that way. The current approach of taking data and injecting it into these proprietary SIEMs is super expensive. People can't afford it, and the data is exploding, so you don't want to actually ingest all this expensive data into these classic legacy SIEMs. So what a lot of people are doing is filtering out the data beforehand to make sure that only the most important data is in there. Well, this is not going to work because we're getting massive attacks now from automated agents. In the last six months, the best hacker in the world is an agentic system called Expo. You can look it up on the HackerOne top list. People are finding exploits using things like Mithos, and even before Mithos—and actually after Mithos, we're going to see lots and lots of exploits. So we need all of the data. It needs to be very cheap, it can't be skyrocketing in cost, and we should just store it all by any means we have in an open, secure lakehouse. And then, on top of that, we've built agents in Lakewatch. What do those agents do? They automatically make sure that they create detections. When you wake up and there are like a hundred alerts of what's going on with your data, they go and triage it for you automatically—very similar to Zero Ops, but this is really for the SOC. SOC is the Security Operations Center, so the SOC analysts wake up and can do that. And then finally, it can do threat hunting for you. It can actually find out that there's a new zero-day attack, and it'll add the pipeline to it and automatically do all of that work. So we are very excited about Lakewatch, so check that out. Welcome to the stage, Databricks Senior Director of Product Management, Ken Wong. All right, good morning. So today, I get to introduce you to Genie 1. Genie 1 is an AI coworker that can connect to all of your data and all of your apps so that everyone in your organization can get insight and even automate action. You can even create autonomous agents just by talking to Genie. But I know what you're all thinking: in 2026, why do we need another one of these AI agents? Well, the answer is actually very simple. The ones that you have today aren't very good with enterprise data. They either can't connect and reason about data at all, or they're just not accurate enough for you to actually trust them to make real decisions, let alone automating your work. So let me show you what I mean. Yesterday, we had our Product Advisory Board meeting at Databricks. Shout out to the PAB! And I wanted to prepare myself for it by getting Genie 1 to help me with a comprehensive profile of all the customers we have and what's going on in their account. This required Genie 1 to connect to all the systems that we have, including my personal email, Slack, and my calendar to figure out who's in the PAB and what we've been talking to them about. It required Genie 1 to connect to Salesforce to pull in the use case information, but it also meant that Genie 1 needed to connect to Databricks in order to pull all the consumption data that they had, because I had a lot of nuanced questions that I wanted to include in the brief about what they're using Genie for. And this is what Genie 1 was able to come up with. As you can see, it gave me a comprehensive profile—I had to blur some of it so you don't see the details—but after querying Databricks and querying these systems, it's able to give me a comprehensive and visual profile of all of our customers. So, what I wanted to do was try this exact same prompt with five of the leading AI agents that were out there. I tried two of the AI assistants that came bundled with some of the software we had, and three of the leading coding and coworker agents. And this is what I saw. The first AI assistant is actually one that I used a lot, one that I used to write docs and things like that. It came back with a response to my prompt very, very quickly—actually a little bit too quickly—and there was something a little bit off about it. The first thing I noticed was that it said we had 24 customer accounts in the PAB, and I knew this was not true. So I asked it, "Hey, where did you get this 24 number from?" And guess what it said. It confessed that it just completely made it up. I was stunned, because this is actually something I use a lot, not just at work but I also have a personal subscription. But it just goes to show you how important enterprise grounding actually is. Now, the other AI assistant that I used actually did a lot better. It actually searched existing documents and pulled out account briefs, but they were all sort of stale. They were all from a quarter ago, information that was no longer relevant to the conversation I wanted to have. So the results of this thing were completely unusable. The good news: it was very, very quick. Now, the coding agents and the coworker agents actually made a genuine attempt and connected to all the systems and the structured sources in order to pull live data out. But if you've ever used one of these things, you've probably experienced what I experienced, which was that it went off and tried very, very hard to pull this information out. It actually hit its time limit where it asked for permission to continue, burning Lord knows how many tokens in the process. And then, after I allowed it to continue, it went away and it iterated again and again, and then it came back with some cool ASCII art, but then confessed again that actually we were far from done and it had only gotten partway through. So we're 30 minutes into this thing, and we have a partial report. It's just a huge contrast with what Genie 1 was able to do. So you might say, "Hey, this is sort of a one-off test; you cherry-picked an example." But you know, it's actually very, very consistent with the research that Databricks Research had been doing on the ability of coding agents to answer novel questions. Actually, what we saw, we curated a benchmark out of real questions that our employees were asking of Genie, and we asked coding agents to solve the same problems. Now, these weren't run-of-the-mill, simple questions that could be easily solved with some semantic layer. These were like nuanced questions, like, "Hey, of all the customers who use Genie, what percentage also use a third-party agent?" These are questions that hadn't been asked before. And we saw that coding agents were able to do it—the best of them—about half the time, and it took minutes. Remember this chart; we'll come back to it. But 50% of the time for a real data question is basically unusable, right? You literally can flip a coin. And if you know how these agents work—and I explained this a little bit already—it shouldn't be surprising, because they basically go through this process of the agentic loop, where really getting to the right answer is a matter of happening upon the right sequence of reasoning steps to chance upon the right context it needs to answer the question. It kind of goes through this walk where it just does its best. There is a very real trade-off between the accuracy that you get and the cost and runtime of these things, and this trade-off results in what we see. Now, our current best approach to solving this problem is to inject some context, right? Like to give it some information that gives it hints on the right thing. If you come from the AI world, you might call these things skills, and if you come from the BI world, you might call these things semantic layers, but they're really the same idea. It's like, "Hey, we can just tell it the things that it needs to do, and then it can do a better job." The problem with this is that while it's totally reasonable and in fact best practice to do it for the most stable business concepts you have, it's unreasonable to do it for the breadth of things that you do within your organization. Think about how each and every single marketing campaign defines leads a little bit differently, or how every single Scrum team uses all the fields in Jira a little bit differently. You just can't realistically write all this stuff down. And before you say, "Hey, can't we just use AI to solve this problem by generating these models?"—well, it actually just doesn't work. Actually, Anthropic published a great blog on the limits of this, talking about the fact that if you do this, what you end up doing is just encoding or anchoring the AI on one specific use case, and then that actually negatively affects the result overall. So, what is our solution to this problem? Well, Ali stole my thunder a little bit. It is the Genie Ontology, right? The Genie Ontology is an automatic context layer that adds a learned layer on top of any modeled context that you have, whether that's in Unity or in one of your existing semantic models or modeling tools. It basically allows Genie and any agents attached to the ontology to do a much better job of accurately answering questions with data. Let me tell you how it works. First, with the Genie Ontology, what we do is connect to the systems that you have that contain knowledge. For example, using the Databricks system, you have a ton of information in your pipelines, queries, dashboards, and things like that that tells an agent how they can reason about data. We extract all of those things, things like expressions and relationships, but also expertise, like the idea that this person is really knowledgeable about a specific topic. We extract all of this into our internal knowledge store. Then we go over this knowledge with an algorithm. This is how you know the engineers still run the show at Databricks: we call it OntoRank, and it's a PageRank-like algorithm that helps us determine which snippets are actually authoritative. With this information, when a question comes into Genie, we're able to look up this information, apply permissions (because we also extracted out the permissions of the underlying source access, so we don't have to worry about leaking permissions), and then we just inject that context into the agent loop so that Genie is able to answer questions much more accurately. And the result of this is so that if I ask a question like "How many people are registered?", without ever having to model it, Genie knows about the nuances of how we captured registrations in our internal systems because it was able to extract that information out of some source asset. And if you want to know where it came from, you can just inspect it and see that it actually learned about these calculations from our underlying dashboard. The marketing team's a little bit mad at me about this because I took this video from two weeks ago, but the numbers are a lot higher now. Coming back to this chart: we saw what happened with generic coding agents, so how does Genie with the ontology do? In our internal testing, what we've seen is a consistent 30 percentage point plus improvement in accuracy and roughly half the runtime of the leading agents. We're really confident that as we continue to tune the OntoRank algorithm and expand the breadth of the ontology, we're able to push this accuracy much, much higher. With this level of accuracy enabled by the Genie Ontology, now we can truly create a data-smart coworker, one that you can give to all of your employees to actually make decisions with data and even automate decision-making. And even with this time spent on maintenance, you still see up to 60 hours of downtime every month. So this sucks. Now you might be thinking, "But Bilal, you just told me that this is a unified stack. Surely it has APIs and endpoints and tables. Maybe a coding agent can just fix it." And unfortunately, not really. Let me walk you very quickly through why. And the big idea here is that data engineering is not software engineering. It's something a bit more. In software engineering, your code is self-describing. It literally describes itself. In data engineering, it's data and code. In software engineering, your tests either work or they don't. They're fully deterministic. In data engineering, you have code and data, and you can't just test data. It's statistical. And finally, in software engineering—and this is the big one—failures are loud. When something goes wrong, you're going to see a trace, you're going to see an exception, and then you can roll back your deployment. In data engineering, failures are silent and permanent. You're probably going to get an angry phone call from a CEO or a stakeholder saying, "Hey, how come this data's bad?" maybe a week later. So, why do coding agents trip up here? Well, that's because when it comes to detection, they're just missing data. They're missing telemetry. And you can give them Spark logs, for example, but these are multi-megabyte traces. You have to be very careful to manage the infrastructure—the context window there. But similarly, when it comes to assessing the root cause, they're missing lineage. Now you have to export your lineage out of the system. And to some degree you can do this. Coding agents are getting better and better, and context windows are getting better. And then you have to remediate. They have to write the code fix. But it's actually the final step, which I'm going to be a little pedantic about and call it Verify. You can't, with data, you can't just run your unit tests. You actually have to take your code fix and you have to run it on production data. Okay? And if this is giving you shivers, that's exactly right, it really should. So what does your agent need? Your operations agent needs to combine code and data. That's tools and skills. To some degree, it can do it. To figure out the root cause, it needs read access to production data. Who's comfortable with that? It's an agent you didn't write and you don't trust. I don't see a single hand raised here. But actually, your coding agent also needs write access to production data. That's the only way it can verify the fix. Like, who here wants to give a random agent write access to their production data? There's got to be one person. Okay, there's no one who wants to do that, right? Because it can cause chaos, and it can drop everything. And this is the big insight. So we've been looking at this problem. It's a tough problem. And the big realization we came to was that your operations agent actually needs to live in the data plane. It cannot live outside of the data plane. And if you think about it, that makes sense because the data plane has the data. It has the lineage. It's also the right governance boundary. So, I'm super, super excited to announce Genie ZeroOps: a new background Genie that puts your data and AI operations on autopilot. Great. I'm going to give you a demo, but let me quickly walk you through how it works. So for detection, Genie ZeroOps autonomously builds per-table machine learning models, and it continuously fine-tunes them. And it has native access to metrics, events, and logs. So you don't have to build this plumbing yourself. We already have tens of thousands of these machine learning models in production with Genie ZeroOps. To assess, it does graph ranking on data lineage in Unity Catalog. So I'll show you how it walks the lineage forward and backward. And then to figure out the root cause—and this is where you spend a lot of your time. The root cause is basically what went wrong. It actually has a supervisor agent and a fleet of sub-agents who do research and then come to a consensus on what is the likely cause. For remediation, Genie ZeroOps works with Genie Code. It cooperates. If Genie Code has access to your code, to your ticketing system, to your version control, it uses all that so it can also update tickets for you as it goes through the life cycle. And finally, verification is the toughest step. To verify, this is the 10 years of building an open Lakehouse, it builds shallow clones of your production data. These are super cheap, super fast branches, similar to what we do in Lakebase. And then it utilizes native network and code isolation in Databricks. Okay, so this realm of OLTP databases was invented in the 80s, and what it was is people needed a database behind every piece of software. So this software, for instance you might have an ATM, you're going to go withdraw money from it, that needs to hit the database. It needs to do that really fast. We want that latency to be milliseconds, we want it to never go down and we want it to be super reliable. It can't be that it ever messes up the transactions. Okay. And over time we've had these niche databases that also have appeared. We have key-value stores, we have now vector search with AI. So that's this island of OLTP. Okay. On the right-hand side of this map, what we have is the world of data warehousing. It appeared shortly after the 80s because people wanted to ask, you know, how many, how much data volume went through this ATM here? Or how much money has actually transacted on all the ATMs? Asking that question on those planes of data warehousing or OLTP on the left would be too complicated. And it would take down those databases and it would affect the ATMs and we never want that. So we want that to be up and running and we want it to be isolated. Okay. So on the right-hand side, we started doing data warehousing and now you could ask these more analytical questions. Okay. But very soon in the last 15 years, people wanted to ask much more advanced questions. Not just what's the total transaction volume on the ATMs, but how much is Ali going to withdraw today? Can you predict the future? So we started doing data science and more and more advanced statistical analysis. Okay. And then last few years, we've now had the real-time analytics appear. And real-time analytics, that's, you know, agents or applications or dashboards that want really low latency. They can't quite answer complicated questions and doing big joins of data and saying, "Let's do this analysis of all this data." But they can give you dashboards that give you some analytical queries. So for each of these we needed the different things, and we needed to move data from the left-hand side from the OLTP databases all the way to the right. Okay, so for that, that's where data engineering came in. And that's where we had all the complexity of moving data and shuffling it from the OLTP databases all the way to the OLAP engines on the right-hand side. Okay, so this is what the world looked like. Okay. You just heard Bilal, so this world of data engineering, that's what he was focused on. How you actually get your data from all these systems ready so that you can start doing operations on it. I think me or Shaan, somebody else would come up on stage and introduce to you some massive performance improvements we've done. But the reality is kind of the warehouses or the Lakehouses hit a wall at about a second. And what do I mean by hitting a wall? It doesn't mean you can never get a response from the Lakehouse or warehouse in less than a second for a query. It just means if you have a very stringent workload that you want to have very tight SLAs in the range of milliseconds or hundreds of milliseconds, it will be very difficult to accomplish that. Because you might actually get latency spikes. As a result, I think for many organizations that actually require workloads that have very stringent SLAs, they start setting up a separate serving stack. They would copy their data out of the data warehouse or the Lakehouse and copy a portion of it that's required for serving into a separate stack. And this causes a lot of problems. It's an extra data pipeline to maintain for every dataset. These serving stacks tend to perform super well on very simple queries, but don't actually work well for the more general workload. And it's a governance nightmare. You now have to worry about, "Hey, did I forget to actually secure my dataset in this specific platform, but I actually did in my underlying warehouse?" So, if the whole theme here is unification, what if you never had to move your data? What if you never have to copy it? And we asked ourselves that question couple years ago. And it turned out it's actually fairly fundamental. It's very, very difficult to tackle this problem. As a matter of fact, Michael Stonebraker, who's a Turing Award winner, and some of you might know him as the original creator of Postgres, wrote a whole academic paper about it called "One Size Fits All: An Idea Whose Time Has Come and Gone." And in this paper, Stonebraker actually argue it's very, very difficult to engineer a single database engine that's capable of running a wide variety of workloads. And ultimately it's because the increase in complexity makes it very, very difficult to actually engineer the system. And the fundamental reason for that is, if you look at how database engineering were done in the last four decades, or even five decades, every database engineering team started working approximately this way. Databricks included. We'd go target sort of, "Hey, here's the new workload we might want to actually study and then understand and then actually build a system for." And the way to do it is to study all the latest and the coolest academic papers about the specific type of workloads. Some of the papers actually go back 40, 50 years, some of the papers are published in the last few years. And you try to study them, understand them, and imagine how they might sort of apply in real life. And you spend a lot of time implementing it. And at some point, you try to roll it out and test how it works with real-world workloads. And inevitably, this happens. Some of the new technique you implement, or new—it could be a new algorithm, could be a new data structure—it works remarkably well for a subset of the queries, and it works terribly for a subset of the queries. So the database ends up, "Hey, we're doing better here and actually regressing." And many of the algorithm and data structures that actually work really, really well for very, very low latency, to guarantee maybe a millisecond response time, tend to actually backfire when you have larger datasets and vice versa. So this is one of the biggest problems. So we, two years or so, decided, "Hey, can we break the status quo? How can we challenge it? And do the best we can." And we actually went back to the drawing board sort of with an amazing engineering team. We actually flipped the way how database engineering are done. Instead of starting with ideas and techniques, we went back to the workloads. So luckily on Databricks we have collected over zettabytes of data that was scanned, traces from all of this data, which in the range of quadrillion actually traces—I have to learn how to pronounce that word—and all coming from trillions of queries that were executed. And based on all of this traces, we're able to actually build a machine learning model that give you very high-fidelity estimation of what might happen in practice given a specific new technique. And based on this machine learning model, we can do two things. The first is that we can select the right algorithm to dispatch very quickly at runtime—by the way, it's not a LLM, because LLMs would have too high latency—we can actually pick the right algorithm to dispatch at runtime. But even more importantly, we can decide and predict what algorithm to even implement before it hits production. Because there are millions of algorithms and data structures out there. It would be virtually impossible to actually find them all. Knowing what to implement is one of the biggest advantage one can get. Right. And we're looking at the trends for the first six months of year 2024, and now we can confidently say we're going to have more software generated in the next year than in the history of humanity. And every application still needs a database. So, what do next generation applications and agents need from a database? So the first thing is it needs to be familiar. And the reason to that is the more agents know about the systems—and they know by scanning the internet and scanning all the Stack Overflow forums and Reddit—the better they are at running and operating it. So it needs to be open source. It needs to be popular. And it needs to be extensible so you can consolidate all your main and also niche workloads on this single database platform. It also needs to be nimble. With this onslaught of applications that are coming at us and all the various dev, test, and staging environments, the system needs to be serverless and it needs to be branchable so you can easily create environments for your agents in which they can safely operate. And finally, as you run those things at scale and at volume, it needs to be cost-effective. The final thing it needs to be mission critical. Operational databases run your business. And so, that means it needs to be infinitely scalable. It needs to be fast and it needs to be extremely reliable. So, familiar, nimble, and mission critical. So we decided to start with Postgres, the most advanced open-source database in the world. It has the largest ecosystem in the world and also lots and lots of extensions. Again, you can consolidate all your additional workloads to the main and also niche workloads on this single database platform into the platform. It's also open source and understood by every agent on the planet. But Postgres is a monolith and the compute and storage in the monolith are tightly coupled. So if you want to make it nimble, you need to do something. You need to re-architect that system. The main idea we had is we can decouple storage and compute and move storage into the lake. But it's actually, you know, much harder than you might think. And so for that we need to re-architect storage from the ground up. The lake storage, you know, has a lot of very, very good properties. It's inexpensive. It's very easy to scale. But it's also slow and transactionally inconsistent. So as we are rebuilding the storage for Postgres to run it on the lake, we had to introduce two additional services, one for reads and one for writes. For writes and transactional consistency, we built safekeepers. They implement a consensus protocol called Paxos. So they give you low-latency writes. And then we introduced another service called pageservers that serve pages to Postgres compute. Those systems are called pageservers and they deliver on low-latency reads. Then we put it all together and integrate with the lake, and that gives us Lakebase: the fully managed serverless Postgres that runs on the lake. So what does Lakebase give us? Well, it certainly is nimble, right? So we can provision Postgres in under 500 milliseconds. And as you have lots and lots of developer staging environments for every potentially PR that you want to move into GitHub, we can scale that also to zero. Right? So with lots and lots of those environments and they're serverless, we need to deliver on TCO. So we automatically shut down environments that you don't use. We introduced two more patterns that are incredibly useful in this new agentic world. The first one is branching. Every Postgres database you can easily branch, and it also branches in about 500 milliseconds. So you can create an additional environment for you to create dev, test, or staging. Another pattern that kind of emerged in that agentic development is snapshot-restore. With any with a simple mouse click or an API call, you can snapshot your database. Then unleash your agent to do work, to build some software, change the schema, change the data, whatever. If the work is to your liking, you can proceed. If not, you can instantly roll back to the previous snapshot. So now it's built for agents, but is it mission critical? Can it support your business? Let's start with scalability. With Lakebase, all you need to set is airbars for your compute. You can say, "Well, don't scale my compute below this minimum or over that maximum," and you want to do it for potentially cost controls. But within those airbars, you actually can scale up and down automatically as your workload changes. Thus, your workload can grow from the initial prototype to fully production at full volume. And Postgres scales, with Lakebase Postgres scales effortlessly. We are bringing high availability and durability natively as Postgres runs on the lake. And Databricks customers have already used Databricks infrastructure for those types of workloads. And finally, of course, security is super important. We got role-based access control, but we also integrating it into Unity Catalog so that Postgres tables are like any other table in the Lakehouse. You can set permissions, you can observe the lineage, you can create quality controls. So, coming to Databricks soon: Lakebase, fully managed serverless Postgres that runs on the lake. Thank you. And back to you, Ali. We will scale the system up at your peak hours, scale things down, at maybe on weekends or nights. Storage of course is on the lake and that gives you infinite scalability. You will never run out of storage and you will never run out... you will never need to run management operations if you are approaching, you know, your disk size or whatever. So now you might be wondering, so you changed the architecture of Postgres at the storage level, so is it fast? How does it compare with the industry implementations of Postgres as a service? So this is a very similar graph to what Reynold was showing for Raydan, where we measuring latency as we scale throughput. Obviously here, it's an operational system, so it runs much smaller queries than analytical systems, but they run it at very high concurrency. So let's bring us back to the data realm. We have the most exciting, actually, announcement still left, so you don't want to miss that. Okay, so what does the data realm look like? We already talked about the right-hand side. This is where you're asking your analytical questions: data warehousing, data science. And then Reynold showed us that now we can get super low latency, world's fastest engine, with Raydan and Lakehouse RT. And then what we did is we went over to the OLTP side and Nikita, who is really our expert on OLTP databases, started focusing on how can we actually get all of that to be fused so that you can do everything Postgres on top of the lake, really fast, really cheap, with disaster recovery across the clouds. So, how do we deal with this onslaught and the storm of agents that is coming our way? We still have these waters that we have to shuffle things between OLTP and OLAP. So, let's welcome back Reynold of Raydan fame back to stage. Thank you again. Thank you again. So, you heard from Bilal simplify data engineering, from myself about how we're going to unify analytics, and Nikita from... modernize OLTP. But honestly, one of the biggest problems, there's still one big thing remains, which is we have this giant continent OLTP databases where applications start putting their data in first. And if your application becomes successful enough, you want to reason on that data and analyze on your data. And to end up actually building CDC pipelines that ship the data from OLTP databases over to your analytics continent. And how many... can I get a show of hands, how many of you love your CDC pipelines? All right. Oh, there's some, like, I would love to learn from you how you actually maintain them. All right, CDC pipelines are basically change data capture pipelines. It reads the binlog of OLTP databases, and it gets all the delta, a little delta changes and ships the delta over to analytics systems and reconstruct the state of OLTP databases in them. They're super fragile, they're very annoying to maintain, many data engineers been woken up at 3 AM actually because it caused the data corruption. As a matter of fact, some people at Databricks actually joke about CDC doesn't stand for change data capture, it really stands for continuous data corruption. And to solve this problem, the industry started introducing zero CDC, zero ETL, mirroring, and all these are just fancy terms of let's do a managed CDC by hiding the pipelines so it automatically happens. But in practice it's still a pipeline under the hood, it suffers from the exactly the same issue, CDC P-E-S, right. So, some of you might have heard of another term called HTAP, hybrid transactional analytical system, or processing. And HTAP is sort of the holy grail of database engineering. The idea is you create a single database system that's capable of handling both of OLTP workloads and your analytics workloads in one system. But HTAP has... I think most of you probably haven't actually used an HTAP system in practice and the reason is HTAP as a category had largely failed. There's very little adoption of it. And all the HTAP systems out there are kind of very proprietary, they don't have a big ecosystem, and by building a single system they start compromising on both the performance for OLTP and the performance of analytics. So, the question is how do we truly unify the two? Right? If HTAP doesn't work and CDC is very annoying and causes corruption all the time, how do we do it? And the solution to this actually goes back to the very fundamental sort of problem or dichotomy of row-oriented storage and column-oriented storage. As Ali and Nikita both alluded to earlier, OLTP system needs row-oriented storage because it needs to do a needle-in-the-haystack lookup, it needs to be actually be doing updates on those rows very quickly. Whereas an analytics system does a lot of scans of large amount of data, and it really benefit from column-oriented storage. And if you look at the architectural diagram that Nikita already showed you, where we have Safekeepers and Pageservers that write data in an actual row-oriented format to the data lakes, the solution actually lies right there. When we looked at and profiled the systems of Safekeeper and Pageserver, those are storage services. It turned out they're very under-utilized from a CPU perspective but very IO-bound because all they're doing is local disk reads, local disk writes, and network reads and network writes. So, it actually give us an opportunity to leverage those idle CPUs on those storage services to do a transcoding at that very moment to turn the data from row-oriented format to a column-oriented format. All right, and it turned out once you convert the data from row-oriented format to column-oriented format, it doesn't actually increase the CPU utilization that much, but actually dramatically shrink the data volume because column-oriented format has a better compression ratio. It's often somewhere between 10 to 1 to 100 to 1. And because of all these services are IO-bound, by having a better compression ratio, we can now actually benefit from this transcoding instead of hurting the performance. And with that, we can actually write the OLTP data into the data lake in column-oriented format. And once they're in the column-oriented format, Delta Iceberg, you can actually apply any analytic compute directly against that data. So how does this work in practice? Imagine inserting a new row into the Lakebase. And the Lakebase compute eventually sends that row to the underlying storage system. And the storage system will do the transcoding right there and actually write the rows directly into the columnar format, in Delta Iceberg. And now you can actually fire up a query against your Lakehouse, and the Lakehouse should read directly the Delta Iceberg format and return your query on the freshest copy of the data. We think this is actually revolutionary. It's probably at least as big as the Raydan talk I was talking about. And the whole concept of Lakehouse plus Lakebase, we can actually have unified the storage for both of them. And we giving this term a... this storage technology a name called LTAP. And LTAP stands for Lake Transactional Analytical Processing. We think LTAP is HTAP done right and accomplishes the goals of HTAP without actually having a single query engine for it. But we able to actually unify the storage, which is by far the most important part. And now you have one copy of data for OLTP databases and one copy of data to govern. So, LTAP can truly unify your data infrastructure. You start with the OLTP databases for applications, but you don't have to copy your data. There's no pipelines to maintain, and most importantly, there's actually no compromise in performance at all for both of your OLTP systems and your analytics systems. And all of these are built on open interoperable formats: Postgres, Delta Lake, Iceberg. So with LTAP we can finally actually combine the separate continents of OLTP analytics into a single giant continent. But we don't want to... we don't want to do this alone. So we'll be open sourcing actually a very fundamental library that allows converting Postgres data directly into columnar format in Parquet.
