For a century before this, kings as far away as England had been trying to hire Florentine historians to come write about them. He could easily have done this, and that is what was expected." "But he doesn't. Machiavelli says, 'No, I'm going to stay, and I'm going to rot, and I'm going to write *The Prince*—which is my job application begging the new regime to bring me back and let me work for them, demonstrating my loyalty. I'm going to send it to them and only them, and my immediate friends. I'm not going to share it with anybody else.' Machiavelli is a patriot, and he will not serve any cause that is not his country. It doesn't matter if the pay at a royal court somewhere would be three times what he would ever get at home. It doesn't matter that this is the regime that just arrested, tortured, and exiled him despite him not having plotted against them. He wants to work for them. Machiavelli fundamentally is possibly one of the most patriotic patriots in Earth's history, and he will faithfully sit in the countryside and rot while begging to work for the people who ordered his torture, so long as they will recall him so that he can serve his country." "This connects to the question we always ask about the target audience of *The Prince*. His other works—his *Discourses*, his histories, his comedic play—were for public circulation. Those increased his fame and made important arguments. His *History of Florence* joined other important histories of Florence in circulation, influencing the way people thought about politics. But not *The Prince*. *The Prince* is secret, proprietary—the secret sauce in how to maintain power—and he will not let any other power have that. It's like a nuclear scientist with diplomatic secrets who is faithful to his country and will not sell out and let those secrets fall into other hands. Machiavelli knows that he has the beginnings of a new world of political science. He will only share that with the government of his country because he wants it to protect his country, and he will not serve any other cause." "This is why it's so weirdly ironic to me that the reputation, the word 'Machiavellian,' means self-serving, when Machiavelli himself is one of the most selfless men I've ever read about in the history of the Earth. He gave up and sacrificed his career, diplomacy, fame, friends, and the opportunity to even be in a city and have a nice day, just to rot in the countryside and be faithful to his country. He would rather serve nothing and no one than give an hour of his time to advancing anything that is not Florence." "You're making the point that he is advocating viciousness, realism, and cynicism, but in service of protecting Florence—not in service of a generic prince of some generic principality." "Exactly. And he doesn't let copies of it circulate to anybody but the rulers of Florence and his immediate scholarly, social, intimate circle of friends—people he has known for decades who are scholar peers who have discussed his ideas with him. That is the audience of *The Prince* during his lifetime." "Does he expect that at some point it will be more widely distributed? Is he writing in a way that suggests that... I mean, it is a literary masterpiece as well. Obviously, I've only read the translation, so I don't know what it's like in the original Italian. But somebody putting in that much literary effort into something that is just supposed to be a very pragmatic manual for a particular person seems a bit weird." "Well, we have to remember this is a moment of transition from the manuscript to the print period. Therefore, it is also an important moment of transition regarding what makes a written work important, and how that written work is important to the career of the person who wrote it. It was a normal thing in Machiavelli's youth for a major, important scholar—like Pontano, one of the greatest of the previous generation—to be hired to write a handbook of princes that would exist in just one copy, or maybe three or four copies, written for a specific prince." "For example, take King Alfonso of Naples, the Spanish king who conquered Naples, Alfonso the Magnanimous. He was made famous for his vast patronage of arts and letters, and for carefully cultivated personal anecdotes. There is the story that he was in the middle of fighting a war, and a messenger rushed into his room, sweaty and covered with dust, to interrupt the king's morning time with his scholar friends discussing Plato. The king turned angrily on the messenger and said, 'Get out, this is a place for men in togas, not for men in armor,' and refused to listen to the urgent message until he'd finished his hour of scholarly contemplation of the soul. As a result of that, he lost that battle, but he actually won the war. His reputation, cultivated by anecdotes like that, made him beloved. He would pay a salary five times what the Republic of Florence would pay to hire somebody like Machiavelli." "And what does he hire them to do? He has a lot of children, princes and princesses, and he commissions a scholar to write a unique, bespoke handbook of how to rule and use power for each of his children. These exist in manuscript in only one or three copies, and the addressee is the Duchess of Ferrara, who is a daughter of King Alfonso. That book is never intended to circulate; it's intended to be private guidance for her, and for her perhaps to pass on to her sons and daughters. Meanwhile, the author's fame is magnified because people are told, 'The special, bespoke handbook of princes cultivated secretly for this important princess was written by so-and-so.'" "That's so cool." "And letters circulate and let you know that it's happening. In the same way that a scientist might become famous because we know he's developing cool proprietary technology that only his government has, we have to think of these books as proprietary technology. In that sense, it's not an unusual thing to write a book with an audience of one, or an audience of one and her immediate circle. This is also one of the moments where a handbook for a prince also means a handbook for women. The title of that book for the princess who becomes Duchess of Ferrara addresses her as a prince and is a handbook of princes, because 'prince' was a gender-neutral word at this point. It's lexically masculine in terms of endings, in the same way a table is feminine in Italian, but 'prince' is used for both men and women. Even Queen Elizabeth is referred to as 'Prince Elizabeth' at this period of her life." "That is so fascinating. We have trouble wrapping our heads around the idea of writing a book for an audience of one. It's just not what a book is to us." "Well, that's the funny thing. I think we are entering a new era where that might once again be possible. It already is somewhat true, where I think at least half of the words I read on a given day are generated specifically for me and nobody else, because of AI. Obviously, AI is not capable of writing something that is a literary masterpiece that everybody would want to read if they had access to it just yet, but eventually it will be. It's interesting to consider that as technology progresses, it might bring us back to this era of bespoke scholars dedicated to a particular prince." "It's sort of important to remember that that never really went away. There are two halves to that. One, for ages it has been true that half of the words we read every day are bespoke only for us, because they're emails or letters. They are correspondence back and forth which has an audience of one—the addressee. That is the majority of what all of us read and write in our lives. It has also always been the case that in the halls of power, there are book-long documents with an audience of one or five. There are historians, scholars, and scientists whose job it is to provide a hundred-page report on, say, the history of Syria to be given to a committee of Congress, where nine senators or Congresspeople need the background on what's happening to understand current events. I have historian friends who work for the Department of Defense Intelligence who produce these book-length research projects with an audience of five, eight, or a couple dozen, because it is the bespoke proprietary knowledge needed by the government in that moment. Sometimes it's technological knowledge, but just as often it's historical knowledge, like: 'Here are the important rivers where military events are likely to happen,' written by a historian who knows the background of this stuff." "Yeah. That actually brings up the question of how many such tracts through history, which are of the quality of *The Prince* and as original at their time, as wonderfully crafted and so on, have been lost to history. Maybe one way to answer the question, or to think about it, is to talk about how *The Prince* itself went into mass publishing. At some point in 1532, the Medici Pope allowed its publishing, and then 27 years later, it was censored by that same papacy. How did this book, which Machiavelli himself did not want in wide circulation, end up in wide circulation, then out of circulation, and then back in wide circulation again?" "There are two halves to that answer; I'll give the zoomed-out and then the zoomed-in answer to that question. It is often the case that a work containing radically unusual ideas will drift along without being particularly noticed by society, and not widely read, until it hits a moment when the new questions being asked in that century or decade are answered by something in that text. Then, suddenly, everyone starts reading it." "A different example of this, probably well-known to the audience because everyone here is a cool, smart, learned person, is Lucretius's *On the Nature of Things*. This is our best capsule of ancient atomism and the atoms-and-vacuum theory of matter, written around the BC/AD turn. It drifted along without being very important for ages until the 1600s, when we started getting the first ideas of the germ theory of disease. People became very interested in new science, and suddenly it got thirty print editions, went all over the place, and influenced science. It was even more influential in the 19th century when we became interested in atoms and cells. A book can exist for literally two thousand years, or close to it, and then—boop—suddenly answer the questions of that decade." "In that sense, *The Prince* drifted along and was not very important for a while. Why was it first published? It was first published when Machiavelli's surviving relatives wanted fame for the family and fame for their beloved, now-dead kinsman. They said, 'Here is a work of his that hasn't been published yet,' and they asked for permission because this could spread his fame. It was also dedicated to members of the Medici family, so the Medici were like, 'Yeah, we get fame for publishing this thing too.' They didn't think as seriously about the power of its contents as its author did. So, it was one more book that could spread the fame of both the Machiavelli family and the Medici family. It got around, and people were like, 'Oh, that's actually full of fairly scandalous ideas. Hmm.'" "Which is how it ended up on the index as book censorship kicked up as a result of the printing press. Every time there is a new information technology, there is a subsequent wave of censorship to try to control it, and a bajillion books get banned all at once. Machiavelli's is not a particularly prominent example among these. The index of banned books that contained his work carefully differentiated between the dangerous books by arch-heretics and slightly dangerous books by less notable people. The arch-heretics were in all caps. I remember when I was first reading through one of these indexes, I was so excited to flip through and find Machiavelli, and there he was, not in all caps. I was so angry! I was like, 'What's wrong with that? He should be in all caps!' But all the all-caps people were Luther, Calvin, Zwingli, and a bajillion Protestant theologians you've never heard of. All-caps arch-heretic status was reserved for Protestantism in this period. Machiavelli didn't catch on until later." "So, he was censored in a wave of censoring everything when there was a big censorious wave, and then it diminished and went up and down. For the second, zoomed-in half of that: if we say Lucretius becomes exciting when people want to know about the germ theory of disease, when does Machiavelli become exciting? Machiavelli became exciting first in the aftermath of the publication of Hobbes' *Leviathan*. Hobbes' *Leviathan* hit European thought like a truck full of bricks. It had incredibly persuasive, gorgeous reasoning that landed you on a terrifying vision of what humanity is, and a terrifying vision of what God is, which people found very scary but also incredibly persuasive. It is no exaggeration to say that in the aftermath of publishing *Leviathan*, there was a forty-year period where the sole goal of Western European philosophy was coming up with a good way to refute Hobbes." "In that moment, they said, 'Okay, Hobbes is using a lot of logic about politics and about history that sounds like Machiavelli.' He was doing these utilitarian, consequentialist analyses of, 'If we do this, there is that result.' He was analyzing the origins of government as if there were no divinity setting it up—he had 'man in a state of nature invents government' instead of God from on high telling Adam, 'Here is how you should organize the world.' So they said, 'Okay, Hobbes is the monster. Hobbes is Leviathan the Great,' or 'the Beast of Malmesbury,' as newspapers called him during his lifetime. 'How do we refute the monster? Let's look at the daddy monster that spawned the baby monster. If we can read Machiavelli and find holes in Machiavelli, maybe we can use those to refute Hobbes.' Suddenly, Machiavelli was useful, not to people who sympathized with him, but to people who saw him as an enemy and wanted to use him to defeat what to them was the greater enemy. He surged in popularity at that point." "A different surge happened in the 19th century. It wasn't until the 19th century that Machiavelli's *The Prince* became a major global staple that you would put in a Great Books series. In the 19th century, in the aftermath of the Enlightenment and the Enlightenment's revolutions—the American republic, the French republic, the transformations and democratic movements happening in lots of other governments—people wanted new ways to think about politics. They wanted to think about politics with a separation of church and state. If you want to think about the separation of church and state, which is a new Enlightenment-era value, what do you need? You need an apparatus for thinking about politics and ethics that doesn't depend on God being a part of it. The vast majority of political treatises available to humanity at that point had some sort of entanglement of religion with politics at their root. But Machiavelli didn't. Machiavelli was this early, foundational, 'What if we think about government in a box without plugging into religion? What if we just think about government operating by itself and its earthly consequences?' It was incredibly useful in the 19th century for developing a statecraft for the separation of church and state." "It was also useful for Italian nationalism to celebrate and claim, 'Hey, we invented the separation of church and state. Here's Machiavelli, the first modern man. He's our bid at Italian culture inventing modernity.' At the same time, England was saying Francis Bacon was the first modern man because he invented the scientific method. France was saying René Descartes was the first modern man because he invented logical reasoning and modern principles of logical deduction. There was a nationalist competition in the 19th century, with different countries wanting to claim their cool thinker was the first modern man. Machiavelli became one of Italy's big bids for 'first modern man' because he came up with the separation of church and state—a phrase that Machiavelli would not have recognized if you said it to him, but would have thought about for a long time, decided was cool, and then written letters about." "Can I try out a counter-thesis just so you can dispel my confusion?" "Definitely." "One reason why Machiavelli might have gained especial significance in the 19th century is that now that you have these republics in the world, there's a question of how you make sure they are maintained. That is really the question that at least the first third of the *Discourses* is obsessed with. But one of the ways in which he says you do this is by having a religion that people take very seriously into consideration. I think he says at some point early in the *Discourses* that more significant than Romulus in the founding of Rome was Numa, or whoever it was that gave the Roman gods and the Roman religion some legitimacy. It is this legitimacy, and the fear of offending virtues because you believe in some God who will punish you, that motivates people to act in a way that defends a republic." "He gives the example of Scipio after the battle in which Hannibal absolutely destroyed the Roman armies, and the people were about to flee Rome because they thought Hannibal was coming. Scipio himself went down with his sword and said, 'Swear to our gods that you will stay and defend your homeland.' Just having them give the oath in that moment was enough to convince them: 'Well, Hannibal can't be worse than the gods, so I have to stay here and defend our republic.' It seems like he thinks religion is super important to the legitimacy of the state." "Agreed. He's thinking about it in a way parallel to how late 18th-century and 19th-century figures were also thinking about it, because we have to separate the institution of religion from the psychological effect of religion on the populace. A useful example here is Thomas Paine, right? We all know Thomas Paine's *Common Sense*. Paine did a lot of thinking about the foundation of US institutions. Thomas Paine was a deist and a radical, and he wrote many treatises about how the most destructive force in the world is institutional religion. Whether it's Catholicism or the Church of England, he felt these institutions were giant, centuries-old or millennial-old conspiracies to control your mind, steal your money, and were incredibly pernicious to everything." "However, he said religion is vital to citizenship because it is what makes people be good, and it is what makes people fear and want to obey the laws. Therefore, said Thomas Paine, every country must have religion, and religious education must be mandatory in schools, but it doesn't matter *which* religion. Thomas Paine advocated mandatory religion in total indifference to what religion it is, based on the idea that fearing God and posthumous punishment is necessary to make a citizen willing to obey the law in a practical sense. Notice how that is Paine thinking in a utilitarian way about the psychological effects of religion, which is very different from the older view where the state and a state religion are entangled, and the state promotes this religion because it believes it to be true, creating a Christian nationalist, Catholic nationalist, or Roman pagan nationalist religion that advances itself against others." "Machiavelli is absolutely thinking about the psychological effects of a religion on the people. He has that wonderful analysis in the *Discourses* of the utility of Roman religion. He talks in one really striking and memorable passage about how Roman religion says that your ghost depends on being remembered. This is out of the Homeric tradition: your ghost only retains its identity to the degree you are still remembered on Earth. If your name is forgotten on Earth, your ghost forgets its name. This is not like Christianity, where your ghost is okay forever. It is a system where your ghost depends on being honored by your descendants on Earth. When you are forgotten, your soul becomes an empty, mindless, wandering shade. Therefore, you have an incredibly strong incentive to be remembered by doing great deeds, especially sacrificing yourself for your country, because then your name will be honored for as long as your country lasts. Machiavelli says this is one of the big motivators that made people sacrifice themselves for the state in ancient Rome, because they were guaranteeing themselves a good afterlife." "While Christianity, he points out, says all that matters for a good afterlife is being pious and then, ideally, dying a martyr. You have no incentive to sacrifice yourself for your state; the safety of your afterlife is guaranteed by your interiority. This encourages a citizen to sit in a box and be a monk, rather than signing up for the military and defending his country." So, says Machiavelli, Roman religion was much better for patriotism and political stability than Christianity. But, he says at the end of the chapter, Christianity has the advantage of being true. Period. End of chapter. And you say, "Hello, Machiavelli. We know that you had to have the mandatory subscript there." But think about Thomas Paine and Machiavelli in parallel there. They're thinking about the utility of religion for forming a citizen, but they're not thinking about, you know, "this religion is true, we are doing God's work, we need to craft our state to match the values of our religion," which is what a theocrat would argue. So what you have is the separation of church and state with the expectation that religiosity will be there, it will affect the people, and it will affect the citizenry and their behavior. You need to think about it, you need to decide whether to cultivate it, but you need to think about it in the same neutral way you think about cultivating literacy skills or math skills in your citizenry. "What skills do we want our citizenry to have for them to be well-informed citizens? What do we need? We need religion and we need good newspapers so that people are up on the news and can vote prudently." You are evaluating those things side by side from a utilitarian standpoint instead of "this religion is true, it is the obligation of our government to advance it and our government expects to receive divine blessings if we advance the correct religion and divine curses if we don't." It is a radically different way of thinking about religion while still recognizing it as a powerful factor affecting the psychology of the populace. That makes sense. So last episode we were talking about the psychological impact on scholarship of having books be so expensive and having then to meditate on the same copies that are available in one library. And maybe Machiavelli is the strongest example of this, where through his life we're seeing the impact of the printing press diffusing and making printing cheaper, but early on in his life, it's still not been that long since Gutenberg came up with the first printing press. And as a result, correct this story for me, but didn't his dad have to do like months of drudge work indexing Livy in order to get a copy of Livy? Yeah. And Machiavelli himself, in order to get books when they were scarce and few in the infancy of printing, had to copy them by hand. For example, one of my favorite manuscripts ever that I've worked with is a copy of Lucretius in the Vatican library, where Machiavelli copied out the entire poem in his own hand. What's really neat is he copied the text from a printed copy, but as he copied it, he integrated into it corrections and improvements of errors taken from a manuscript copy. What he produced was actually better than either the printed version or the manuscript version. And then he made his marginal comments as he went. But notice this is somebody who, even though print copies of this book exist, is so much in the manuscript world that he's happy to spend months, probably, copying out and making his own custom, improved version of this text that he can then work from. This is true even though inevitably new print copies will come out in a few years that may have the very corrections that he's working with. But he isn't going to wait for that, and he's not sure. So he makes his own version. He comes from this moment when print and manuscript are parallel technologies being used at the same time. The very people who are buying the first printed books are also producing manuscripts imitating those printed books and being influenced by those printed books. I want to think about how having this copy of Livy, which presumably is one of the very few books that young Machiavelli had access to, influenced his intellectual development. We have this mode of scholarship at the time where, for instance, why does he spend like two decades writing *Discourses on Livy*? Well, presumably unlike us, where we can listen to an audiobook a week or read our Kindle at night, he's just reading this book again and again and again and again. He is trying to connect it to the events he's seeing in his own life on his, like, tenth reread. That, I feel, is very interesting psychologically in understanding how scholarship and intellectual thought must have been different at that time as compared to now. Yeah. I mean, Machiavelli can easily access other books by visiting friends, or by asking to go to the library of his Medici patrons when he's working for the Medici, or of his Soderini patrons when he's working for the Soderini. But that's different from having it at home, being able to have it at your bedside and look at it at all hours, and having this intimacy with it. It is your father's copy, and then it is your copy. Another part of that, though—and this is kind of weird for modern people to understand—is that in the Renaissance, there is so much enthusiasm for antiquity. Antiquity is the cutting-edge thing; antiquity is where it's at. Antiquity is how we're going to end the chaos of the previous world and have this new world where we're basing everything on ancient Rome. There's going to be peace, there's going to be a golden age, and it's all coming from imitating antiquity. Therefore, if your book is a comment on an ancient, it is going to be way more popular, sell way better, and people will care more and think more of you than if your ideas are original. Nobody wants original ideas. Original ideas are out of vogue. Original ideas are dead. All ideas need to be from the ancients. And so a Renaissance scholar will bend over backwards to pretend that his beautiful original ideas are actually from Livy or Plato, or to couch them as a commentary on these things, because that's going to have a way bigger audience, be more popular, and be taken more seriously than if it's original. Right? So there are points where Giordano Bruno, in his commentaries on Aristotle, claims that Aristotle says things when Aristotle absolutely says the opposite of what he claims. But if he claims it's Aristotle, people will take it more seriously. The most extreme version of this is the brilliant and fascinating figure of Annius of Viterbo, whom Tony Grafton has written a great book about. Annius of Viterbo had this radical vision of how he wanted to rethink history and faked ancient texts. He made them up, faked archaeological digs, secretly buried artifacts, and then dug them up to great drama, forging antiquities to create a book that advanced his visionary original idea of ancient history. He did this because if he pretended he got it from antiquity, people would take it more seriously than if it was an original book. So Machiavelli's *Discourses on Livy* is his big bid to have a popular, important, prestigious thing. A discourse on Livy is a bigger deal, more important, more interesting to everybody, and more likely to sell and get attention than a Florentine history or a treatise of original thought on princes—who wants that? That's a very niche kind of thing. *Discourses on Livy*? Oh, exciting, we have to have this. And this goes on for the next century. For example, huge amounts of radical political thought, including, believe it or not, commentaries on Machiavelli, happen in the footnotes in editions of Seneca and Livy. In these books, the text of Seneca will be a small square in the middle of the page, and then there are these masses of footnotes and commentary. A huge original moment of political thought for the entirety of the 1600s is going on in wars in footnotes in editions of Seneca. But it's not presented as original thought. It's all about Seneca because that was what was in vogue then, and the vogue of scholarly stuff shifts fast and is very interesting. And this is one of the weird reasons that Renaissance philosophy and Renaissance innovative thought, with the exception of a couple of oddball works like *The Prince*, gets sort of pushed out of the history of philosophy, especially in the 19th century. Because when you get to the 19th century, the vogue dictates that everything has to be original. The philosopher's ideas should be born like Athena, fully formed from the head of Zeus. The ideal philosopher lives in a cabin by the raging sea, contemplating in the wilderness, and what they want are original treatises. And if you look at a 19th-century historian of philosophy, they'll say, you know, in the Renaissance there was almost no original thought. There was Machiavelli's *The Prince* and there was maybe a little bit of Giovanni Pico della Mirandola's *Oration on the Dignity of Man*—parenthesis: we have since proved it's not an oration and it's not about the dignity of man—and these things are like the few lights in the darkness. Everything else in Renaissance philosophy is dismissed. Here's a quote from a philosophy department person who actually said this to me: "The Renaissance is 200 years of people being wrong about Plato." And a lot of people look at it, read it, and pick up Ficino, and he's like, "Plato said these things," and you're like, "No, Plato totally did not say those things at all. That's absolute gibberish. What are you saying, Ficino?" And if you think Ficino is what he says he is, a commentary on Plato, then indeed the Renaissance is 200 years of people being wrong about Plato, being wrong about Livy, and being wrong about Aristotle. But you have to realize that their style guide requires original thought to be presented in the form of a commentary on an ancient. What it actually is is 200 years of original thought using the ancients as the trellis up which the rose climbs in order to bloom. When you restore that and realize it, you recognize that to get at the real Renaissance, you need to not read the goofy outlier works like Machiavelli's *The Prince* which present themselves as original—which is a weird thing to do—but read the *Discourses on Livy*. That's where the original stuff is hidden by pretending, claiming, and sometimes sincerely convincing themselves that this is the secret coded true meaning of the ancient thing. Like Ficino, the translator of Plato, definitely genuinely believes that all of the incredibly original cosmology and magic that he's figured out is secretly coded in Plato. And he's wrong, it's not. It's so adorable that he really, really believes it is. But what it is is an incredibly original vision of the universe that he got from reading Plato, thinking hard about it, and combining it with other things. So he presents it as a commentary on Plato, or a commentary on Dionysius the Areopagite. So that's core to why this is a *Discourses on Livy*, because this is what a scholar is supposed to be doing, and all the other things Machiavelli does are sort of second-tier, weird things for a scholar to be doing on the side. Okay, so adult Machiavelli is now seeing some of his work start to get mass-produced. What is his reaction to this? At first excitement, but also horror. Because Machiavelli is facing this fascinating moment in the history of being an author when printing has come into being but there isn't copyright yet. In the manuscript period, there's no such thing as copyright. If you find out that someone has made a copy of your book, you say, "Oh, thank God, there's another copy of my book!" That reduces the chances of it being completely destroyed in a fire. You know, making one copy of a book is six months of incredibly difficult labor. You're just grateful every time a text is reproduced. But when it comes to printing, you have this experience, which Machiavelli is one of the first men ever to have, of finding out that a local printer is printing a work of his without ever having asked him, without ever having talked to him. And he looks at it, and it's full of typos and minor errors. He's panicking in these letters and saying, "Oh no, everyone's going to think I'm a bad scholar. There are all these little mistakes in the text, and they aren't me. They're the compositor having made typos when setting it up, and no one will know that. They'll blame me, and it'll destroy my reputation. What do I do?" There's nothing he can do because there's no legal process and no legal recourse. Printing has just come into being. And it's neat seeing him and friends writing to each other about, like, "What can I do about the fact that this printer has printed my book without asking me? There is no law, there is no apparatus, there is no anything." And his friends are like, "Well, write letters to everybody who matters and tell them that the typos aren't you. That's all I can suggest." Because they don't have the idea of authorial copyright yet. It's going to come in in the next couple of decades. And the weird thing is how this gets entangled with censorship. Counterintuitively, copyright and censorship are born together in Machiavelli's world from the Inquisition. Because when the Inquisition begins book censorship after 1515, which is during Machiavelli's lifetime, the policy that the Catholic Church promulgates is, "Before you may print any text, you must take it to an authority licensed by the church to do this," meaning an inquisitor or a bishop, and they must read it and give permission for it to be printed. This is so that they can make sure there isn't heresy in it. So all books are effectively born pre-banned until you get permission for them to be printed. And in return for this, you get a monopoly license, and only the printer that took the book through the process can print it. You may now use the actual Inquisition record of having gone through censorship as the document to prove that you and only you have the right to print the book, and therefore you can sue people for plagiarizing it or printing an unauthorized edition. And so the very first version of copyright is the Inquisition. And places outside the Catholic world then, like England, look at this, and there's actually popular demand in England for censorship. They say, "Hey, we need what the Inquisition does, because this Inquisition is so cool. They let printers have a monopoly on printing a book and they let authors deny print permission. We need something like that." And the very first version of what is not yet copyright passed in England, which is of course the ancestor of what applies in all Commonwealth nations and in the US, was originally an imitation of the Inquisition. And it was, "Okay, you need a license before you can print your thing, and then in return you get a monopoly." Later, when there was a push for freedom of the press—and by later I mean this is happening over the course of the first half of the 1600s, so about a century after Machiavelli's death, so it takes a century for all this to get ironed out—the first version of copyright law is basically saying, "Okay, well, we're going to keep the copyright half of censorship while getting rid of the censorship half of censorship or changing the censorship half of censorship." But it's all born out of the Inquisition having met this weird demand that you feel in Machiavelli where he's like, "They printed my book, they did a bad job, there's nothing I can do. Help, authorities, give me some way to do something about this." And so that's where you can feel Machiavelli as part of one of the first generations that need copyright that will then be born in the aftermath. Fascinating. And what was the Inquisition's incentive to enforce the author's prerogative on the text? Partly, the Inquisition does it because that encourages authors to come to them. It makes people much more willing to collaborate with their process. But also think of an individual inquisitor as an individual person who lives in a place, needs to have relationships in that place, and needs to have an income, and who is not usually getting enough to live on from the Inquisition itself. If you're working for the Inquisition, you're an officer of the Inquisition, you're probably a Dominican monk, you get some support from the monastery, but you have reason to want money, and you have family, and they want money. You're as pragmatic and self-serving as any other average human. So the fact that people want to have this positive relationship with you means they might gift you some bottles of wine in return for you being extra generous in your reading of their text. They also have to negotiate with local authorities. One thing the Inquisition wants us to think of it is as very centralized and very monopolar, right? "The Inquisition, the Vatican, it controls everything." Which is completely untrue and is propagandistic. The Inquisition is overseen by a whole bunch of isolated guys who are in isolated towns, and it takes weeks or months to even communicate with the Vatican. They're making their own decisions. And for the most part, they don't have their own large amount of funding, they don't have their own officers to jail people, they don't have their own jails, and they don't have their own authority to arrest directly. They get all of those from the local government. They collaborate with the local government. Which means if the local government likes them and is pleased by them and is like, "Ooh, the Inquisition. I can use this to scapegoat my enemies," then the local government will drown the Inquisition in funding and give them all the guards and all the incentives they could want. So when we hear about the infamous Spanish Inquisition, which everyone was expecting me to mention, the Spanish Inquisition is infamous because Ferdinand and Isabella of Spain really want to scapegoat the Jewish and Muslim populations that they're anxious about. So they throw money at their Inquisition and really cultivate and make it big. And that's coming from them; it's not coming from Rome. Meanwhile, if you're somewhere like Florence under the early Medici dukes, the ruler is a Medici. He's in deep with the weird, Ficinian Platonic soul-projection magic people. He's an intellectual radical descended from intellectual radicals. His court is full of intellectual radicals. And here you are, the inquisitor, and you're like, "Your Grace, can I arrest this guy?" And he's like, "No, that guy works for me. You can't touch him." And you can only arrest as many people as the Duke will give you funding for, or the local republic will give you funding for. So you need to please the local government if you're the inquisitor. And we have letters of inquisitors complaining, you know, "this is a really liberal duke, he's protecting all these heretics around him, and there's nothing I can do about it because I depend on the local authority for my ability to do stuff." So, this is a really bizarre comparison, but think of the Inquisition operating kind of like Doctors Without Borders. It's not the government; it's an international organization that's set up to try to achieve a goal that it believes is beneficial in different places. But it's only as strong or as weak as the government's willingness to collaborate with it. And if the government collaborates with it, it can be enormously powerful in an area and do a lot. If the government is hostile to it and starves it of resources and doesn't let its people in and insists on pushing it out, then you can get bubbles where the Inquisition is nearly impotent, and every time they want to arrest someone they have to go to the Duke's agents, and if the Duke's agents keep saying "No," they can't do anything. What this really creates is bubbles of privileged access where, if you're in with the government, you can be as radical as you like and the Inquisition can't touch you. This is also a lot of how homosexuality operates at the time. If you are in the protection of a powerful person, they can prevent the Inquisition or other officers of the church from getting at you. They just won't do it, and they're more powerful than those agents are, so they can't touch you. There's a really neat letter where Machiavelli and some of his gay friends correspond. Machiavelli was, I would say, very definitely solidly bisexual in that this is a man who recreationally had boyfriends and girlfriends throughout his life that he writes to. We have homoerotic poetry, we have heterosexual poetry, and he's definitely very excited by both sexes. And he has a lot of gay friends. And he and his gay friends are writing back and forth about how, at this particular moment in Rome, one of the agents in charge of Rome's sort of enforcement is really cracking down on homosexuality. And therefore, all of their gay scholar and artist friends are rushing to get jobs working for cardinals. Because if you work for a cardinal, nobody can touch you. Almost all of their friends succeeded in getting jobs working for cardinals except for one. So he resorted to hiring two female prostitutes to hang out with him all the time and make him seem straight by having him hang out with sexy courtesans to defend himself against charges of homosexuality. Heresy and homosexuality operate very similarly in this period. They're both sort of forbidden by the same things and policed by the same structures. And so if you work for the cardinal or you work for the Duke, you can be doing very radical magic, radical philosophy, radical politics, and radical sexuality, and nobody in authority can touch you because authority is trumped by a higher authority that is protecting you. This is part of the patronage system. And how does that come back to the copyright stuff? So the way that comes back to copyright stuff is the Inquisition needs to please local authorities in order to get to operate at all. And so the Inquisition will therefore try to figure out things that will please local authorities. And if a book is being presented for publication that has a recommendation letter at the beginning written by an important political figure, the Inquisition will, you know, push it through. And when printing presses and authors say, "Hey, can we have this be a monopoly license?" figures like Machiavelli realize we could ask for, "Hey, you're giving us permission, can you deny everyone else permission?" The Inquisition immediately realizes this is a great way to get publishers on our side, to get authors on our side, and to get their bosses on our side because we are protecting the book that is important to the Duke because it's dedicated to the Duke or it's dedicated to his grandfather. You know, the Medici give permission to print *The Prince* partly because it's dedicated to a member of the family and it celebrates their fame. They have an incentive to be able to control its quality and make sure that it's published in good quality and that it always has that dedicatory letter at the front. They have an incentive to control what we now think of as copyright. The Inquisition, wanting to please them, has an incentive to give them that control. To close off on, do you have some sense of how to think about why Machiavelli's remembered so differently from not only what he wrote, but why he was writing? So sometimes in the history of thought there are authors who become separated from their work, and you have a parallel where there is the actual content of what the person did and said, and separately there is the idea of this person. In the case of Machiavelli, right, we have Machiavelli the patriot, Machiavelli who did all this work. And separately we have Machiavellian, right, "the murderous Machiavel," as Shakespeare calls him, and "Old Nick," which is a nickname for the devil but becomes popularized because Niccolo Machiavelli, Old Nick, literally is a synonym for the devil. And he splits so that the idea of Machiavelli—the Machiavellian, villainous figure that Shakespeare's Richard III invokes as someone he's modeled himself on—is useful to people as a character, as an idea. The idea of the scheming politician who is probably atheistic, definitely self-serving, and who wants nothing but to advance himself in power. Which of course isn't the real Machiavelli if you read the work. The real Machiavelli is not about advancing yourself. It's not a manual for getting ahead, right? It shouldn't be shelved next to *How to Win Friends and Influence People*, because it's a manual not of how to gain power but of how to keep power. If you have a government and want it to be stable and protect the people's lives, do this. But the idea of the murderous Machiavel is very exciting. And this happens at other times to other intellectual figures. It happens to Thomas Hobbes in the phase that Thomas Hobbes is the Beast of Malmesbury, and the idea of Thomas Hobbes sort of separates. It happens fascinatingly to Spinoza, an important radical Jewish thinker of the later 17th century. And Spinoza is a neat one because when you actually read Spinoza, he's really warm and sweet and, like Machiavelli, passionate and cares about people, and in his case is incredibly pious, a theist, a monist—he believes the entire universe is the body of God. You are part of God, the table is part of God, the camera is part of God, everything is God—isn't that great? But a fact about Spinoza—and I know this feels tangential but it's not—a fact about Spinoza was that he was the first person in ages and ages to be targeted with the Jewish equivalent of excommunication, these sort of ceremonial declarations of "Your radicalism is too radical, we are expelling you from the community of Jews." It was such a rare ceremony that the Jews of his region actually had to send somebody traveling all around Europe to find a Jew who knew the ceremony because it was so incredibly rarely done. And the fact of that spread around and people had the idea of "Ooh, Spinoza must be even more weird and heretical than any heretic if even the Jews would expel him." And the idea of Spinoza the arch-heretic becomes a character, and everyone talks about Spinoza the arch-heretic, and then you read him and it's nothing like it. But sometimes the character is useful, right? The idea, the thought-experiment figure of Machiavelli the villain, is useful for our philosophy and we like to talk about "What is a Machiavellian self-serving politician? What would they do?" And this has a separate life from Machiavelli's real ideas. Right, to the degree that all the way through the 16th century there are these amazing discussions of Machiavellianism in Spain where they're talking about the Jews as Machiavellian and Machiavelli as the prince of the Jews. And you're like, "Machiavelli was in no way a Jew at all." But what they mean by Machiavellian and by Jewish is somehow the political thought that is undermining our good Catholic Spain. Right? And so Jewish and Machiavellian can become synonyms, mad as that is for us. Because for them both of these are labels for the sinister underground of thought, and now we're talking about the sinister underground of thought. The idea of Machiavelli as the villain is itself enchanting and interesting. And as we look at when Machiavelli is invoked in the modern day, when *The Prince* sits on the shelf and feels like something exciting to buy and to read and to think of as a manual of getting ahead, when having it on your shelf makes it feel like you're participating in the idea of strategic advancement and rationalism—that's much more Machiavelli the character, you know, Old Nick, than it is the Niccolo Machiavelli who faithfully sat in exile, willing to give up wealth, fame, society, the ability to visit his wife, anything, in order to serve his country. And to me I think even more fascinating than looking at either Old Nick, the fictitious Machiavellian villain, or Machiavelli the patriot, is to look at how did we double-image this? And what is the fascinating tendency of our society to take something real, powerful, exciting, intimate and then say, "But we can also make the character"? And the character is itself interesting. So if you take away a main message from this with Machiavelli, it's: Machiavelli the character of thought-experiment is an important backbone of our society, we use him as we think about politics. Machiavelli the actual innovator is a different backbone of our society in how we think about politics. And if Machiavelli can be two such different things, Old Nick and Machiavelli the patriot, so many other things we encounter in life have actually been teased apart by our social utility and made into multiple things which are useful to us in different contexts. So that, and if you have *The Prince* on your shelf, read it and remember it was written by somebody who was willing to give up anything to serve his country, and you'll see a very different Machiavelli come through. I think that's an excellent place to close. Ada, thanks so much for hopping on. This was a pleasure as always. I hope it won't be the last time. I hope so too.

Clay’s Unusual Path to Building a Multi-Billion Dollar Company

Invest Like The Best(77.1K)·60:11·2.9K views·2026-06-16
Patrick O'ShaughnessyHost, Invest Like The Best Kareem AminCo-founder and CEO, Clay
TL;DRKareem Amin, co-founder and CEO of Clay, outlines his unconventional philosophy of building a highly successful software company by creating from a place of "wholeness" rather than "lack," optimizing for self-respect, and embracing true risk.
By viewing go-to-market tools...

TL;DR

Kareem Amin, co-founder and CEO of Clay, outlines his unconventional philosophy of building a highly successful software company by creating from a place of "wholeness" rather than "lack," optimizing for self-respect, and embracing true risk. By viewing go-to-market tools as an open-ended programming medium rather than static software, Clay scaled from 1 to 100 in two years, utilizing operational and product insights drawn from non-dual meditation, music theory, and magic.

The Origin and Strategy of Clay: "Go-To-Market Engineering"

  • The Vision of Democratized Programming: Co-founders Kareem Amin and Nicola started Clay to give the power of computation to non-technical business users. They aimed to establish a highly fluid, low-latency interaction between humans and machines, heavily inspired by music software like Ableton Live and Douglas Engelbart's 1968 "Mother of All Demos."
  • Targeting Sales and Marketing: In 2017, they selected go-to-market (GTM) teams because sales and marketing personnel are highly creative but lack the engineering resources to execute complex data experiments at scale.
      • *Example:* A garbage-disposal company using Google Satellite View to identify locations with high trash accumulation to target sales outreach—an innovative idea easily automated via Clay's flexible platform.
  • The Three Core Growth Pillars: Clay’s rapid growth from 1 to 100 in two years was enabled by three non-obvious foundational assumptions:
      • Creative, Open-Ended Tools: Rejecting the industry norm of simple, "coin-operated" sales software, Clay built a powerful, open-ended platform designed to let users discover unique "go-to-market alpha."
      • Defining the "RevOps" Persona: They positioned their tool for Revenue Operations (RevOps) and framed the discipline as "go-to-market engineering."
      • Usage-Based Pricing: They chose to bill based on platform usage rather than seat counts, ensuring that client productivity gains remained aligned with Clay's economic incentives.
  • The "Guitar" Analogy: Amin contrast commoditized software with expressive software.
      • *Microwave vs. Guitar:* Most SaaS products are "microwaves" (commodity appliances with push-button utility). Clay is a "guitar"—a tool with simple, fixed parameters (six strings, 12 frets) that users can spend a lifetime mastering to create entirely unique outcomes.

Redefining Risk, Courage, and "Post-Lack" Ambition

  • What Capitalism Truly Rewards: Amin argues that capitalism rewards genuine risk-taking rather than hard work or pure talent.
      • The Formula for Real Risk: True risk requires stepping into a situation where the outcome is genuinely unknown, paired with a high potential for personal, public shame. Amin warns that many founders avoid this by hedging and refusing to commit to a singular, specific customer group.
  • Creating from Wholeness vs. Lack: VCs often search for founders with "a chip on their shoulder" who are motivated by an internal void. Amin consciously shifted to a "post-lack" state of mind.
      • *The Post-Lack Advantage:* Creating from a place of internal wholeness reduces corporate politics, minimizes organizational destructiveness, and serves as powerful negotiation leverage because the founder has nothing to lose.
  • Optimizing for Self-Respect: Drawing parallels to Airbnb CEO Brian Chesky's experiences with the emptiness of massive wealth, Amin asserts that external prestige and adulation fail to satisfy. The only sustainable North Star is self-respect, which demands adhering to your own internal standards, which are inevitably higher than those of outside observers.

The Three Elevators Statues: Courage, Justice, and Truth

Asked what abstract values he would build as statues in his office lobby, Amin highlighted three:

  • Courage: Necessary to make decisive, scary commitments and follow them through to their logical, unhedged conclusions.
  • Justice (and Integrity): Built on treating people with fairness and respect. Amin operates on being "long-term greedy," which requires absolute integrity so that partners, employees, and customers remain willing to build with you over decades.
  • Truth: Inspired by a profound non-dual experience during a meditation retreat, Amin emphasizes the value of seeing reality as it is—recognizing that we are all deeply interconnected and that human suffering is shared. He views corporate and social squabbling as a tragic waste of human potential.

Unconventional Management and Operations

  • Over-Investing in the Non-Obvious: Clay deliberately over-allocates resources to areas most software companies under-fund, such as brand, content, community, and recruiting.
      • *PM-Level Content Compensation:* Clay hired an early employee, Mishthi, for a content role but compensated her on par with a Product Manager, reflecting their high valuation of brand communication.
  • Deep Commitment to High-Potential Talent: Rejecting the "hire fast, fire fast" startup trope, Clay will support struggling employees for up to nine months to understand if the context, role, or management style is the limiting factor. This approach results in a 50% success rate in turning struggling employees into organizational superstars.
  • Radical Transparency in Terminations: When letting people go, Amin believes in establishing a shared reality. He explicitly takes personal executive ownership of the decision rather than hiding behind performance metrics, and he promises departing employees that he will speak honestly but supportively to future reference-checkers.

Cross-Disciplinary Inspiration: Magic, Clowning, and Music Theory

  • Magicians and "Re-Enchanting" the World: Clay actively incorporates magicians into their events. Amin learns from magic's emphasis on storytelling and psychology to spark curiosity and wonder. In an era dominated by AI, Amin believes businesses must help "re-enchant" a world that people falsely assume they fully understand.
  • The Four Types of Clowning: Amin studies clowning to expand his personal and organizational boundaries. He breaks down clowning into four practices:
      • *Physical:* Traditional slapstick.
      • *Emotional Range:* Navigating people rapidly through happiness and sadness.
      • *The Jester:* Speaking truth to power.
      • *The Trickster:* Delivering deep spiritual truths through humor.
  • Music Theory and "The Emancipation of Dissonance": Drawing from music theory, Amin cites Arnold Schoenberg's concept of *The Emancipation of Dissonance*—the historical progression of music accepting increasingly complex, dissonant sounds as harmonious. Clay applies this by learning to accept unconventional ideas and non-traditional human behaviors as highly productive when placed in the right corporate context.

Vision, Wealth, and the Corporate Lifecycle

  • The Fallacy of Constant Vision: Amin argues that leaders do not always need a pre-packaged grand vision and should never manufacture one to satisfy public expectations. Instead, they should optimize for discovery, letting the vision emerge naturally.
  • The Purpose of Wealth: Material abundance is primarily valuable because it buys back a founder’s time. Operating a company because you *choose* to, rather than because you *need* to, unlocks the freedom to take much larger, unhedged swings.
  • A "Death Doula" for Companies: Amin challenges the societal assumption that every business must scale infinitely. He proposes that companies should have natural lifecycles. Once a company achieves its core mission (similar to Microsoft's original goal of placing a computer on every desk), it should be allowed to gracefully disband or birth new ventures rather than transforming into a low-quality, value-extracting "zombie" corporation.

Notable Quotes

"I think capitalism rewards risk more than anything else... To take real risk, you need to not know what's going to happen genuinely, and it needs to be coupled with a high potential for shame." — Kareem Amin
"I had this moment where I was like, you know what, I'm not going to create from a place of lack anymore... I think you can create from a place of wholeness and in fact you will be less destructive in the world." — Kareem Amin
"We’re not building a microwave... We’re building a guitar. With a guitar there's just six strings and 12 frets, but you could spend your whole life just getting better." — Kareem Amin
"A death doula for companies is: if your company has achieved its mission, should we help it pass? ... Instead of trying to scale things that become zombies or become worse versions of their former selves." — Kareem Amin
I feel like people don't talk enough about things that actually matter in building a business. I think capitalism rewards risk. You need courage to make a leap and actually commit to these ideas. To take real risk, you need to genuinely not know what's going to happen. That is how you discover something new. You might fail in a way that feels scary or is associated with shame, but when you are doing that, you are on the verge of discovering something new. That's when you can take real risk. Kareem Amin. Patrick O'Shaughnessy. Invest Like The Best. Can you describe the long, strange journey you've been on? Why you started, what it was like to exist and have done some interesting stuff when this thing happened, and then how you took advantage of it? I find the two-chapter story so interesting. We started with the really abstract ambition of how to give the power of programming to more people. My co-founder Nicola and I had worked in product, even though we both studied engineering, and we were trying to build things. I think it was really just: hey, you're in the 21st century, you have computers that can do things for you, and if you can't tell them what to do, then you can't capture any of the value that's being created. We had a lot of ideas and we wanted to build them, but every time we tried to build something, it was taking so long. I was also starting to play around with producing music, and I really liked tools like Ableton Live. If I have an idea, I can extract it from my mind and put it straight into the machine. We were also in this kick of looking at the history of computation. We looked at things from the 1960s, like the "mother of all demos," just seeing the fluidity of interaction. The interaction between you and a computer needs to be super fluid for it actually to lead you to the next stage of thinking. You need low latency between "I have an idea" and "it happened." Because we were coding, we felt that adrenaline that everybody is feeling right now, which is: hey, I can tell the machine to do something and it does. In the 21st century, the material really is computation. It's kind of like how oil or steel was in the past; today, it's computation. The more we can give that power to more people, the more interesting things happen, and the more egalitarian it is. It's kind of interesting because that was our desire before there were any LLMs. Actually, we started off by thinking about the terminal, trying to figure out how to reinvent the terminal where engineers spend all of their time. But then we realized that was just going to speed up engineers. We needed to find something that gives that power to more people. In 2017, we started thinking: do we give it to consumers or business users? We ended up deciding on business users for a variety of reasons. Within that, we asked ourselves which department in a business actually has a ton of ideas but isn't able to execute them. We landed on sales and marketing, where we think people are really creative but lack the ability to turn a lot of their ideas into reality. I'll give you one example that's real today, which is kind of wild. We have one company that sells to businesses that have a lot of garbage. Now, they are using Google satellite view to see which areas have a large accumulation of garbage, and then they figure out if that's a good business to sell to. That's a great idea, but it might be super hard to execute at scale. Now, something like Clay can actually translate that for you. Because the whole intention around Clay was how to give the power of programming to people, that's how the name came about. We were like, "Okay, it's go-to-market, but now you're an engineer." We came up with that before it was really possible to use LLMs to fully code. It was about how to systematically think about a problem, break it down, and execute it. We've kept that ethos in the company, and that's what makes us really different from everybody else in our space. There are simple things that we've been saying out loud, but I think people don't really believe that's why it works. One is that it's a creative task, and if it's a creative task, we need to leave it somewhat open-ended. Many of our competitors in this space say that what you really want is a system for salespeople who are—and people have literally used this word—"coin-operated." They think you need something super easy to use that just gives you the answer, tells you who you're selling to, says what you need to say, and just works. Whereas we think you always need to find go-to-market alpha in sales. How are you different from everybody else? Otherwise, it's just noise. In order to do that, you need to have a really powerful tool that allows you to experiment quickly, try things out, and see what works. So we built that instead, which was very counterintuitive. Describe the takeoff of chapter two: okay, now LLMs come out. Sort of what happened in that, what that moment felt like, and what the subchapters have been since. What's interesting in that story, and is maybe worth talking a bit more about, is that it wasn't as simple as that. This was back in 2022, and we had made a decision to say, "Okay, we are targeting go-to-market teams, specifically sales." Specifically, we were going to start with outbound, and specifically with people who want to get data from lots of different data providers in order to do outbound. In fact, it was going to be agencies and not startups, and we were going to support them in public and build a community. We made a lot of decisions that I think were non-obvious. The main thing that happened was really having the courage and commitment to those decisions, which is what actually allowed the company to take off. The company was already taking off before ChatGPT came out, and we were well-set to include LLMs in Clay. First, we were set up to allow any integration to anything, and second, we were a coding-like product designed with go-to-market primitives. As soon as we had LLMs, it just boosted everything. There were three core assumptions that we made, and I actually think this is how you build a really big company that grows quickly. We went from 1 to 100 in two years, but that wouldn't have worked if we weren't pointing in the right direction. You need to set everything up so that you're pointing in the right direction, so that when the wave comes, you go with it and you're not fighting it. First, people in go-to-market are creative, so we're going to give them the most powerful tool. If you are going to give them the most powerful tool instead of the simplest out-of-the-box tool, then you need to find the right customer or user for it. For that, we targeted RevOps, which is an existing role, and we called the subset of jobs to be done "go-to-market engineering" to give it a framing. These tasks are all the activities you need to do to grow your business and remove any obstacles to growth. And then the last part was that we're going to charge for usage instead of per seat, because we're trying to drive productivity. If you have fewer people, we didn't want that to be an anti-incentive for us. If you look at those three decisions, almost every other decision in the company falls immediately out of them. Anyone can come up with what we should do next if they believe those three things. I think that's the best way to run a company that's growing quickly, because I just have to communicate those three assumptions. Whenever you're making a decision, check to see if it works within those three concepts. I feel like people don't talk enough about the things that actually matter in building a business. I feel like we've forgotten that these abstract ideas are what actually matter in the moment. I think capitalism rewards risk more than anything else. A lot of people think it rewards meritocracy or hard work, which I think is silly. There are many people working incredibly hard who do not make a lot of money, so it's definitely not just hard work. There's some correlation, but it's not just that. Again, there are many highly skilled people doing things I can't even dream of, like eating swords or juggling, and they aren't making as much money as someone making a YouTube video. To take real risk—and there are many people who think they're taking risks but aren't taking real risks. You must be able to fail in a way that feels scary or associated with shame. When you are doing that, you are genuinely about to learn and discover something new. That's when you can take real risk, and to do that, you need courage. Let's take justice for a second, because I want to live in a stable society that allows me the time and space to think and explore. The world is a pretty crazy place. I went to see the movie *Stop Making Sense*. I was downstairs in the movie theater, and I had a joint that I was about to smoke. I was trying to light it up, but I didn't have a lighter. I asked a guy standing nearby if I could borrow his lighter, and he said, "Sure, but can I ask you a question?" I said, "Yeah, of course." He asked, "What is this?" I said, "What do you mean, *this* whole thing?" He said, "Yeah, what is this?" I replied, "Well, that's a big question, and I haven't even smoked my joint yet." If I were just thrown into this world and asked what it is, I would say the world is a mysterious and amazing place, and there is so much to know. We are just starting to make progress on how wonderfully weird it is, and we should be spending more time exploring that. To do that, we need stability, and to get stability, we need justice between people. There is no stable way for one group of people to dominate others; it simply doesn't work. No matter how strong you think you are, the other person becomes a tremendous nuisance if they feel they are being treated unfairly. There is no stable configuration there. If you look at the economy or countries, you can't just bluster your way out of it. You can't say, "We're the strongest, so you have to do what we want." When you have power over people, you tend to think they must do what you want because you control things, but that is not stable. You can get by for a little bit, and then you get tremendous pushback. So I think justice is essential. One thing I think about is how to treat people with respect and fairness all the time, in every situation, whether you are hiring them, firing them, or disagreeing over what they should be doing. That is what fuels democratization. People are stable when they feel they have a fair opportunity to reach the same level you have. They are okay with where they are if they feel they have a fair chance. On a personal note, I used to play soccer, and if my team fouled the other team and got away with it, I would feel off. It was a weird issue of integrity. Do you have a sense of where that came from? It's an intuitive knowing for myself. I am a long-term greedy person, which is something we talk about a lot at the company. The only way to be long-term greedy is to stay in integrity, so people feel there is justice all the way along. That way, you build trust. It is also a belief in yourself. You don't have to cheat in the short term to win; you believe you are going to win anyway. If you play a game, cheat, and then win, it just doesn't feel as good. I heard your interview with Brian Chesky, and the statement he made about reaching a hundred billion dollars and feeling like shit. I really related to that. We are in a much smaller spot, of course, but even early on when we raised our Series A, I remember having that sensation. If you are doing this to get adulation, prestige, or money, there is no end to it. It's an empty, passing thing. The only real judge of your work is yourself and your own self-respect. You have to do it so you feel what you've done is interesting and worthwhile. When you're an ambitious person, your standards are actually way higher than anyone else's because you know everything you're thinking and everything you've done. I know my own thoughts and actions, and my standards are higher than anyone else's. There's this thing I like to do where when I spend some time with somebody I wait an hour and then I write down notes in my phone because I benefit from the hour of cleansing. Whatever lasts in my brain through that hour is what I want to write down. When we last were together, this is the one thing I wrote down: this notion of optimizing for self-respect over everything else as the North Star for behaving. I'm curious how optimizing for self-respect relates back to risk and courage. Do you think those things are related? And do you think it's possible to help people become more courageous and take more risk? I think in order to do that, you just need to understand your mind and your motivations more, and be more honest about them. It's very hard to admit to yourself why you are doing something. If you look at the public narratives of companies, none of them relate to the actual, honest motivations of the founders. The real story is usually a mixture of things we don't talk about in public. For me, for example, why am I building a company? Do I think I will be more liked or loved if I succeed at a world-class level? And can I get that love right now? I've had friends tell me, "Hey, I don't care if you succeed or fail, I love you." That was incredibly helpful because it made me realize that while part of my desire is fueled by that need, I already have it. Now, I can take real risks in the business without trying to fill a personal void. VCs have this common trope that they invest in people with a chip on their shoulder, or some lack they are trying to fulfill. This is common in creative work too, where the trope is that you need to be anxious or depressed to create, and you're afraid to lose that anxiety because you think you'll be less productive. Through my journey, I had a moment where I decided I was no longer going to create from a place of lack. I went around, probably pretty annoyingly, telling people, "I have everything I need. I'm good." Being "post-lack" is great negotiation leverage because there is nothing you can offer me to change how I feel; I already feel complete. From that place, you can still create. I think people tremendously misunderstand this. They think if we were in a post-scarcity world, everyone would just lounge around and do nothing. But if you've been to Burning Man, you know people still build and do amazing things even when they have what they need. You can create from a place of wholeness, and you will actually be less destructive in the world. You will cause less damage because you are focused on what you are giving to your customers, rather than managing internal politics or trying to please people for your career. The more whole you are, the more risks you can take because you have nothing to lose. What was the Day 5 insight, or was that the insight itself? No, the Day 5 insight was a momentary experience of understanding what truly matters. I felt connected to all things, like I was a small component of this massive whole, and that everything matters. I realized I am no better and no worse than anyone else. It gave me this deep, real empathy. When I see someone suffering, they are also me. It's not just that I empathize with them as if I were them; it is that they actually are me. That is a pretty out-there belief. The strange thing is it's actually not at all; it's the longest-lasting set of beliefs in the deepest, richest philosophical traditions. What was crazy wasn't the idea itself, but the direct experience of really feeling it. I understood what Buddhism means by the "freedom of all beings," or knowing that the suffering of one is the suffering of everyone. Going back to the justice thing, I can't solve everyone's problems, and it would actually hurt me to try to help everyone when I don't have the capacity. But it was powerful to realize that other people are carrying their struggles for me, rather than thinking they just messed up while I succeeded because I worked harder. Is the third statue then Truth? Is that Day 5 insight just Truth? Yes. If we go back to capital-T Truth, it is about really understanding that connection. I understand why philosophical and religious traditions emphasize remembrance, because while you can get that momentary understanding, remembering it when making business or personal decisions is very tricky. But that is what is necessary for us to reach the next stage of what is interesting in the world. There are so many scientific mysteries and fascinating things out there, yet I feel like we are constantly squabbling in circles over the same nonsense. My real anger is: what are we doing? We are wasting our time and our human potential. As your business scales up, everything gets more complex, especially your compliance and security needs. It seems also like an interesting throughline is in the world we're going into, how important it will be to make people feel something, but maybe also something new. She took a content role, which she normally wouldn't have done because content roles aren't typically the most highly paid. We compensated her like a PM and told her to run with it. We take those risks and we commit to them. When you combine risk and courage, the next step is commitment. You have to follow through to see what actually happens; you can't just quit early on. If we see someone with real star potential, we stay with them much longer than most companies do. People always talk about hiring fast and firing fast. We've had people who were floundering nine months in, and we said, "This person is excellent, but is it the role, the context, or our own decision-making that is limiting them?" In many cases, we eventually find an amazing spot for them, and they become a superstar. The other half of the time, we simply can't create the right conditions for them to succeed. My co-founder Varun and I are never angry when someone doesn't work out. We just view it as: we are here, and you are there, and you aren't getting to where we need you to be fast enough right now. I don't have an opinion on whether you are "good" or "bad." The question is whether you were the right fit for the specific moment we needed you. There are generally four types of clowning. First is physical clowning, which is what you typically picture. Second is emotional range, where the goal is to make you happy, then sad, then happy again, letting you feel the full spectrum of emotion. Third is the jester, who tells truth to power.
