RBC CEO on AI, Jobs, SpaceX IPO, US Trade, Energy Demand

Bloomberg Television(3.2M)·30:21·923 views·2026-06-16
Dave McKayCEO, Royal Bank of Canada
TL;DRRoyal Bank of Canada (RBC) is capitalizing on a highly active, "risk-on" market environment by expanding its US footprint, investing equity in Canadian scaling companies, and deploying a highly customized, proprietary AI stack.
RBC CEO Dave McKay emphasizes that a...

TL;DR

Royal Bank of Canada (RBC) is capitalizing on a highly active, "risk-on" market environment by expanding its US footprint, investing equity in Canadian scaling companies, and deploying a highly customized, proprietary AI stack. RBC CEO Dave McKay emphasizes that a combination of rapid AI adoption and massive baby-boomer retirement demographics will fundamentally accelerate learning curves and career progression for the next generation of finance professionals.

Market Dynamics, USMCA Trade, and Global Expansion

  • Risk-On Market Sentiment: There is an intense hunger for new capital issues, space and satellite technologies (such as the SpaceX IPO, where RBC was the sole Canadian bank involved), and AI compute capacity.
      • This capital intensity is driving massive demand across the entire capital stack, including syndicated bank debt, high-yield issuance, and equity.
  • Geographical Growth Divergence: RBC is actively taking on more risk and expanding its balance sheet in the United States, where tech-driven demand and the economy remain incredibly strong.
      • In Canada, commercial credit demand is more muted, partly due to prolonged negotiations surrounding the United States-Mexico-Canada Agreement (USMCA).
  • USMCA Stability: Despite political tensions, the $1.3 trillion bilateral trade relationship between the US and Canada remains too critical to fail. McKay notes that 35 US states count Canada as their top export partner, meaning cancellation of the deal would cause severe inflationary shocks.
      • The ongoing negotiations focus on the *termination of the extension option* (post-2036) rather than a complete cancellation of the agreement.
  • Global Diversification Strategy: Canada is actively diversifying its trade network beyond the US to export more liquefied natural gas (LNG), crude oil, rare-earth minerals, and agricultural products.
      • RBC's International Footprint: To support this global push, RBC is expanding its wealth and capital markets capabilities in Europe (including Paris), the Middle East, and Asia. This global transition was heavily accelerated by RBC's recent acquisition of HSBC Canada.

Growth Equity and the Canadian Scaling Challenge

  • The Scaling Capital Gap: While Canada has excellent academic institutions and seed-stage capital, it lacks late-stage scaling capital. Historically, 70% of Canadian growth capital has come from the United States.
      • To prevent high-growth Canadian firms from moving their intellectual property (IP) to the US or selling prematurely, RBC is stepping in to provide direct equity investments to help domestic companies scale.
  • Strategic Investment Sectors: RBC's equity investments focus heavily on companies with strong technology and innovation angles:
      • Quantum & Photonics: A major investment in a Vancouver-based photonics and quantum computing business.
      • Digital Healthcare: An equity stake in a scaled digital health provider serving North America.
      • Agri-Tech: A pending investment in an innovative, lentil-based protein player utilizing advanced extraction technologies for export to Asian markets.

The "Agentic CEO" and RBC's Proprietary AI Strategy

  • The Agentic CEO Office: McKay designed and implemented a custom suite of "agentic" AI tools to streamline his daily operations. He spends at least one hour every morning reviewing a customized, model-generated report that links external macroeconomic trends with internal bank data. His custom tool includes:
      • Macro Model: Evaluates and compares global and Canadian economic forecasts against RBC's internal data.
      • Markets Model: Monitours real-time foreign exchange (FX), treasury yields, and trading desk positions.
      • Consumer Behavior Model: Tracks regional credit card spending, default rates, and purchasing patterns (e.g., travel vs. goods).
      • Mortgage & Risk Modules: Monitours term vs. variable mortgage ratios, pre-sales, and default pipelines.
  • Proprietary GPU Tech Stack: RBC chose to build its own on-premise GPU tech stack with NVIDIA rather than relying solely on cloud hyperscalers.
      • The "Adam" LLM: Leveraging its 100+ PhDs at the Borealis AI Institute, RBC developed its own proprietary frontier Large Language Model (LLM), named "Adam," which converts AI computing into a fixed cost rather than a variable "per-token" cost.
  • Macroeconomic Impact of AI: McKay believes AI will follow a similar adoption curve to the personal computer in the 1990s. While initially inflationary due to heavy upfront infrastructure costs, it will eventually become highly deflationary as massive productivity gains are realized across the economy.

The Future of Banking Talent and Workforce Evolution

  • Revenue Enablement vs. Job Cuts: RBC is not deploying AI to cut back-office headcount. Instead, the bank is using AI models to automate adjudication and fulfillment, shifting staff from the back office to front-office, customer-facing roles.
      • The bank aims to leverage these efficiency gains to scale its customer base from 17 million to 25 million.
  • Demographic Imperative: Over the next five years, 30 million Americans and nearly 2 million Canadians from the baby boomer generation are expected to retire. Because there are not enough trained workers to replace them, AI is critical to boosting societal productivity.
  • Accelerating the "Learning Flywheel": Historically, it took banking associates 10 to 15 years to gain the experience required to make highly material decisions. With LLM-based training tools, young professionals can master banking concepts and take on major responsibilities in a fraction of that time.
      • RBC is leaning heavily into this young talent pipeline, hiring over 3,600 students this summer alone—a 5% increase year-over-year.

Notable Quotes

"We needed more Canadian growth capital and therefore RBC stepped into that gap to say, 'we have to put more equity into these companies so they can scale here'... If they don't get that scaling capital from Canada, invariably they move their IP to the US or they sell globally." — Dave McKay
"Most of the deployment of our AI models now are not cost take-out in the back office, they are revenue enablement models, adding more customers, making our front line more efficient and more effective... we want to grab another 8 to 10 million customers through this." — Dave McKay
"One pathway to success at a big company, but even a small company, is creating a learning flywheel. You have to learn, learn, learn, learn fast... If you master these tools in school and come into the organization knowing how to leverage an LLM... you're going to be able to take on more responsibility so much more quickly." — Dave McKay
There is a sense that the world is moving very quickly, and markets very much are in focus. I want to start by getting an overview of how you're seeing things right now. SpaceX IPO—you were the only Canadian bank to play a part in the distribution of that. How would you describe the state of markets that we're seeing right now? Well, I think there's a real appetite to take risk. We see that in Canada, and you see that in the United States. It's absolutely risk-on right now. There's a hunger for new issues, as you saw with SpaceX. There's a real focus on technology, without a doubt. But even look at Canada. When we talk about building new pipelines, building rare-earth mineral capability, and exporting more food, the capital flow is very intense and strong. In fact, we're going to have an Investor Day for Canada in September that I'm sure we'll talk about. So when you look at the risk-on environment, we're certainly feeling that in our own stock and seeing the greatest foreign interest we've seen in the RBC stock in quite some time. There's a hunger for traditional businesses, but then, as to your point, there are these emerging technologies in space and satellites, absolutely. The push for AI and capacity—compute capacity, memory capacity—is driving an insatiable appetite for all forms of capital, debt and equity. It's insatiable both on the part of the companies looking to borrow, as well as the investors looking to get in. It feels like there's this race. Why? Why is everyone so eager at this moment? Why is it important to just hoover up as much cash as you possibly can and lend your money out as much as you can? Well, we're being careful. We're not just throwing it out there, but we're certainly within our credit standards. There are a couple of things happening. One, there is a race among the hyperscalers and among the compute and memory providers to capture this demand, right? So there's a race to serve clients. All these hyperscalers and frontier models have demand for their product. There's a shortage currently; we don't know what the long-term demand and supply cycle is, but certainly there's a shortage of supply against demand. So there's the race to capture that, and who can get these things built. Energy supply is also critical, and there's a "not in my backyard" NIMBYism creeping up. So there is a race to capture this demand and scale. It's a scale bet, and I think that's driving demand for capital. There's also on the other side a fear of, "Will there be enough capital for all the demand out there?" It's driving a huge amount of not only bank-to-bank debt and syndicated bank debt, but certainly high-yield issuance and equity issuance. We're using the full capital stack already to satiate this short-term demand. Are we running out? Are we running out? No, but I think you're certainly seeing in the syndicated bank market that it's not infinite, and you see bank balance sheets filling up really quickly. It's not an unlimited amount. If you're prudent about limiting your sector approach, you have to watch your balance sheet as well. So I think you're seeing high-yield issuance, and you're also seeing equity issuance on this side. It is a very significant growth engine for the economy and for bank balance sheets. As a bank, are you taking more risk right now or paring it back because balance sheets are filling up? It's a great question. I think depending on the economy you're in, we're certainly taking more risk in the United States right now because the economy is performing strongly and there's demand, particularly out of the tech sector. In Canada, I think demand is a little more muted in a number of areas because we haven't resolved the USMCA. I think the confidence in investing in the long-term is a little slower right now. We're certainly seeing some demand come out of the Canadian economy, but I would say it's slowing. So it depends on the sector and the market, but demand out of the US—and where we're seeing most of our balance sheet growth—is in the United States right now. Well, you've been expanding in the US. You've seen this pretty considerably over the past number of years. Are you continuing to expand in the US at a time where the relationship between the US and Canada is increasingly fraught? I think the relationship, which goes back hundreds of years, is actually strong because it's embedded in 1.3 trillion dollars of trade that go back and forth every year between the two countries. In gross trade, that's roughly 750 billion each way. This trade is largely balanced between the two countries, and so the foundation of the USMCA is really, really strong. But economies evolve. The US economy evolves, the Canadian economy evolves, and sometimes you get out of alignment. We are going through a public-private process of negotiating those changes. I think that is a normal process, and we are making progress in recognizing what the administration needs in a change in the United States and what Canada needs in a change. I'm confident we'll find that agreement over time, but it is a protracted process. There's been no mention of canceling the agreement. There is a big difference between termination and cancellation. Termination just means that we're not extending for six years from 2036, while cancellation means you're giving notice of a permanent withdrawal. This agreement is too important to the United States, Canada, and Mexico, I believe, to cancel. Because of that trade, for 35 states in the United States, Canada is their number one export partner. So it would have a significant shock, an inflationary shock. That's why we're discussing more the termination of the extension option, but not the cancellation of the deal. We're trying to find a way to bring everything together with the changes in our economy. Do you see Canadian companies trying to expand their operations outside of the US-Canadian borders? In other words, maybe looking more to Europe, looking more to even Asia to try to get a little bit more diversity? Absolutely. It's a big part of our Prime Minister's strategy to diversify. Canada has 80% of its trade with the United States. If I sat down with a customer and said, "You have 80% of your sales with one customer, don't you think you should diversify a little bit to de-risk that?" That's what Canada has. This is an "and." We want to maintain our 1.3 trillion dollar relationship with the United States and grow that. At the same time, why wouldn't you want to grow other relationships? We want to export another million to two million barrels of oil a year. We want to export another, you know, 100% increase in our LNG—our billion cubic feet a year up to, I think, 100 billion cubic feet. We want to export more rare-earth minerals. We can be a protein superpower. We've doubled our current capacity to grow food, particularly canola and wheat, and we think we can export more of that. We need more infrastructure to do it, so Canada is looking for more partners and wants to expand and diversify its economy. It's an "and," and I think that creates prosperity and a greater ability to pay our own way. The US has been very clear, particularly on defense: "You've got to pay your own way, the right way," and we've increased our spend up over 2% now. A stronger Canada makes a stronger North America. So it's an "and," and I think it has created unprecedented opportunity for the next decade for Canada. Are you expanding as well in places like Europe and China? Are we in China? Not so much. Our expansion is United States first, into Europe second. We're certainly growing out with a large wealth and a large capital markets operation in Europe. We're expanding into the continent to a greater degree, with a bigger office in Paris, and then we're looking at the Middle East, as you heard, and Asia. Part of serving the needs of Canadian and US exporters is to have a greater global network. So it's about global transaction banking, FX capabilities, trade finance, and lending capabilities. It's about being present in those markets as well. So, yes, we have very much more of a global mindset. The HSBC acquisition in Canada brought that to us a little bit, and they showed us how to serve that client in a very different way. We learned a lot by bringing HSBC Canada into RBC, and the number of true global clients that came with that was super exciting. It has created a greater ambition for us, alongside our national ambition, to serve a more global client base. I love that Canada could be a protein superpower, so you're going to drive the sort of fitness energy around the world that much more. You're going to try to really get that going. Maybe get Ryan Reynolds to participate in that. Anyway, one thing that you've talked about is you taking equity investments in Canadian companies, and that that's important to you. It seems like this is actually a popular thing to do. The idea, JP Morgan's done this in terms of taking equity stakes in certain companies that are crucial to the infrastructure. What types of companies are you investing in and why now? I'll start with the second part of the question. We have great seed capital in this country. It comes in all forms. We have great universities, and we have a lot of ideas. Those ideas are percolating up and getting executed on, but we don't have great scaling capital. We have a challenge, as do many countries in Europe, including the UK. The US scales really, really well given its market size and the amount of risk capital you have there. Historically, 70% of the scaling and growth capital in Canada came out of the United States, and we've seen a diminishment of that. We needed more Canadian growth capital, and therefore RBC stepped into that gap to say, "We have to put more equity into these companies so they can scale here." They start here, they scale. If they don't get that scaling capital from Canada, invariably they move their IP to the US or they sell globally. We need them to scale into larger companies and create that flywheel effect that you've seen with Shopify, BlackBerry, OpenText, and so many others. So that's the why. We are short on scaling and growth capital, so we need that. As for the types of companies we are looking at, we made an investment in quantum to start, and in photonics in Vancouver, which has an incredible capability. They're doing exceptionally well and competing for the next major launch of that machine capability. We also made an investment in a healthcare provider that serves North America in a very scaled digital technology space. They all have a technology angle to them. We're looking seriously at our next investment in a protein player—a lentil-based protein with an innovative tech story on how to extract that protein out of lentils with off-take to Asia. Again, we are trying to follow those themes with a tech angle, an innovation angle, a growth angle, agriculture, tech space, and compute. That's what we're trying to push. You kept mentioning tech, so I think we should get into how you're deploying technology throughout RBC. The AI tools that you've used, and can you just start with how it's changed your day personally? Well, great question. I have actually sat down and put some thought into it over the last year, and I designed and asked the team to implement, if they could, an "agentic CEO office." I designed a suite of agentic tools that I thought would empower me to be more insightful about what's going on externally and internally. It has a 24/7 always-on capability so that I can interact with a model and get an answer. It also allows me to be more efficient with my team. Instead of sending an email at night asking them to produce a report, getting that report a week later, digesting it, and calling them about the answer, I get that report in about four seconds. It's built on our data and, obviously, an LLM searching the entire universe of information. As for components of that, I've got a macro model where I can interact on what's going on in the macroeconomy. I can look at our forecast, look at other banks' forecasts, and see what the global and Canadian economies are doing. I can interact and drill that down to any element I want, comparing it to our own internal data. I've also got a markets model where I can talk about what FX is doing, what treasury yields are doing, what the drivers are, what people are saying, what our traders are saying, and what our positions are. So I've got that markets model. Then I have a consumer behavior model where I can look at credit card trends, regional credit card trends, and default rates. What are people buying? Where are they shopping? What types of services are they using—is it goods or travel? How is that changing, how is it growing, and how does that link into the economy? I can look at the mortgage business as well—what mortgage terms or pre-sales we are doing, whether we are in pre-sale mode, going term, or going variable, and what the default rates are. I can look at all of that to get a feel for how the consumer is behaving and link that back into what the economy is doing. It's all really important. I've got a risk module, and I'm currently building an HR module to look at talent. I have this 24/7 always-on capability based on all our internal data—an incredible amount of internal and external data—and it gives me this unique view to understand the external and internal environments at the same time. How much time do you spend in the morning? I start every morning with it. So how long? How long depends on my schedule in the morning, but I get lost in it a little bit, and my email backlog builds up. I would spend at least an hour on it in the morning. Okay. First thing I do is sit down and let it produce a report of what it thinks I should know as a CEO, based on what I've been looking at historically and things I'm tracking. Then, I either listen to that or disregard it and go into specific things that I need to know. Okay, so the staff is in the room. Plug your ears. Do you need the same number of people? Do you need the same team around you if you can do this all yourself? It shortens the cycle, but I still invariably call my team and say, "Hey, the model told me this and it seems intuitively right. Does that seem intuitively right to you at the end of the day?" And then, more importantly, "What should we do about it?" Because if it's just information and we're not doing anything, then I'm not creating real value at the end of the day. So for me, the model adds value when it leads me to ask what we should be doing differently. It shortens the conversation. So I think absolutely you need the team, but it increases the capacity of what we can handle and condenses the speed. I was talking to a client yesterday in the tech industry, and we talked about coding. And he said, "What it used to take eight employees two months to do, takes two employees four hours to do." Eight employees, two months—takes two employees eight hours. He said, "What I've done is I've massively expanded the capacity of the group and the ambition of the group to do more." So I think, honestly, where we are now is just massively expanding the capacity of the organization to move, create more capability and content, and serve more customers. We serve 17 million customers; our ambition is to serve 25 million customers. Most of the deployment of our AI models now is not for cost take-out in the back office; they are revenue enablement models, adding more customers, and making our frontline more efficient and more effective. That's where we put our focus because we want to grab another 8 to 10 million customers through this. That's where we're focusing. How does that change the composition of the bank staff? You're going to have more producers who are customer-focused, and you'll see a shift from the back office to the front office, for sure. The machine will be able to start from adjudication and move back to fulfillment, closing your service contracts with customers more efficiently. Therefore, you'll be able to serve more customers per employee and have more customers in the franchise, so certainly there's a shift. I am also seeing individuals learn the business more quickly. It is unbelievable how fast these models are training our staff about banking. Instead of searching around for information and taking all this time, the information is right in front of you. You learn, you learn, and you learn. You get on these learning flywheels. I've seen young people come into the bank, and they are learning our business so quickly. We want to make sure everybody in the organization gets on that learning curve. As a society, we're going to learn so quickly, and my hope is we master these models because they're going to make us smarter too—a lot smarter. Do you think that there are fewer people you need though overall? I mean if RBC let's say gains market share and can do more with the same number of people, arguably it's taking from another maybe smaller bank that would be doing the same type of thing. I just wonder from your hiring and kind of what you're seeing if that's kind of the path forward. I think we have to, because we have an aging demographic. We have so many boomers—30 million Americans are going to retire over the next five years, and just under two million Canadians of my generation are going to retire. We don't have enough trained people to replace them. In healthcare, education, engineering, and everything else we have to do, including banking, we don't have enough people. Therefore, we're going to need to be more efficient, in my view. Our view is that we'll be more efficient, and we'll maybe need fewer people, but a lot of that is coming through attrition and demographic change. We need that in our society, and we need these tools. We've seen a number of companies, Uber most notably recently, pull back on the number of tokens it was allowing some of its staff to work with just because it's expensive. And you know, it turns out humans need water and they need energy and so do the tokens that do some of the work. I mean, have you experienced the same type of pressures or limited the token use of your staff? That's a great question. One of the unique capabilities that we had the foresight to build is our tech stack. We worked with NVIDIA and Jensen Huang to build it. So not only do we have our own data center, our own data, and our own on-prem cloud, but we built our own GPU tech stack with NVIDIA. I have my own compute power, so I don't have to rent that compute power from a hyperscaler. On top of that GPU tech stack, we built our own frontier LLM called Adam. We've had a hundred PhDs in our Borealis Institute at RBC for over a decade now, working with machine learning and reinforcement learning. Eventually, we started experimenting with LLMs. When OpenAI's ChatGPT came out, we were already experimenting with that type of capability, even though we weren't building it for the exact same purpose. Having our own tech stack allows us to have our own tokens. When we're experimenting and learning, we're using these LLMs internally. While we still rent some tokens from Anthropic, OpenAI, and Cohere, most of it comes from our own capability. This keeps the cost fixed rather than variable, and it allows us to do more than others. I think that's a huge advantage for us. Do you see AI right now as being more inflationary or more deflationary in the long term? The cost to build is certainly part of the growth curve in the economy, and it's driving the economy. When you go back to how the personal computer came in 20 to 30 years ago, it was inflationary to start. You didn't see the productivity immediately; it took about six to seven years for that productivity to really hit. I think we're on a similar curve. There's a cost for tokens, there's a cost for this infrastructure, and we're learning how to deploy these models into our business and sales processes. The models are performing well; they have veracity and are accurate enough to deploy. Therefore, we're just training our employees how to use them right now, so the productivity lift will start to come. I think at the end of the day, this will be deflationary because it will create so much productivity capability inside your organization and our economy. But as we've seen, the curve starts inflationary and then goes to deflationary. You know, this is a conundrum for a lot of people seeing the risk appetite right now in markets. Because when it becomes deflationary, there won't be as much money for certain companies maybe that are able to charge whatever they want at a time for a race. And it's starting to lead to some concerns about valuation models and whether things are getting a little bit over their skis. How worried are you? There is going to be change to different models. We've seen this SaaS question coming up: Will you continue to rent SaaS models from third-party providers, or will you be able to code them? I think it's a bit of both. There are certainly capabilities we will code internally that we previously outsourced to third-party vendors. We have a list of them and are working through them right now, which creates cost savings for us to invest in other parts of the organization. From that perspective, at the end of the day, there are going to be new business models coming that we just can't foresee, where people sit down with this tool and say, "I can create value by doing this for you." I think we're also seeing that we're getting a lot more efficient in running these queries. Even if you don't have your own tokens like we do and you're buying them, there are a lot of routines and providers out there trying to sell you solutions to use tokens more efficiently. For example, if your employees are asking the same question a thousand times a day, why not just cache the answer so you don't consume a token every time? So there is an efficiency play that we're just learning how to do, and there's going to be an efficiency curve applied as well. I do see significant change coming in the world of software, how it is produced, and how it is consumed. The market is right to be concerned, but it will take time because we are playing both offense and defense, and defense consumes time as well. Where are we in the default cycle, given that some of the companies that are most subject to disruption are starting to see the effects, but they still are cash-flow positive? I'm thinking of whether it's software-as-a-service, the SaaS-pocalypse as some people are calling it, whether it's producers in Hollywood, whether it's some of these other things that could potentially be disintermediated. How far along are we in that? When you look at most software providers, I don't think it's a default issue because they have low costs of production, right? For them, it's probably a valuation issue where your valuation is based on cash flow at a certain price point. When you're sitting down with a customer like us and we have the option to reprogram with Anthropic code or something similar, that price negotiation goes a bit differently. Therefore, the revenue model is more at risk. But there's so much margin in those products that I don't think it creates a default cycle necessarily. It is a valuation challenge, which the market has already appreciated, absolutely. Going forward, how would you sort of characterize where we are in the business cycle, both in Canada as well as in the US, which are two very different ones partly because the two consecutive quarters of negative growth in Canada, a lot of it having to do with some of the new tariffs and other US-Canada trade tensions. They are two very different markets. The US is going to print a very large number, I think, for its Q2 growth, driven by significant government spend with a 7% structural deficit. Add to that the hyperscaler investments and a resilient, strong consumer in the United States, growing at over 2%. A lot of the engines in the United States are working well. In Canada, we are impacted, but I would say you're going to see a positive print coming in the next quarter. We exited the first quarter with much stronger performance, and that's why there's a lot of dispute about it being a technical recession—we don't really believe we're in a recession. We think there's going to be a 1-plus percent positive print. Job numbers are very strong, with two consecutive months of robust job creation, and the Canadian consumer is resilient and spending as well. We're starting to see the green shoots of the residential mortgage market come back, which is a big part of the Canadian economy. We're feeling generally good about our momentum exiting Q1, even though we had that technical negative print. Another major factor in the Canadian economy right now is the pause in immigration. Our population growth has slowed. Immigration was a big part of our growth story for the last 5 to 10 years. Right now, the year-over-year numbers are down, but GDP per capita is up because each consumer is consuming a little bit more, even though we have fewer new consumers. Those are the key drivers. I would say we're coming out of this with major superchargers: 80 billion dollars of defense spend coming, the construction of at least two pipelines, investments in port and rail infrastructure to get grain to the Vancouver port, and rare-earth mineral infrastructure. All these catalysts are coming, which is why we are seeing a risk-on environment in Canada right now. How much do you see this as a moment for big banks that's almost revenge of the big guys, coming back in to take over where private capital left off after they were unable to raise all the money that all of these big companies needed to and suddenly you have to help out and come in and frankly take market share? And just using our balance sheet to support. We never exited that market, and we've seen strong growth. Now, we're seeing accelerated growth in the United States because of the absence of private capital. That demand is definitely there. That is good for our business and good for shareholders. We like the risk, and the margins are good. Funding levels are still tight, particularly for investment grade, but even in non-investment grade markets, things are pretty well-priced. I think it's efficient to raise money right now, and you're seeing a number of companies jump in because the curves are really supportive. There's worry about a backup of inflation later on causing higher costs, but we are seeing strong demand on the DCM side as well. But we've got a big balance sheet, right? We have a strong base as one of the largest banks in the world, and we're looking for more customers at the end of the day. As we talked about, those growth markets are global, not just in Canada. What's the pipeline like? The pipeline is really strong. As you look at these deals coming through, we are busy—the team is really busy. You see that in the weekly meetings, in our commitments committee on the balance sheet, and in our underwriting meetings. We are really, really busy. And we're hiring. As you saw, we're hiring more bankers in the United States and more bankers in Canada. It's great to be busy, and I think that is a foreshadowing that there's another leg of growth coming. How is it partly also driven by energy security issues around the conflict in the Middle East, the situation with the Strait of Hormuz? Even if there is a deal, there is a feeling that maybe oil prices remain at a higher level going forward. How much is that underpinning some of that strength? There absolutely is a diversification strategy. I wish Canada got ahead of that curve a decade ago. We're a bit late, but we're certainly looking to capture that off-take. You look at Saudi Arabia building new pipelines, and the UAE needing to build a pipeline. There is obviously a strategy to decrease reliance on the straits so this can't happen again. With that infrastructure play in place, there's a reliance on partners like Canada, for sure. We have a chance to export another two million barrels a day. We currently produce roughly five million barrels a day, so that is a pretty significant increase. There's demand for that off-take on both the LNG side and the oil side, and we're looking to provide and support that capital. They're well on their way, and you're probably going to see an announcement over the next couple of weeks. All right, I want to finish up before we open it up to questions and answers about what advice you would give someone entering the finance world today given everything that we just talked about, given the very robust financing cycle, given the AI technological changes, given the fact that trees don't grow to the sky. What would you say? To a young person coming into the business now? I say the same thing to all young people: One pathway to success at a big company—or even a small company—is creating a learning flywheel. You have to learn, learn, learn, and learn fast. I think you have to come in and leverage these tools, because if you don't, your learning flywheel is going to be a lot slower. If you master these tools in school and come into the organization knowing how to leverage an LLM or a small language model, you're going to accelerate your learning. You're going to be able to take on more responsibility so much more quickly in the organization because you're going to have a deeper understanding of finance. I'm pretty excited for the next generation because I think their learning and career curves can shoot straight up. We're going to need that because we have so many boomers with extensive experience retiring. We'll need to move young people through the organization much more quickly than we did before, and you're going to contribute. I got frustrated when I started in the '80s—to date myself—because it took 10 to 15 years before I made a decision that really mattered. Today, I think you're going to do that in years, and these tools will provide that opportunity. So I'm super excited for the next generation. I know it's been hard to start, and we're in this low-hire, low-fire world, but once you get going, the path is going to be very different than it was for previous generations over the last hundred years. So you haven't stopped hiring or limited the number of people you've hired. No, we've actually hired more students this year than we ever hired before because we need that talent and capability to come into the organization to address those demographic gaps. We need to create that opportunity for young people in the United States and in Canada across our entire network, so this summer we accelerated that.

Context Needed to Reach AGI, Says Databricks CEO

Bloomberg Technology(728.0K)·10:07·2.2K views·2026-06-16
Ali GhodsiCEO, Databricks Ed LudlowHost, Bloomberg Technology
TL;DRThe primary bottleneck to achieving artificial general intelligence (AGI) in the enterprise is not raw intelligence, but a lack of organizational context.
Databricks is addressing this constraint by building "agentic-first" database architectures and semantic ontologies...

TL;DR

The primary bottleneck to achieving artificial general intelligence (AGI) in the enterprise is not raw intelligence, but a lack of organizational context. Databricks is addressing this constraint by building "agentic-first" database architectures and semantic ontologies that enable AI agents to compute complex business queries in real time.

Context as the Bottleneck to AGI

  • The Context Gap: While modern AI models possess immense intelligence, they are not yet widely integrated into enterprise workflows because they lack access to internal company data, processes, and institutional knowledge.
  • Enterprise Ontology: To solve this, Databricks is utilizing enterprise ontologies—interconnected webs of organizational data that map how documents, people, and processes relate.
      • The Search Metaphor: Instead of an AI agent searching through separate systems one by one (similar to early web search), an ontology acts as a centralized index (like Google) that allows agents to tap into computed knowledge instantly.

Computational Agents vs. Recitative Agents

  • Genie 1's Computing Power: Unlike traditional AI assistants that merely find and recite existing documents, Genie 1 can compute live data and perform complex mathematical queries on the fly.
      • Novo Nordisk Case Study: The pharmaceutical giant uses Genie 1 to allow its scientists to quickly analyze and compute trial data for drug studies (such as Ozempic), reducing analysis time to a fraction of what it was.
  • Databases Built for Agents (Lake-tap & Lakebase): Modern database architecture was designed for human speeds, which are too slow and costly for high-frequency AI agent requests.
      • Unified Infrastructure: Lake-tap (L-tap) and Lakebase unify standard transactional databases with analytical data warehouses into a single, high-speed system optimized for AI agents.
      • Prada Case Study: Luxury brand Prada stores its KPIs within Lakebase, enabling AI agents to instantly compute and deliver accurate business metrics to leadership.

Hurdles to Enterprise AI Adoption

  • Security and Legal Friction: Even though Databricks has a 15-year track record of enterprise trust, actual deployment of deep AI systems is throttled by rigorous security and legal department reviews.
  • Chatbot Stagnation: Because of these internal compliance hurdles, most corporate AI adoption is currently limited to low-risk chatbot use cases. Implementing AI in high-stakes areas like financial reporting or drug development requires significantly more time.

Funding and Databricks' IPO Strategy

  • Aggressive Product Expansion: Databricks is aggressively moving into new verticals with "agentic-first" products, including *Customer Lake* (marketing tech) and *Lake Watch* (security operations).
  • Funding and Research Costs: Addressing rumors of a new funding round valuing Databricks at up to $75 billion, Ghodsi noted that recruiting top AI researchers and expanding globally requires substantial capital.
  • Postponing the IPO: Ghodsi stands by his assertion that 2024 is a "terrible year" to go public. Databricks plans to remain private until the market stabilizes and there is more predictability post-major tech IPOs.

Notable Quotes

"These AIs are plenty smart... But then, yet, we're not seeing them in the workplace. They're not everywhere... Why is that? They just don't have the context. If you could take all of the data, all the processes, everything that people are thinking and put them in the models, they could actually help us tremendously." — Ali Ghodsi
"The big difference between Genie 1 is that Genie 1 computes, other agents recite... What other agents will do is that they recite. They will try to find: is there a document that has this answer?... But what if that answer does not exist in any document and you have to actually compute it live?" — Ali Ghodsi
"I prefer for the company to go public when, you know, waters are a little bit more calm and there's a little bit more predictability. Uh, so, you know, I stand by what I said. I think for most other companies, uh you know, it's probably best to wait it out." — Ali Ghodsi
You know, Ali, this is a really big week, right? It's a really big deal. You know, Databricks, like, maybe it's not a household name to lots of people, but you would argue this is the biggest AI conference in terms of attendance and, like, energy in the world. And we'll go into all the nitty-gritty of what you're announcing and what people will be discussing there. I want to start with this kind of mile-high big picture, and that's your argument that AGI's already here. Intelligence is not the constraint. What is the constraint? It's the context. These AIs are plenty smart. Anyone who's interacted with them, if you just ask a big audience, "How many of you think this is super smart? Smarter than many of the people you work with or have around you most of the time?"—all the hands go up in different crowds. But then, yet, we're not seeing them in the workplace. They're not everywhere. We don't have hundreds of agents working together doing work for you and we're all managing thousands of agents. That's just not reality. Why is that? They just don't have the context. If you could take all of the data, all the processes, everything that people are thinking and put them in the models, they could actually help us tremendously. But there is a big gap there. So we really want to infuse that context into the existing AIs, and when you do that, magic happens. You know, this is why we launched Genie Ontology. That's exactly what it tries to do. Right. The idea of an enterprise ontology. You know, you just said it, you hear people in industry and in the culture use that term. What is it? Why is it important? Yeah, it's actually kind of, you know, it might be a confusing term. What it is is that it's sort of a big web of all the knowledge and how the knowledge is interconnected. So it could be: this person opened this document; this document was read by these other people; this document is about this particular information; you know, and tagging and explanations of what that is and what the key elements of that document are. And that is not just for that document, but for everyone in the organization and everything that they're doing. Okay, a lot of that information actually already exists. It's just not infused into the AI. So what the agents today do is they try to live go one by one and try to find it. That's what the current agents do. My analogy is, it's sort of like when Google invented the ten blue links. Imagine instead of giving you the ten blue links, it would go and search like one website, read it, and then find another link, read it, and so on. That would have taken forever. It would have cost you a lot of money, and it wouldn't have been very good. That's how the agents are doing it today. So, ontology is essentially like that index that Google had but for the enterprise, computing it behind the scenes. And then live, when you're asking questions or you're having the agent do things for you, it can tap into that index. You know, everybody has an agent, or maybe they package it as an AI assistant. I think a really simple place to start is: what is different about Genie 1? Yeah, so the big difference with Genie 1 is that Genie 1 computes, while other agents recite. Maybe the best way to explain this would be with an example. I can ask it, and I ask it all the time, "Who are the new customers on the platform in the last 24 hours?" Or, "Which of our biggest customers churned in the last ten days?" What other agents will do is that they recite. They will try to find if there is a document that has this answer, search through it very quickly, and then try to give you that answer. But what if that answer does not exist in any document and you have to actually compute it live? You need numeracy for that and you need to understand the data. So that's what's different about Genie 1. It can do that, and it can do that really fast because it uses that ontology. This is what Novo Nordisk uses Genie 1 for. Novo Nordisk has actually empowered their scientists to use this. Before, it would take them a very long time when they ran different experiments and they wanted to know, "How is this obesity study going? How is this drug affecting the different control groups, and what are we seeing in the results?" But now, Genie 1 computes that. It's not trying to recite it because that result doesn't exist; it computes it live, and it's able to do that in a fraction of the time than they did before. So, you know, that's what they use. A lot of people know about Novo Nordisk maybe from the drug that many of us are familiar with, which is Ozempic. So that's just one example of what it can do. You're making a bigger claim here than just sort of product launches, right? And I think it's that those systems that were built for human use, software that was built for humans, don't work as well as systems that are built—we're talking about the plumbing, the architecture, the infrastructure—built for agents to use the software. You and I got into this a couple weeks ago, right? That brings us to Lake-tap. What are you trying to say needs to be rebuilt here? Yeah, so what's happening, and you kind of nailed it, is that the cost of creating software is almost going to zero. Anyone can create software anywhere right now. But the problem with that is every piece of software that you're creating needs a database behind the scenes. That's just how software works. That's why we have these big database companies. But these databases were actually created for humans. Agents are different. Agents want to move very fast. They experiment, and sometimes they break the database. You know, they create maybe thousands of databases. You don't want to pay for thousands of databases. And also, they want to move fast, so they don't want to sit and wait 20 minutes. It would slow them down significantly. So, that's exactly what Lakebase is, and it really helps them do that. Now they can actually operate fast on that database where all the information is stored. Now, if they want to ask more complicated questions, like a data science question of the data, they have to use a different database today. That's called a data warehouse in the industry. So, now there are like two separate systems in the industry to do that. Lake-tap brings those two together. We basically have unified them into one database that's really fit for agents.
